Liability for Actions in a Clan Mosque: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on liability for mosque-related accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding liability for placing objects in a clan mosque and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A clan member's absolute permission in his own mosque vs. the devotional nature of enabling prayer: who bears the cost of an accident?

When a member of a clan mosque places a lamp or mat, and someone gets injured, should he be liable? Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on whether the victim is from the clan or not, while the Sahibayn hold that devotional acts (qurba) are exempt from liability altogether. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and identifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested scenario
You are given a scenario: a mosque belongs to a specific clan. A member of that clan hangs a lamp, places mats, or digs a well. Someone, either from the clan or an outsider, is injured.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, if the injured person is from outside the clan, the clan member is liable. If the injured is from the clan, no liability. His evidence: management of the mosque belongs to the clan, so their actions are absolutely permissible without condition of safety.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: the clan member is never liable, regardless of the victim's affiliation. Their evidence: these are devotional acts (qurba) like enabling prayer; all Muslims are equal in such acts.
4
Compare the analogies
Abu Hanifa analogizes the clan's right to the mosque to property rights; the Sahibayn analogize it to general acts of charity that are rewarded even if they cause harm.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Halwani reports that most shuyukh adopted the Sahibayn's view, and the fatwa is upon it. Thus, in practice, the clan member is not liable in either case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (مسجد لعشيرة فعلق رجل منهم قنديلاً أو جعل فيها بواري أو حصاة فعطب به رجل لم يضمن وإن كان من غيرهم ضمن وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وقالا : لا يضمن في الوجهين لأن هذه قربة يثاب عليها الفاعل فصار كأهل المسجد وكما لو كان بإذنهم، وهذا لأن بسط الحصير وتعليق القنديل من باب التمكين من إقامة الصلاة فيه فيكون من باب التعاون على البر والتقوى فيستوي فيه أهل المسجد وغيرهم. وله أن التدبير فيما يتعلق بالمسجد لأهله دون غيرهم كنصب الإمام واختار المتولي رفع بابه وإغلاقه وتكرار الجماعة حتى لا يعتد بمن سبقهم في حق الكراهة وبعدهم يكره فكان فعلهم مباحاً مطلقاً من غير قيد بشرط السلامة وفعل غيرهم مقيد بها وقضية القربة لا تنافي الغرامة إذا أخطأ الطريق كما إذا انفرد بالشهادة على الزنا، وكما إذا وقف على الطريق لإماطة الأذى ولدفع المظالم فعثر به غيره يؤجر على ذلك ويغرم والطريق فيه الاستئذان من أهله وقال الحلواني : أكثر المشايخ أخذوا بقولهما وعليه الفتوى وعن ابن سلام باني المسجد أولى بالعمارة، والقوم أولى بنصب الإمام والمؤذن . وعن الاسكافي أن الباني أحق به قال أبو الليث : وبه نأخذ إلا أن شخصاً ينصب والقوم يرون من هو أصلح لذلك. وفي الجامع الصغير: أو حصيراً. وفي الذخيرة : أو حفر بئراً فعطب به إنسان لا شيء عليه، وإن كان الحافر من غير العشيرة ضمن
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'A mosque of a clan (ashira), if a man from them hung a lamp or placed mats or pebbles in it, and a man was injured by it, he is not liable; but if (the injured person) was from outside (the clan), he is liable. And this is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is not liable in both cases, because this is a devotional act (qurba) for which the doer is rewarded, so he becomes like the people of the mosque and as if he had their permission. This is because spreading mats and hanging lamps are from the category of enabling prayer in it, which is cooperation in righteousness and piety, so the people of the mosque and others are equal. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that management of what pertains to the mosque belongs to its people, not others, like appointing the imam. And the one entrusted with it chooses to raise its door and close it and repeat congregations so that those who preceded them in the right of dislike (karaha) are not considered, and later (the mosque) becomes disliked. So their act is absolutely permissible without the condition of safety, while the act of others is conditioned by it. And the nature of a devotional act does not preclude liability if he misses the mark, as if he alone testifies to adultery, and as if he stands in the road to remove harm and prevent wrongs, and another stumbles over him; he is rewarded for that but bears liability. And in the road, permission from its people is required. And al-Halwani said: Most of the shuyukh adopted the view of the two companions, and the fatwa is upon it. And from Ibn Salam: The builder of the mosque has more right to its maintenance; and the people have more right to appoint the imam and muezzin. And from al-Askafi: The builder has more right to it. Abu al-Layth said: We adopt this, except that a person appoints (an imam) and the people see someone more suitable for that. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'or a mat.' In al-Dhakhira: 'or if he dug a well and a person was injured by it, nothing is on him; but if the digger was from outside the clan, he is liable.'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability of a person who places objects like lamps, mats, or pebbles in a mosque belonging to a specific clan (ashira). The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq states: if a member of the clan does so and someone is injured, the clan member is not liable if the victim is from the clan, but if the victim is an outsider, the clan member is liable. This is the position of Abu Hanifa. His argument is that management of the mosque belongs to the clan (ahluhu), so their actions in the mosque are absolutely permissible without the condition of safety, like wearing a cloak (as in the previous lesson). Outsiders' actions are conditioned on safety, hence they are liable. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they say the doer is not liable in either case because these are devotional acts (qurba) like enabling prayer, and all Muslims are equal in such acts; the reward is the same, and liability does not follow just because the victim is from outside. They compare it to someone standing in the road to remove harm: he is rewarded but liable if he causes an accident. The text then cites al-Halwani saying that most scholars adopt the view of the two companions, and the fatwa is upon it. It also mentions opinions about who has the right to manage the mosque (builder vs. inhabitants). The passage ends with citations from al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Dhakhira to confirm the exceptions for mats and wells.

Key Terms
العشيرة (al-'ashira): A clan or extended family; the mosque is specifically for their use.
القربة (al-qurba): A devotional act that brings reward (thawab).
التمكين من إقامة الصلاة (al-tamkin min iqamat al-salah): Enabling the performance of prayer, which is the purpose of mosque fixtures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position regarding a clan member placing objects in a clan mosque that injure an outsider?
A) The clan member is liable because he caused harm.
B) The clan member is not liable because the act is a devotional act (qurba).
C) The clan member is liable only if the mosque was built by his clan.
D) The clan member is not liable if he had permission from the imam.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that a clan member is not liable for injuries caused by placing objects in the clan mosque, regardless of who is injured, because it is a devotional act.

Liability for Fixtures in a Clan Mosque: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for mosque fixtures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the ruling of Abu Hanifa from that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding liability for mosque fixtures and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa distinguishes residents from non-residents in mosque fixture liability; the fatwa follows the Sahibayn who treat all equally.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. The issue: when a person places a fixture (lamp, mat, stone) in a mosque belonging to a specific clan, and someone is injured, who is liable? Abu Hanifa makes a distinction based on whether the person is a resident of the clan or not. His two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, hold both are not liable. We'll explore the proofs and see which view prevails for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
The act is placing fixtures (hanging lamp, spreading mat, placing stone) in a clan mosque. The parties are residents (from the clan) and non-residents.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Residents are not liable; non-residents are liable. Residents' actions are absolutely permissible due to their authority over the mosque; non-residents' actions are conditioned on safety.
Abu Hanifa analogizes to appointing an imam – management belongs to the people.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Both residents and non-residents are not liable. These acts are qurba (acts of worship) and cooperation in righteousness, so reward negates liability.
They argue the act facilitates prayer, so it is like the residents' own permission.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa counters that qurba does not negate liability, citing examples: the solitary witness for zina (rewarded but liable if he fails to produce four witnesses) and stopping on the road to remove harm (rewarded but liable if someone stumbles). These show reward and liability can coexist.
The key: residents have inherent authority; non-residents do not.
5
Identify the tarjih
Al-Halwani reports that most scholars and the fatwa adopt the Sahibayn's view. The mufta bihi is that no liability attaches to either resident or non-resident for such fixtures.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : (مسجد لعشيرة فعلق رجل منهم قنديلاً أو جعل فيها بواري أو حصاة فعطب به رجل لم يضمن وإن كان من غيرهم ضمن وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. وقالا : لا يضمن في الوجهين لأن هذه قربة يثاب عليها الفاعل فصار كأهل المسجد وكما لو كان بإذنهم، وهذا لأن بسط الحصير وتعليق القنديل من باب التمكين من إقامة الصلاة فيه فيكون من باب التعاون على البر والتقوى فيستوي فيه أهل المسجد وغيرهم. وله أن التدبير فيما يتعلق بالمسجد لأهله دون غيرهم كنصب الإمام واختار المتولي رفع بابه وإغلاقه وتكرار الجماعة حتى لا يعتد بمن سبقهم في حق الكراهة وبعدهم يكره فكان فعلهم مباحاً مطلقاً من غير قيد بشرط السلامة وفعل غيرهم مقيد بها وقضية القربة لا تنافي الغرامة إذا أخطأ الطريق كما إذا انفرد بالشهادة على الزنا، وكما إذا وقف على الطريق لإماطة الأذى ولدفع المظالم فعثر به غيره يؤجر على ذلك ويغرم والطريق فيه الاستئذان من أهله وقال الحلواني : أكثر المشايخ أخذوا بقولهما وعليه الفتوى وعن ابن سلام باني المسجد أولى بالعمارة، والقوم أولى بنصب الإمام والمؤذن . وعن الاسكافي أن الباني أحق به قال أبو الليث : وبه نأخذ إلا أن شخصاً ينصب والقوم يرون من هو أصلح لذلك. وفي الجامع الصغير: أو حصيراً. وفي الذخيرة : أو حفر بئراً فعطب به إنسان لا شيء عليه، وإن كان الحافر من غير العشيرة ضمن ذلك كله؛ هذا هو لفظ هذا الكتاب. وفي الأصل يقول : وإذا احتفر أهل المسجد في مسجدهم بئراً لماء المطر أو علقوا فيه قناديل أو جعلوا فيه حباً يصب فيه الماء أو طرحوا فيه حضاً أو ركبوا فيه باباً فلا ضمان عليهم فيمن عطب بذلك، فأما إذا أحدث هذه الأشياء من هو من غير أهل المحلة فعطب به إنسان فهذا على وجهين : إما أن يفعلوا بغير إذن أهل المحلة إن أحدثوا شيئاً أو حفروا بئراً فعطب فيها إنسان فإنهم يضمنون بالإجماع، فأما إذا وضعوا حباً ليشربوا منه الماء أو بسطوا حصيراً أو علقوا قناديل بغير إذن أهل المحلة فتعقل إنسان بالحصير فعطب أو وقع القنديل وأحرق ثوب إنسان أو أفسده قال أبو حنيفة : إنهم يضمنون . وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : لا يضمنون . قال الشيخ الإمام شمس الأئمة الحلواني وأكثر مشايخنا أخذوا بقولهما في هذه المسألة وعليه الفتوى.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (A mosque of a clan: if a man from them hangs a lamp, or places mats, or a stone, and a person is injured by it, he is not liable; but if it was from someone else, he is liable. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: He is not liable in both cases, because this is an act of worship for which the doer is rewarded, so it is like the mosque residents and as if it were with their permission. This is because spreading mats and hanging lamps are means of enabling prayer therein, so it is cooperation in righteousness and piety; thus residents and non-residents are equal. And for him (Abu Hanifa): Management of mosque affairs belongs to its people, not to others, such as appointing an imam. So their action is absolutely permissible without the condition of safety, while the action of non-residents is conditioned by safety. The matter of worship does not negate liability if one errs, as in the case of a solitary witness for zina, and as in the case of stopping on the road to remove harm and prevent injustices: if he is stumbled over, he is rewarded and also liable. The road requires seeking permission from its people. Al-Halwani said: Most scholars adopted the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the fatwa is upon it. From Ibn Salam: The builder of the mosque has more right to its maintenance, and the people have more right to appoint the imam and mu'adhdhin. From al-Iskafi: The builder has more right to it. Abu al-Layth said: We adopt this, unless a person appoints and the people see someone more suitable. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: or a mat. In al-Dhakhira: or a well: if he digs a well and a person is injured, there is nothing upon him; but if the digger is from outside the clan, he is liable for all that. This is the wording of this book. In al-Asl: If the residents of a mosque dig a well in their mosque for rainwater, or hang lamps, or place a container for water, or throw in a stone, or install a door, there is no liability on them for anyone injured by that. But if someone from outside the locality does these things, then it is of two types: if they do something or dig a well without permission of the locality and someone is injured, they are liable by consensus. If they place a vessel to drink from, or spread a mat, or hang a lamp without permission of the locality, and a person trips on the mat and is injured, or the lamp falls and burns a person's garment or damages it, Abu Hanifa said: They are liable. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: They are not liable. The Shaykh Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and most of our scholars adopted the view of the two in this issue and the fatwa is upon it.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It discusses liability for injuries caused by fixtures placed in a mosque belonging to a specific clan or locality (masjid al-'ashira). The key issue: when a resident (from the clan) or a non-resident hangs a lamp, spreads a mat, places a stone, etc., and someone is injured, who is liable? According to Abu Hanifa, residents are not liable because their actions are absolutely permissible (mubah mutlaq) due to their authority over the mosque, similar to appointing an imam. Non-residents, however, are liable because their actions are conditioned on safety and they lack the inherent authority. His two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, argue that both are not liable because these are acts of worship (qurba) and facilitate prayer, making them cooperation in righteousness, thus equally permissible for all. Abu Hanifa counters that acts of worship do not negate liability if harm occurs, citing examples like the solitary witness for zina and removing harm from the road. Al-Halwani reports that most scholars and the fatwa adopt the view of the two companions. The passage also includes examples from other works (al-Asl, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Dhakhira) confirming the same principle.

Key Terms
عشيرة (ashira): Clan or tribe; here refers to the residents of the mosque locality.
قربة (qurba): An act that draws one closer to Allah; a meritorious act of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, who is liable if a non-resident hangs a lamp in a clan mosque and it falls and injures someone?
A) The non-resident
B) The residents of the clan
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The fatwa in the Hanafi school follows the Sahibayn: both residents and non-residents are not liable for mosque fixtures, as acts of worship do not automatically negate liability but the mufta bihi treats them equally.

Liability for Sleeping on a Mat in the Mosque
Ruling on liability when someone sleeps on a mat and another trips over the person or mat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a person spreads a mat in the mosque and sleeps, and distinguish the two scenarios based on the object tripped over.

Core Idea
A person is not liable if someone trips over him, but he is liable if they trip over his mat.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from a less authoritative transmission (riwaya majhula) found in al-Muntaqa. It addresses a common scenario in mosques: someone spreads a mat to sleep, and another person trips. The ruling distinguishes between tripping over the sleeping person (no liability) and tripping over the mat (liability). Understanding this distinction helps clarify the boundaries of liability for objects versus persons in public spaces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the Arabic text carefully. There are two distinct cases: tripping over the sleeper (النائم) and tripping over the mat (الفراش). Note that each leads to a different legal consequence.
The key difference is the object of the stumble: person vs. object.
2
Determine the legal effect
For tripping over the sleeper, there is no liability (لا ضمان). For tripping over the mat, liability is established (ضامن). Ask yourself: why the difference? The sleeper is a person who is not an obstruction per se, while the mat is an object that may cause obstruction.
Liability here means the person who spread the mat must compensate.
3
Assess the authority of the ruling
The text states this is a 'riwaya majhula' (unknown transmission). In Hanafi methodology, such transmissions have lower authority than Zahir al-Riwayah. This means the ruling is recorded but may not be the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
Always check the authority weight when applying rulings from non-standard sources.
4
Apply to new scenarios
Consider a similar case: someone places a bag in the mosque and sleeps leaning against it. If someone trips over the bag, would there be liability? The principle from this ruling extends: objects placed in pathways can create liability if they cause harm.
The underlying principle is about causing harm through objects, not persons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى رواية مجهولة : وإذا فرش الرجل فرشاً في المسجد ونام عليه فعثر رجل بالنائم فلا ضمان، ولو عثر بالفراش فهو ضامن
And in al-Muntaqa, an unknown transmission: If a man spreads a mat in the mosque and sleeps on it, and a person trips over the sleeper, there is no liability; but if he trips over the mat, he is liable.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from a less authoritative transmission (riwaya majhula) in al-Muntaqa. It distinguishes two scenarios: If a person sleeps on a mat in the mosque and someone trips over the sleeping person, there is no liability on the sleeper. However, if the tripping occurs because of the mat itself (e.g., the mat is protruding or causing an obstruction), then the person who spread it is liable. The logic: the sleeper is a person, and the mat is an object. The ruling is consistent with general principles: a person who occupies permissible space is not liable if someone trips over him, but placing an object in a pathway can create liability if it causes harm.

Key Terms
فرش (farsh): A mat or carpet spread on the ground.
رواية مجهولة (riwaya majhula): A transmission whose chain is unknown or weak, thus of lesser authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the ruling if a person trips over the mat on which someone is sleeping?
A) No liability
B) The sleeper is liable
C) The person who spread the mat is liable
D) Both are liable

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, tripping over a person in a permissible space imposes no liability, but tripping over their property can impose liability on the owner.

Liability for Building a Mosque or Bridge Without Permission
Rulings on liability for constructions on public roads without authorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute liability (Abu Hanifa) and harm-conditioned liability (Abu Yusuf) for unauthorized construction on public roads, and apply the istihsan exemption for desert areas.

Core Idea
Liability for unauthorized construction hinges on harm: city roads vs. desert, intentional crossing vs. accident.

When does building a mosque or bridge on a public road without permission make you liable for injuries? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq reveals a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on the role of actual harm, and introduces a key istihsan exception. We also learn a distinctive bridge ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The passage covers two distinct cases: building a mosque on a Muslim road, and constructing a bridge over a river. Each has its own ruling based on location and intention.
Mosque: on city or desert road? Bridge: crossing intentionally or accidentally?
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds the builder absolutely liable for any injury caused by the wall of a mosque built without the ruler's permission, regardless of location or harm.
Strict liability – no condition of actual harm.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's condition
Abu Yusuf restricts liability to cases where the construction causes tangible harm – narrowing (tadyiq) or injury (izarar) on city roads. In desert areas, no liability by istihsan.
Harm is the operative cause ('illa).
4
Analyze the bridge ruling
Muhammad al-Shaybani rules: if a man crosses a bridge built without permission intentionally and falls, the builder is not liable because the victim assumed the risk.
Contrast with mosque: no mention of intention there; here intention matters.
5
Apply istihsan reasoning
Istihsan overrides strict qiyas (which would find liability) in desert/non-harmful constructions because of custom and public benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه أيضاً رواية مجهولة : إذا بنى مسجداً في طريق المسلمين بغير أمر السلطان فعطب بحائطه فهو ضامن في قول أبي حنيفة، وكذلك في قول أبي يوسف إذا كان في الطريق الأمصار حيث يكون تضييقاً أو اضراراً، وإن كان في الصحراء بحيث لا يضر بالطريق غير أنه في أفنية المصر فلا ضمان عليه استحساناً، ولو أن رجلاً أخرج من داره مسجداً وبنى كان أولى الناس من أهل المحلة وغيرهم بإصلاحه والإسراج وليس لأحد أن يشركه فيه بإذنه. وعن أبي يوسف برواية بشر عن أبي حنيفة لأهل المسجد أن يهدموا مسجدهم ويهدموا بناءه وليس لغيرهم أن يفعل ذلك إلا برضاهم. قال محمد في الجامع الصغير في رجل جعل قنطرة على نهر بغير إذن الإمام فمر عليها رجل متعمداً فوقع فعطب فلا ضمان عليه؛ هكذا ذكر المسألة هنا.
And in it also, an unknown transmission: If a man builds a mosque on the road of the Muslims without the ruler's permission, and a person is injured by its wall, he is liable according to Abu Hanifa, and likewise according to Abu Yusuf if it is on the road of cities where it causes narrowing or harm; but if it is in the desert such that it does not harm the road except that it is in the courtyards of the city, there is no liability by istihsan. And if a man takes out a mosque from his house and builds it, he has more right than others from the locality and others to repair it and provide lighting, and no one may include themselves without his permission. From Abu Yusuf, by way of Bishr from Abu Hanifa: The residents of a mosque may demolish their mosque and its structure, and no one else may do so except with their consent. Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If a man constructs a bridge over a river without the ruler's permission, and a man crosses it intentionally and falls and is injured, there is no liability; thus the issue is mentioned here.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage gathers several rulings from an unknown transmission (riwaya majhula) and al-Jami' al-Saghir. It covers: (1) Building a mosque on a Muslim road without the ruler's permission: if its wall causes injury, the builder is liable according to Abu Hanifa in all cases. Abu Yusuf agrees but restricts it to roads in cities where it causes narrowing or harm; in deserts (sahara) or areas not harming the road, there is no liability by istihsan (juristic preference). (2) A man who takes a mosque out of his house has preferential rights over maintenance and lighting, and no one can share without his consent. (3) The residents of a mosque may demolish it and its structure; non-residents cannot unless they consent. (4) A bridge built over a river without the ruler's permission: if a person crosses it intentionally and is injured, there is no liability on the builder. This is because the person voluntarily assumed risk.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference overriding strict analogy due to custom or necessity.
قنطرة (qantara): A bridge or arched structure built over water.
تضييق (tadyiq): Narrowing of the road, causing obstruction.
إضرار (izarar): Causing harm or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, when does building a mosque on a road without permission result in liability?
A) Always
B) Only if the road narrows or is harmed
C) Only in deserts
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Building on public roads without permission incurs liability only when harm is caused; istihsan may excuse if no harm, especially in open areas, and intentional crossing on a bridge removes liability.

Liability for Objects Placed in Mosque by Non-Residents
Ruling on liability for wells, jars, lanterns placed by non-residents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between cases where a non-resident is liable for harm caused by objects placed in a mosque according to the Hanafi school, and state the mufta bihi position regarding liability for benign objects.

Core Idea
For objects placed without permission in a mosque, the later Hanafi position absolves non-residents of liability for benign items like lanterns and mats.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a nuanced tort case: a non-resident places a lantern or mat in a mosque, and someone is harmed. Does he owe compensation? The ruling revolves around a key distinction between inherently dangerous structures and merely beneficial objects, with an intra-Hanafi disagreement that was later resolved.

Guided Path
1
Separate residents from non-residents
Identify the threshold: residents (ahl al-mahalla) are never liable for objects they place in the mosque. The dispute only concerns non-residents.
Residents have customary ownership rights over the mosque.
2
Classify the non-resident's action
Two categories: (1) inherently dangerous acts like digging a well – liability by consensus. (2) benign objects like mats, lanterns, jars – this is the disputed area.
The text explicitly mentions that digging a well without permission triggers liability by ijma'.
3
Record the conflicting positions
Abu Hanifa holds the non-resident liable; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold no liability. Understand that both views are within the Hanafi school.
The source does not provide explicit proofs, but the dispute is based on analogical reasoning (qiyas).
4
Identify the mufta bihi
Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani and most later scholars adopted the Sahibayn's view (no liability for benign objects). Fatwa is given accordingly.
This represents the mature school position (authority_weight: fatawa_mutakhkhirin).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا احتفر أهل المسجد في مسجدهم بئراً لماء المطر أو علقوا فيه قناديل أو جعلوا فيه حباً يصب فيه الماء أو طرحوا فيه حضاً أو ركبوا فيه باباً فلا ضمان عليهم فيمن عطب بذلك، فأما إذا أحدث هذه الأشياء من هو من غير أهل المحلة فعطب به إنسان فهذا على وجهين : إما أن يفعلوا بغير إذن أهل المحلة إن أحدثوا شيئاً أو حفروا بئراً فعطب فيها إنسان فإنهم يضمنون بالإجماع، فأما إذا وضعوا حباً ليشربوا منه منه الماء أو بسطوا حصيراً أو علقوا قناديل بغير إذن أهل المحلة فتعقل إنسان بالحصير فعطب أو وقع القنديل وأحرق ثوب إنسان أو أفسده قال أبو حنيفة : إنهم يضمنون . وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : لا يضمنون . قال الشيخ الإمام شمس الأئمة الحلواني وأكثر مشايخنا أخذوا بقولهما في هذه المسألة وعليه الفتوى.
And if the people of the mosque dig a well in their mosque for rainwater, or hang lanterns in it, or place a jar into which water is poured, or put [something else] or install a door, there is no liability upon them for anyone who is harmed by that. As for when these things are done by someone who is not from the residents of the locality, and a person is harmed by it, then this is of two types: either they do so without the permission of the residents — if they do something or dig a well and a person falls in, they are liable by consensus. As for if they place a jar for people to drink from, or spread a mat, or hang lanterns without the permission of the residents, and a person trips over the mat and is harmed, or the lantern falls and burns a person's garment or damages it, Abu Hanifa said: they are liable. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: they are not liable. The Shaykh Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani and most of our scholars took their (i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) opinion in this matter, and upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability for objects placed in a mosque by non-residents. The ruling begins by stating that residents of the locality are not liable for things they place in the mosque, such as wells, lanterns, jars, doors, etc. However, when non-residents do the same, the ruling is split. If the non-residents dig a well or create a structure without permission, they are liable by scholarly consensus. But if they place items that are not inherently dangerous (like a mat for sitting or a jar for drinking or a lantern for light), Imam Abu Hanifa holds them liable, while Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad hold they are not liable. The commentator notes that the majority of later scholars (including Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani) adopted the opinion of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and fatwa is given accordingly. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that liability for torts due to acts of permissible benefit may be mitigated by public utility and permission, even if implicit.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Civil liability or responsibility to compensate for harm caused.
أهل المحلة (ahl al-mahalla): Residents of the locality or neighborhood who have customary rights over the mosque.
إجماع (ijma'): Scholarly consensus. Here it is the consensus that digging a well without permission imposes liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) opinion, if a non-resident hangs a lantern in a mosque without permission and it falls and burns someone's garment, is he liable?
A) Yes, according to Abu Hanifa.
B) Yes, according to consensus.
C) No, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
D) No, according to all scholars.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, non-residents who place benign objects (like lanterns or mats) in a mosque without permission are not liable for harm caused, according to the mufta bihi opinion of the Sahibayn.

Liability for Sitting in the Mosque: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Dispute on liability if someone trips over a person sitting in the mosque

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on liability for sitting in the mosque and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Liability for tripping over a sitter in the mosque depends on the purpose of sitting, with Sahibayn's view being the relied upon.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi discussion. The ruling on tort liability (daman) when someone trips over a person sitting in the mosque is not uniform; it depends on whether the sitting is for an act of worship or for something else. We will unpack the positions of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and later scholars to determine the operative view for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
The text begins by presenting a clear case: a person sits in the mosque for non-worship activities (conversation, sleeping, standing without prayer, or passing through) and someone trips and dies. Abu Hanifa says the sitter is liable; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he is not liable unless he walked over the person.
Note the immediate practical consequence: the ruling changes based on the activity.
2
Identify the core distinction
The text then shifts to sitting for worship: waiting for prayer, teaching, i'tikaf, remembrance of Allah, or reciting Quran. For these, there is no explicit narration from Abu Hanifa in the canonical books, leading to later disagreement among Hanafi scholars about what his view would be.
This absence is critical — it creates room for ta'yul (speculative extension).
3
Analyze the later ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafis (like Abu Bakr al-Razi) held that Abu Hanifa would impose liability even for worship sitting; others (like Abu Abdullah al-Jurjani) held he would not. Both positions claim to follow Abu Hanifa's reasoning.
This shows how a missing narration leads to ijtihad within the madhhab.
4
Note the consensus and exceptions
The text reports consensus (ijma') that sitting for conversation imposes liability, as it is not mubah. Also, if the person is actually praying, there is no liability by agreement. Additionally, sitting for Quran recital in i'tikaf is exempted according to Abu Bakr al-Balkhi.
These serve as anchoring points for the dispute.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later scholars (mutakhkhirin) have adopted the Sahibayn's view as the operative ruling for fatwa: no liability unless the sitter was walking. This is the relied-upon position in fatwa.
This is the practical takeaway for a mufti: apply the Sahibayn's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فيه أيضاً : إذا قعد الرجل في المسجد لحديث أو نام فيه أو قام فيه بغير الصلاة أو مر فيه مار لحاجة من الحوائج فعثر به إنسان فمات قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله بأنه ضامن، وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد بأنه لا ضمان عليه إلا أن يمشي فيه على إنسان، فأما إذا قعد لعبادة بأن كان ينتظر الصلاة أو كان قعد للتدريس وتعليم القضاء وللاعتكاف أو قعد لذكر الله تعالى وتسبيحه وقراءة القرآن فعثر به إنسان فمات هل يضمن؟ على قول أبي حنيفة لا رواية لهذ في الكتاب والمشايخ المتأخرون اختلفوا فيه ; فمنهم من يقول يضمن عند أبي حنيفة وإليه ذهب أبو بكر الرازي، وقال بعضهم لا يضمن وإليه ذهب أبو عبد الله الجرجاني. فأما إذا كان يصلي فعثر به إنسان فلا ضمان عليه سواء كان يصلي الفرض أو التطوع، السغناقي. قال الفقيه أبو جعفر : سمعت أبا بكر البلخي يقول : إن جلس لقراءة القرآن معتكفاً في المسجد لا يضمن عندهم جميعاً. وذكر فخر الإسلام والصدر الشهيد في الجامع الصغير : إن جلس للحديث فعطب به رجل يضمن بالإجماع لأنه غير مباح له؛ الذخيرة.
He also said: If a man sits in the mosque for a conversation, or sleeps, or stands without prayer, or a passerby passes through for some need, and a person trips over him and dies, Abu Hanifa said: he is liable. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: there is no liability upon him unless he walks upon a person. As for when he sits for worship, such as waiting for prayer, or sitting for teaching, teaching judiciary, for i'tikaf, or sitting for remembrance of Allah, glorification, and reciting Quran, and a person trips over him and dies, is he liable? From Abu Hanifa, there is no narration in the book, and the later scholars differed: some said he is liable according to Abu Hanifa, and that was held by Abu Bakr al-Razi; some said he is not liable, held by Abu Abdullah al-Jurjani. As for when he is praying, and a person trips over him, there is no liability, whether he is praying obligatory or voluntary. Al-Sighnaqi said. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said: I heard Abu Bakr al-Balkhi say: If he sits to recite Quran in i'tikaf in the mosque, he is not liable according to all of them. Fakhr al-Islam and al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If he sits for a conversation and a person is harmed, he is liable by consensus because it is not permitted for him. (Al-Dhakhira).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage details an intra-Hanafi dispute about liability when a person sitting in the mosque causes someone to trip and die. Imam Abu Hanifa makes a distinction based on the purpose of sitting: if it is for non-worship (like conversation, sleeping, standing without prayer), the sitter is liable. If it is for worship (waiting for prayer, teaching, i'tikaf), there is no explicit narration from Abu Hanifa in the books, leading to a ikhtilaf among later scholars: some (Abu Bakr al-Razi) say he is liable; others (Abu Abdullah al-Jurjani) say he is not. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the sitter is never liable unless he walks over someone. The later scholars generally adopted the Sahibayn's view. The passage also states that if the person is actually praying, there is no liability by agreement. Additionally, sitting for Quran recitation in i'tikaf is exempted, and sitting for conversation is liable by consensus because it is not a permitted activity. The fatwa is likely on the Sahibayn's view.

Key Terms
جلوس (julus): Sitting in the mosque, specifically for purposes other than prayer.
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the scholars that sitting for conversation (hadith) imposes liability.
مباح (mubah): Permitted act. Here, sitting for conversation is not considered mubah because it is unrelated to the purpose of the mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a person who sits in the mosque to teach fiqh (a worship act) and someone trips over him, based on the mufta bihi?
A) He is liable because teaching is not prayer.
B) He is not liable unless he was walking.
C) He is liable according to Abu Hanifa.
D) There is no consensus on this case.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi for sitting in the mosque is that there is no liability for tripping unless the sitter was walking, regardless of the purpose of sitting.

Liability for Objects and Buildings in Mosques and Public Roads
Liability rulings from al-Muntaqa on mats and buildings in public spaces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on liability for tripping over objects in a mosque and for buildings in public roads, distinguishing when istihsan overrides strict liability.

Core Idea
Liability for harm caused by a placed object or building depends on direct causation and location; istihsan removes liability in the desert where no real harm occurs.

This lesson examines two rulings from al-Muntaqa concerning liability in public spaces: first, a man who spreads a mat in a mosque and sleeps on it, and second, a mosque built in a public road. The rulings demonstrate the Hanafi emphasis on causation and the use of istihsan to achieve equitable outcomes. Notably, these narrations are of weak authority (riwaya majhula) but still illustrate important principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
List the two cases: (a) tripping over the sleeper vs. tripping over the mat; (b) building a mosque in a public road causing harm by its wall.
Both scenarios involve harm in a public space (mosque or road).
2
State the default ruling according to Abu Hanifa
For the mat: no liability for tripping over the sleeper, but liability for tripping over the mat. For the mosque: absolute liability if the wall causes harm.
Abu Hanifa's view is absolute; no condition of constriction.
3
Analyze the qualification of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf restricts liability for the mosque to cases where the building causes constriction or harm specifically in cities.
This is a condition of tadiiq (constriction) or idrar (harm).
4
Apply istihsan to the desert scenario
In the desert or outskirts (afniat al-misr) where there is no actual harm, liability is removed by istihsan, overriding qiyas.
Istihsan is used because the harm is negated by location.
5
Discuss the authority of these narrations
Both narrations are riwaya majhula (unknown transmission), meaning they are not from the core six books (Zahir al-Riwayah) and carry less weight.
They are considered weaker but still used in fatwa in some cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى رواية مجهولة : وإذا فرش الرجل فرشاً في المسجد ونام عليه فعثر رجل بالنائم فلا ضمان، ولو عثر بالفراش فهو ضامن وفيه أيضاً رواية مجهولة : إذا بنى مسجداً في طريق المسلمين بغير أمر السلطان فعطب بحائطه فهو ضامن في قول أبي حنيفة، وكذلك في قول أبي يوسف إذا كان في الطريق الأمصار حيث يكون تضييقاً أو اضراراً، وإن كان في الصحراء بحيث لا يضر بالطريق غير أنه في أفنية المصر فلا ضمان عليه استحساناً، ولو أن رجلاً أخرج من داره مسجداً وبنى كان أولى الناس من أهل المحلة وغيرهم بإصلاحه والإسراج وليس لأحد أن يشركه فيه بإذنه. وعن أبي يوسف برواية بشر عن أبي حنيفة لأهل المسجد أن يهدموا مسجدهم ويهدموا بناءه وليس لغيرهم أن يفعل ذلك إلا برضاهم.
And in al-Muntaqa there is an unknown narration: If a man spreads a mat in the mosque and sleeps on it, and a person trips over the sleeping man, there is no liability. But if he trips over the mat, he is liable. Also, an unknown narration: If he builds a mosque in the path of the Muslims without the command of the ruler, and someone is harmed by its wall, then he is liable according to Abu Hanifa, and likewise according to Abu Yusuf if it is in the road of cities where it causes constriction or harm. But if it is in the desert such that it does not harm the road except that it is in the courtyard of the city, there is no liability upon him by istihsan. And if a man takes out a mosque from his house and builds it, he is the most entitled of the people from the locality and others to its repair and lighting, and no one else has the right to share in it without his permission. And from Abu Yusuf, in the narration of Bishr from Abu Hanifa: the people of the mosque may demolish their mosque and demolish its building; others may not do so except with their consent.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents several specific rulings on liability and property rights in mosques, all from secondary sources (al-Muntaqa and other narrations). First, if a person sleeps on a mat in the mosque, tripping over the sleeper does not incur liability on the sleeper, but tripping over the mat itself does, because the mat is a physical object placed by the person. Second, building a mosque in a public road without permission incurs liability for harm caused by its wall, but only in urban areas where it causes constriction; in the desert or outskirts, istihsan overrides the general rule to avoid liability because there is no real harm. Third, if a person builds a mosque from his own house, he retains priority in maintenance and lighting over others; no one can share in that right without his permission. Finally, the people of a mosque (i.e., those who use it and have built it) have the right to demolish and rebuild it, but outsiders cannot do so without their consent. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi approach to property rights and tort liability in public spaces.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: in the desert, liability is removed due to lack of harm, by istihsan.
رواية مجهولة (riwaya majhula): A narration of unknown authority or chain of transmission.
أهل المسجد (ahl al-masjid): The people who use a particular mosque, often those who built it or are responsible for its maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the unknown narration, if a man spreads a mat in the mosque and sleeps, and someone trips over the sleeping man, what is the ruling?
A) The sleeper is liable
B) The mat owner is liable
C) There is no liability
D) Both are liable

Key Takeaway
Liability for harm in mosques and public roads depends on direct causation by an object and location, with istihsan overriding strict liability in non-harmful situations.

Rights of Mosque Founders and the People of the Mosque
Rulings on priority in mosque maintenance and demolition rights from al-Muntaqa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who has priority in maintaining a mosque built from private property and who has the right to demolish it, applying the Hanafi principle of the founder's precedence and community consent.

Core Idea
The founder of a mosque retains priority in its maintenance, and only the people of the mosque may demolish it; outsiders cannot act without consent.

This lesson covers two rulings from al-Muntaqa (via Bishr from Abu Yusuf) concerning the rights of a person who builds a mosque from his own house and the rights of ahl al-masjid (the people of the mosque) to demolish it. These rulings emphasize the founder's retained property rights and the community's authority over the mosque's physical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First ruling: A man who takes out a mosque from his house has priority in its repair and lighting; no one can share without his permission. Second ruling: The people of the mosque (ahl al-masjid) may demolish their mosque; others cannot without their consent.
Note that both rulings concern rights over the mosque, not liability.
2
Explain the basis of priority
The founder built the mosque from his own property, so he retains special rights akin to ownership until the mosque is fully dedicated.
This priority is exclusive, not shared.
3
Clarify 'ahl al-masjid'
Ahl al-masjid are those who built the mosque or regularly use it; they have collective authority.
They are distinct from the general public.
4
Note the authority
The second ruling is from Bishr from Abu Yusuf, indicating a specific chain of transmission.
This is not a riwaya majhula but a known narration.
5
Derive the principle
Property rights (founder's) and consent (of ahl al-masjid) are key in mosque administration; outsiders have no default right.
These rulings protect the mosque from arbitrary changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى رواية مجهولة : وإذا فرش الرجل فرشاً في المسجد ونام عليه فعثر رجل بالنائم فلا ضمان، ولو عثر بالفراش فهو ضامن وفيه أيضاً رواية مجهولة : إذا بنى مسجداً في طريق المسلمين بغير أمر السلطان فعطب بحائطه فهو ضامن في قول أبي حنيفة، وكذلك في قول أبي يوسف إذا كان في الطريق الأمصار حيث يكون تضييقاً أو اضراراً، وإن كان في الصحراء بحيث لا يضر بالطريق غير أنه في أفنية المصر فلا ضمان عليه استحساناً، ولو أن رجلاً أخرج من داره مسجداً وبنى كان أولى الناس من أهل المحلة وغيرهم بإصلاحه والإسراج وليس لأحد أن يشركه فيه بإذنه. وعن أبي يوسف برواية بشر عن أبي حنيفة لأهل المسجد أن يهدموا مسجدهم ويهدموا بناءه وليس لغيرهم أن يفعل ذلك إلا برضاهم.
And in al-Muntaqa there is an unknown narration: If a man spreads a mat in the mosque and sleeps on it, and a person trips over the sleeping man, there is no liability. But if he trips over the mat, he is liable. Also, an unknown narration: If he builds a mosque in the path of the Muslims without the command of the ruler, and someone is harmed by its wall, then he is liable according to Abu Hanifa, and likewise according to Abu Yusuf if it is in the road of cities where it causes constriction or harm. But if it is in the desert such that it does not harm the road except that it is in the courtyard of the city, there is no liability upon him by istihsan. And if a man takes out a mosque from his house and builds it, he is the most entitled of the people from the locality and others to its repair and lighting, and no one else has the right to share in it without his permission. And from Abu Yusuf, in the narration of Bishr from Abu Hanifa: the people of the mosque may demolish their mosque and demolish its building; others may not do so except with their consent.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents several specific rulings on liability and property rights in mosques, all from secondary sources (al-Muntaqa and other narrations). First, if a person sleeps on a mat in the mosque, tripping over the sleeper does not incur liability on the sleeper, but tripping over the mat itself does, because the mat is a physical object placed by the person. Second, building a mosque in a public road without permission incurs liability for harm caused by its wall, but only in urban areas where it causes constriction; in the desert or outskirts, istihsan overrides the general rule to avoid liability because there is no real harm. Third, if a person builds a mosque from his own house, he retains priority in maintenance and lighting over others; no one can share in that right without his permission. Finally, the people of a mosque (i.e., those who use it and have built it) have the right to demolish and rebuild it, but outsiders cannot do so without their consent. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi approach to property rights and tort liability in public spaces.

Key Terms
أهل المسجد (ahl al-masjid): The people who use a particular mosque, often those who built it or are responsible for its maintenance.
رواية مجهولة (riwaya majhula): A narration of unknown authority or chain of transmission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration of Bishr from Abu Yusuf, who has the right to demolish a mosque?
A) Any Muslim
B) The ruler
C) The people of the mosque
D) The founder only

Key Takeaway
The founder of a mosque retains priority in maintenance, and only the people of the mosque have the right to demolish it.

Liability for Building a Bridge Without Permission
Detailed ruling on liability for building a bridge without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for building a bridge without permission and apply the conditions of river ownership and victim's intention to determine liability.

Core Idea
Liability for harm caused by an unauthorized structure depends on whether the victim intentionally assumed the risk.

In this passage from al-Jami' al-Saghir, Imam Muhammad presents a nuanced ruling on building a bridge over a river without permission. The ruling is not absolute; it depends on whether the river is private or public, and whether the person who fell did so intentionally or accidentally. This lesson will dissect the layers of this ruling and the qiyas (analogy) that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is building a bridge (qantara) over a river without the imam's permission. The context includes river ownership and the passerby's intention when crossing.
The bridge builder's act is the potential cause of harm.
2
Classify the river type
Determine if the river is owned by the builder, private for specific people, or public for all Muslims. Ownership and classification affect liability.
The text outlines three categories: owned by builder, private, public.
3
Apply the intention condition
Check if the passerby intentionally walked over the bridge or unintentionally (e.g., blind, at night). Intention can negate liability.
Unintentional crossing (ignorance or circumstances) may shift liability.
4
Determine liability
Based on river type and intention, state whether the builder is liable (daman) or not. Note both the Zahir al-Riwayah position and Abu Yusuf's view for public rivers.
Zahir al-Riwayah holds the builder liable for public rivers; Abu Yusuf has an exception.
5
Trace the qiyas
Identify the original case (digging a well on another's property) and how the illa (transgression + victim's lack of intentional assumption of risk) applies to the bridge case.
The analogy extends to placing a wooden beam in a road.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد في الجامع الصغير في رجل جعل قنطرة على نهر بغير إذن الإمام فمر عليها رجل متعمداً فوقع فعطب فلا ضمان عليه؛ هكذا ذكر المسألة هنا. واعلم أن هذه المسألة على وجهين : أما إذا كان النهر مملوكاً أو لم يكن مملوكاً، فلو كان مملوكاً له فلا ضمان وإن صار مسبباً للتلف لأنه غير متعد في هذ السبب، وإن لم يكن النهر مملوكاً له فهذا على وجهين : إن كان نهراً خاصاً لأقوام مخصوصين فلا ضمان عليه إن كان تعمد المرور عليها، وإن لم يتعمد المرور عليها. وفي الكافي بأن كان أعمى أو مر ليلاً فهو ضامن وصار الجواب فيه كالجواب فيما إذا حفر بئراً في ملك إنسان فوقع فيها إنسان، أما إذا كان نهراً عاماً لجماعة مسلمين وقد فعل ذلك بغير إذن الإمام فالجواب فيه كالجواب فيما لو نصب جسراً أو قنطرة على نهر خاص لأقوام معينين ؛ هكذا ذكر في ظاهر الرواية. وروي عن أبي يوسف في غير رواية بشر إلا إذا كان النهر عاماً لجماعة مسلمين فإنه لا ضمان على واضع القنطرة والجسر سواء علم الماشي عليه فانخرق به فمات إن تعمد المرور عليها لا ضمان على واضع القنطرة، وإن لم يعلم المار به ضمن کمن نصب خشبة في طريق فمر به كان ضامناً قالوا : إن كانت الخشبة الموضوعة صغيرة بحيث لا يوطأ على مثلها لا يضمن واضعها لأن الوطء على هذه الخشبة بمنزلة تعمد الزلق وإن كانت الخشبة كبيرة يوطأ على مثلها يضمن واضعها هذا إذا كان النهر خاصاً لأقوام مخصوصين، فإن كان النهر لعامة المسلمين في ظاهر الرواية يكون ضامناً، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يكون ضامناً.
Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir regarding a man who made a bridge over a river without the permission of the imam, and a man intentionally walked over it, fell, and was harmed: there is no liability. Thus the issue is mentioned here. Know that this issue has two aspects: either the river is owned or not owned. If it is owned by him, then there is no liability even if it becomes a cause of destruction, because he is not transgressing in this cause. If the river is not owned by him, then this has two aspects: if it is a private river for specific people, there is no liability if he (the passerby) intentionally walked over it, but if he did not intentionally walk over it (e.g., he was blind or walked at night), then he (the bridge builder) is liable, and the ruling is like the ruling when someone digs a well on another's property and a person falls in. As for if the river is a public river for all Muslims and he did that without the imam's permission, then the ruling is like the ruling if one erects a bridge or causeway over a private river for specific people. Thus it is mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf in other than the narration of Bishr that if the river is public for all Muslims, there is no liability on the placer of the bridge or causeway, whether or not the walker knew. So if he (the walker) intentionally walked over and broke through and died, there is no liability on the placer. But if the passerby did not know, he (the placer) is liable, like one who places a wooden beam in a road and someone walks over it, he is liable. They said: If the placed wooden beam is small such that one would not normally step on it, the placer is not liable, because stepping on such a beam is like intentional slipping. But if the beam is large such that one would normally step on it, the placer is liable. This is when the river is private for specific people. In Zahir al-Riwayah, if the river is for the general Muslims, he is liable. And from Abu Yusuf that he is liable.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed ruling from Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir on building a bridge over a river without permission. The ruling is layered: first, if the river is owned by the builder, no liability. If not owned, then subdivision based on whether the river is private for specific people or public. For a private river, if the passerby intentionally walks over, no liability; if unintentional, liability. For a public river, the Zahir al-Riwayah holds the builder liable. There is also a rivayah from Abu Yusuf that for a public river, no liability if the passerby intentionally walks, but liability if unintentional. The text also analogizes to a wooden beam placed in a road: if small, no liability (stepping is like intentional slipping); if large, liability. The ruling uses qiyas (analogy) to the case of digging a well on someone else's property. The overall principle is that transgression ('udwan) is key: if the cause is set up without right but the victim intentionally encounters it, the transgression of the setter is offset by the victim's intentional act. This is a classic Hanafi tort analysis.

Key Terms
قنطرة (qantara): A bridge or arch built over water to cross.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression or acting beyond one's right.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted position from the founding imams.
ضمان (daman): Liability; the obligation to compensate for harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man builds a bridge over a private river without permission, and a blind man walks over it unintentionally and is harmed, who is liable?
A) The bridge builder.
B) The blind man.
C) No one.
D) The river owner.

Key Takeaway
Liability for an unauthorized structure hinges on whether the victim intentionally assumed the risk or not.

Liability for Causing Harm While Sitting in a Mosque: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on the ruling of a person sitting in a mosque causing harm to another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding liability for harm caused while sitting in a mosque, identify their proofs, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Sitting for prayer is a necessity; liability depends on whether the act is primary or secondary to the mosque's purpose.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: if a person sitting in a mosque causes another to trip and be injured, is he liable? Imam Abu Hanifa distinguished between prayer time and other times, while the two Sahibayn held no liability in all cases. In this lesson, we will examine their proofs and understand why later Hanafi authorities favored the Sahibayn's view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa: liable if sitting outside prayer time, not liable during prayer time. Then the Sahibayn: no liability in any case.
2
Identify the opponent
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) offer a differing view within the same school, not an external madhhab.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn argue that mosques are built for worship and remembrance; sitting is a necessity for prayer and other acts of worship; the hadith 'the one waiting for prayer is in prayer' extends to all necessary acts.
Quranic verses: al-Nur 36, al-Baqarah 187.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes primary use (prayer) from secondary use (other acts). Only prayer-related sitting is exempt; other acts are conditional on safety.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: if someone sits for teaching, Abu Hanifa says liable; Sahibayn say not liable.
6
Identify the tarjih
Later authorities like Sadr al-Islam, al-'Ayni, and al-Dhakhira favor the Sahibayn; the mufta bihi is no liability in any case.
Shams al-A'imma clarified that even Abu Hanifa's view only applies to acts not specific to the mosque.

Book of Diyat. Chapter: What a man does in the path causing another to fall; he is liable if it is not prayer time, and if it is prayer time, he is not liable. Know that this issue has two aspects: If the canal is owned, then no liability even if it causes harm because he is not transgressing in this cause. If the canal is not owned, then two sub-aspects: if it is a private canal for specific people, then no liability if the passerby deliberately walked over it; if not deliberate, then liable. In al-Kafi: if the passerby was blind or passed at night, the digger is liable, and the ruling is like digging a well in someone's property. If the canal is public for all Muslims and done without the imam's permission, the ruling is like placing a bridge or arch over a private canal. This is in Zahir al-Riwayah. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf in a non-Bishr narration that if the canal is public, there is no liability on the placer of the arch/bridge, whether the passerby knew or not. But if the passerby deliberately walked and fell, no liability; if he did not know, the placer is liable, like placing a beam in a path. If the beam is small so that people do not step on it, no liability; if large, liability. If the canal is public, in Zahir al-Riwayah the placer is liable, and from Abu Yusuf also liable. Al-Tamartashi said: if the mosque becomes crowded, the regulars may prevent those who are not from prayer. In al-'Ayni: the mosque can be for all Muslims but the regulars manage it, as the Prophet (saw) returned the keys of the Ka'ba to Banu Shayba. He (author) said: 'And if a man sits in the mosque and another is harmed by him, he is liable if it is not prayer time, and if it is prayer time, not liable.' This is according to Abu Hanifa, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no liability in any case. Their proof: mosques are built for prayer and remembrance; one cannot pray in congregation without waiting, so sitting is a necessity; the one waiting for prayer is considered in prayer; teaching and reciting Quran are worship; the mosque is built primarily for prayer, so other acts are secondary, and one may displace a sitter for a worshipper; but a worshipper cannot be displaced. Hence the distinction: prayer-related sitting is unconditionally permitted, other acts are conditional on safety. Sadr al-Islam said the view of the two is stronger. In al-'Ayni, our scholars adopted it. In al-Dhakhira, fatwa is given with their view. Shams al-A'imma said the correct position of Abu Hanifa is that the one waiting for prayer is not liable; the dispute is only about acts not specific to the mosque.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding a person sitting in a mosque who causes harm to another. The author begins by expanding on various scenarios of causing harm in a path, using analogies of canals and beams to illustrate the principles of liability. The key distinction is whether the person is sitting for prayer-related purposes or not. Abu Hanifa holds that if the sitting is during prayer time (for prayer), there is no liability; otherwise, there is liability. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold no liability in all cases, arguing that mosques are built for all forms of worship and sitting is a necessity. The commentary provides proofs from the Quran (Surah al-Nur 36, Surah al-Baqarah 187) and Hadith (the one waiting for prayer is in prayer). The author then discusses the tarjih, noting that Sadr al-Islam and later scholars (like in al-Dhakhira) favor the position of the Sahibayn, and fatwa is given accordingly. Shams al-A'imma clarifies that Abu Hanifa's own position is actually that the one waiting for prayer is not liable, and the dispute is only about other activities like teaching or recitation.

Key Terms
عطب ('atiba): To cause harm or injury, specifically causing another to fall and be injured.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for harm caused.
متعد (muta'addin): Transgressor, one who acts without right or beyond bounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in this passage, when is a person who sits in a mosque and causes harm to another liable?
A) Always liable
B) Never liable
C) Liable only if sitting for non-prayer purposes
D) Liable only if sitting during prayer time

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon view is that sitting in a mosque for any purpose does not incur liability for harm caused, because sitting is considered a necessity for worship.

Liability for a Leaning Wall: Istihsan Overriding Qiyas
Ruling and proof: owner liable under istihsan after demand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school imposes liability for harm from a leaning wall and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the concept of istihsan.

Core Idea
Omission after ability to act creates liability – istihsan overrides qiyas for public safety.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi fiqh scenario: a wall leans onto a public road, causing harm to persons or property. The Hanafi school, using istihsan (juristic preference), holds the owner liable if he failed to demolish it after being requested. The Shafi'i school, following strict qiyas, holds that no liability arises because the owner did not directly cause the harm. This lesson dissects the reasoning, the role of public interest, and the proof from early authorities.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: the owner is liable for all harm (life and property) if a Muslim or dhimmi demanded demolition and he had time to act but did not.
Liability also applies if the wall was built leaning from the start.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school holds the owner is not liable because there is no direct act or causation from him; liability requires a positive action.
This is the qiyas (analogy) position mentioned in the text.
3
Analyze the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument: no act (فعل), no direct causation (مباشرة علة), no condition/cause (مباشرة شرط أو سبب) – the wall falling is not the owner's doing.
This is the default rule in tort liability: you are not liable for mere inaction.
4
Explain the Hanafi response via istihsan
The Hanafi school uses istihsan, preferring rulings from 'Ali, Shuraih, al-Nakha'i, and the principle that private harm must be borne to prevent public harm. The owner's omission after demand and ability to act constitutes transgression (تعدي).
Also, the owner 'occupied the airspace' (أشغل هواء الطريق بملكه) and has the duty to remove the danger.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Hanafi law, the 'aqila (tribe) bears liability for lives lost (to avoid hardship on the individual), but Muhammad al-Shaybani dissents that the 'aqila does not bear – a minor intra-madhhab variance.
The mufta bihi (operative position) is that the 'aqila does bear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
حائط مال إلى طريق العامة ضمن ربه ما تلف به من نفس أو مال إن طالب بنقضه مسلم أو ذمي ولم ينقضه في مدة يقدر على نقضه وإن بناه مائلاً ابتداء ضمن ما تلف به وهذا استحسان. والقياس أن لا يضمن وهو قول الشافعي رحمه الله تعالى لأنه لم يوجد منه صنع هو فعل ولا مباشرة علة ولا مباشرة شرط أو سبب والضمان باعتبار ذلك فصار كما إذا لم يشهد عليه وبطل نقضه منه ووجه الاستحسان ما روي عن علي وعن شريح والنخعي وغيرهم من أئمة التابعين ما قلنا ولأن الحائط لما مال فقد أشغل هواء الطريق بملكه ورفعه في قدرته، فإذا طولب برفعه لزمه ذلك، فإذا امتنع مع التمكن منه صار متعدياً فيلزمه موجبه، ولأن الضرر الخاص يجب تحمله لدفع الضرر العام كالكفار إذا تترسوا بالمسلمين ثم ما تلف به من النفوس تحمله العاقلة لئلا يؤدي إلى الإجحاف وقال محمد : لا تتحمل العاقلة
Chapter: A wall leans toward a public road; its owner is liable for any life or property lost if a Muslim or dhimmi demanded its demolition and he did not demolish it within the time he could, and if he built it leaning initially, he is liable for what is lost. This is istihsan. The analogy is that he would not be liable, which is the view of al-Shafi'i, because no act, direct causation, or condition/ cause is from him, and liability is based on that, so it is as if he was not warned and his demolition is void. The basis of istihsan is what is narrated from 'Ali, Shuraih, al-Nakha'i and others of the tabi'un, and because when the wall leaned, it occupied the airspace of the road with his property and he has the ability to remove it; if demanded to remove it, he must; if he refuses while able, he becomes a transgressor and must bear the consequences, and because private harm must be borne to prevent public harm, like when unbelievers shield themselves with Muslims. Then what is lost of lives is borne by the 'aqila to avoid hardship. Muhammad said: the 'aqila does not bear it.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a leaning wall that poses a danger to a public road. The Hanafi position, based on istihsan (juristic preference), is that the owner is liable for harm caused if he failed to demolish it after being requested by any Muslim or dhimmi. This liability applies whether the wall was built leaning or became leaning later. The qiyas (analogy) would suggest no liability, which is the Shafi'i position, because the owner did not directly cause the harm. However, istihsan overrides due to considerations of public safety and preventing harm, supported by rulings of early authorities like 'Ali and Shuraih. The passage also mentions a dispute within the Hanafi school: Muhammad says the 'aqila (tribe) does not bear compensation for lives; the default Hanafi position is that the 'aqila bears it.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference overriding qiyas for public good or established practice.
عاقلة ('aqila): The tribe or group responsible for paying blood money (diyat) on behalf of the killer.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression; acting beyond bounds, here applied to omission after demand creates liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when is the owner of a leaning wall liable for harm caused to a passerby?
A) Immediately when the wall leans
B) Only if he built it leaning initially
C) After a Muslim or dhimmi demands demolition and he fails to do so within a reasonable time
D) Never, because there is no direct act

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, omission after ability to prevent harm can create liability through istihsan, overriding qiyas for public safety.

Liability for a Leaning Wall: Istihsan over Qiyas
Commentary on the legal liability for a leaning wall on a public road.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a leaning wall, identify the istihsan proof, and explain the difference from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Liability arises from the owner's failure to remove a known danger after demand, not from the wall's fall itself.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator explains the Hanafi position on liability for damage caused by a leaning wall on a public road. The owner is not automatically liable; liability is triggered only when a Muslim or dhimmi demands demolition and the owner fails to act within a feasible period. This ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) rather than strict qiyas, and it contrasts with the Shafi'i school's position. We will explore the proof from narrations and the rationale behind the Hanafi approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the wall leaning towards a public road. The ruling is that the owner is liable for any damage it causes if he fails to demolish after a valid demand.
Liability is conditional, not automatic.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites narrations from Ali, Shurayh, and al-Nakha'i as the basis for istihsan. These narrations establish a precedent for liability.
The proof is from the practice of early authorities.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The narrations show that failure to remove an obstruction after demand constitutes transgression, making the owner responsible for resulting harm.
The owner's capacity to demolish and his refusal are key.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds no liability by qiyas since the owner did not directly cause the fall. The Hanafi response is that istihsan overrides qiyas due to public welfare.
This is a classic case of istihsan vs. qiyas.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa because it protects public safety and is supported by the stronger evidence from early practice.
The mufta bihi ruling is that the 'aqila bears blood-money for lives lost.
