عليه فيما سواه . وإن كان هو الذي سقط من أعلى الحائط على إنسان من غير أن يسقط به الحائط وقتل أنساناً
Book of Blood-Money, Chapter: What a Man Causes on the Road, Section: A wall leaning towards a public road: its owner is liable for whatever is destroyed by it, whether life or property, if a Muslim or a dhimmi demanded its demolition and he did not demolish it within a period in which he was able to demolish it. And if he built it leaning initially, he is liable for what is destroyed. He prays in it, and if the sitter is engaged in remembrance of Allah or teaching or in i'tikaf, no one has the right to disturb the prayer from his place which he preceded to, because it was built for it, and its name indicates it, for masjid is a name for the place of prostration. And customarily too, the building of a mosque is not known except for prayer. So if that is so, then it is necessary to show the distinction between them, so being in it with respect to prayer is absolutely permissible without restriction of safety, and with respect to other than it is restricted by the condition of safety to show the distinction between the principal and the subsidiary. And it is not far that an act of worship be restricted by the condition of safety; do you not see that one who stands in the road for reconciliation is an act of worship in itself, yet it is restricted by safety in the correct view. And Sadr al-Islam mentioned that the more apparent is what they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said, because sitting is a necessary consequence of prayer, so it is annexed to it, because what is established by necessity for a thing has the same ruling as that thing. And in al-'Ayni on al-Hidaya, and our scholars adopted it. And in al-Dhakhira, fatwa is given by their saying. And Shams al-A'imma mentioned that the correct view from Abu Hanifa is that the one who sits waiting for prayer is not liable; rather the dispute is about an act that is not specific to the mosque, like reciting Qur'an and teaching fiqh and hadith. And Allah knows best. And in, Section. Section of the leaning wall: When the author, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned the rulings of killing related to a person directly and by causation, he began to explain the rulings of killing related to inanimate objects, which is the leaning wall. And it would have been right to delay it after the issues of all animals, giving precedence to animals over inanimate objects, except that the leaning wall, because it is akin to the projecting beam, the window projection, the wing, the latrine, and others, its issues were attached to them. For this reason he expressed with the word 'section' not with the word 'chapter'. Thus in al-Nihaya and others. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: A wall leaning towards a public road: its owner is liable for whatever is destroyed by it, whether life or property, if a Muslim or a dhimmi demanded its demolition and he did not demolish it within a period in which he was able to demolish it. And this is istihsan. And by qiyas he is not liable, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, because no action of his exists that is an act, nor direct causation of harm, nor direct causation of a condition or cause, and liability is based on that, so it is as if he had not been warned and his negligence is nullified. And the face of istihsan is what has been narrated from 'Ali, and from Shurayh, and al-Nakha'i, and other imams of the Tabi'in, what we said. And because when the wall leaned, it occupied the airspace of the road with his property, and it is within his power to remove it. So if he is asked to remove it, it becomes obligatory upon him. So if he refrains while able, he becomes a transgressor, so its consequence is incumbent upon him. And because private harm must be borne to repel public harm, like the disbelievers when they shield themselves with Muslims. Then for the lives destroyed by it, the 'aqila bears it, lest it lead to injustice. And Muhammad said: The 'aqila does not bear it. And it is valid only if the witnesses testify to three things: to the prior demand for demolition, and that he died from falling upon him, and that the house belongs to so-and-so. And for the property destroyed by it, the liability is upon him because the 'aqila does not bear property. And the condition is the demand for demolition from him, not the witnessing; rather witnessing is mentioned to enable proof in case of denial or denial of the 'aqila. So the approach of caution is the demand with any wording that conveys the demand for demolition, such as saying: 'Your wall is dangerous or leaning, so demolish it so that it does not fall.' Similarly, if he says: 'Bear witness that I have warned this man about his wall to demolish it.' And if he says: 'You should demolish it,' this is not valid as a demand or witnessing. And it is a condition that the demand be made to one who has the authority to demolish, such as the owner, the father, the grandfather, the executor regarding the property of a minor, the trading slave whether he has debt or not, the mortgagor regarding a mortgaged house because he is able to demolish, and the mukatib. Then if the mukatib dies while the kitaba remains, his value is due because payment is prevented, and after emancipation it is on the 'aqila of the master, and after restriction it is not due on anyone because of the inability of the mukatib and the lack of witnessing against the master. If the warning is given to a tenant or the mortgagee or the master, it is not considered. So if it falls and destroys something, the tenant and the owner are not liable. And it is a condition that the ability persists until the time of falling, so if it leaves his ownership by sale after the witnessing, he is free of liability because of his inability to demolish. And witnessing is not valid before it leans because the cause is non-existent. And he equated in al-Mukhtasar between whether the demand for demolition is made by a Muslim or a dhimmi, because the right of passage is for all, unlike slaves and minors because of their inability to demolish, unless the master authorized them for litigation, then their demand and witnessing are valid because they are treated as adults. Then after witnessing, the litigation is before a ruler or his deputy. If he becomes insane after witnessing permanently, or apostatizes and joins (the enemy) and then returns as a Muslim and his house is returned to him, then the wall falls after that and kills a person, it is of no consequence. Similarly, if the insane person recovers, and similarly if it is returned to him due to a defect or option of condition or option of sight, liability is not obligatory except with renewed witnessing. If part of the wall is sound and part is weak, and he witnesses against it, then the weak part falls along with the sound part and kills a person, the owner of the wall is liable, unless the wall is long such that part of it is weak and part is not weak; then he is liable for what the weak part hits and not liable for what the sound part hits, because when it is like that, it is like two walls, one sound and one leaning, and he witnessed against them, then the leaning did not fall and the sound fell, so it is of no consequence. And in it also: A foundling has a leaning wall, and he witnessed against it, then the wall fell and killed a person; the blood-money of the slain is from the public treasury because his inheritance goes to the public treasury. Similarly, a disbeliever if he accepts Islam. If a man is on his wall, and the wall is leaning or not leaning, and the man falls with the wall without his own action and hits a person and kills him, he is liable for what is destroyed by the wall if he had witnessed against it regarding its demolition, and there is no liability on him for other than that. If he is the one who fell from the top of the wall onto a person without the wall falling with him and killed a person...
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the ruling of a leaning wall on a public road. The author first presents the basic ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: if a wall leans toward a public road and the owner fails to demolish it after being demanded by a Muslim or dhimmi, he is liable for any damage it causes. If he built it leaning initially, he is liable immediately. The commentary then discusses istihsan as the basis for this ruling, contrasting it with qiyas which would not impose liability (the Shafi'i position). The istihsan relies on narrations from Ali, Shurayh, and Nakha'i, and on the principle that the owner's failure to remove the obstruction after demand constitutes transgression. The passage then details conditions: the demand must be made to the person with authority to demolish (owner, guardian, etc.), witnesses are needed for proof, and the demand must be clear. Various scenarios are covered: change of ownership, partial weakness of the wall, foundlings, and cases where a person falls with or without the wall. The mosque paragraph appears to be a separate discussion about sitting in a mosque, likely related to the permissibility of non-prayer activities under a condition of safety.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that abandons strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable ruling based on revealed or public interest considerations.
العاقلة (al-ʿāqila): The group of male agnates (or, in practice, the community) who bear the blood-money (diyah) for unintentional killing.
الإشهاد (al-ishhād): The act of having witnesses present to attest to the demand for demolition and the cause of death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in the passage, when does the owner of a leaning wall become liable for damage it causes?
A) Immediately when the wall becomes leaning, regardless of any demand.
B) Only if he built it leaning initially.
C) If a Muslim or dhimmi demanded its demolition and he failed to demolish within a period he was able to.
D) Only if the wall falls on a person and kills him.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on a leaning wall imposes liability on the owner after demand and failure to act, based on istihsan, and differs from the Shafi'i qiyas that requires direct causation.

Three Conditions for Liability When a Wall Collapses
Stating the three testimonies required for liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three testimonies required for liability when a wall collapses and explain why witnessing is only for evidence, not a condition.

Core Idea
Liability for a collapsing wall depends on a prior request to demolish; witnessing is only for evidence, not a condition.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the criteria for holding a property owner liable when a dangerous wall falls and causes harm. The key factor is whether the owner was properly asked to demolish the wall. The text distinguishes between the essential condition (the request itself) and the means of proof (witnessing). Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the ruling correctly in legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three testimonies
Read the passage and list the three things witnesses must testify to for liability to attach. These are the prior request for demolition, that the victim died from the collapse, and that the wall belonged to the owner.
2
Distinguish condition from proof
Note that the text explicitly says the condition (shart) is the request itself, not the witnessing. Witnessing is only mentioned to enable proof if the owner or his 'aqila denies the request.
This is a key legal nuance: the requirement is substantive, not procedural.
3
Define a valid request
Any expression that clearly conveys a demand to demolish, such as 'your wall is leaning, demolish it so it doesn't fall', counts as a valid request. The passage gives examples.
4
Recognize invalid expressions
A mere suggestion like 'you should demolish it' does not constitute a valid request and does not establish liability.

Source Text

ويصح حتى يشهد الشهود على ثلاثة أشياء : على التقدم في النقض، وعلى أنه مات بالسقوط عليه، وعلى أن الدار لفلان وما تلف به من الأموال فضمانه عليه لأن العاقلة لا تتحمل المال والشرط الطلب للنقض منه دون الإشهاد وإنما ذكر الإشهاد ليتمكن من إثباته عند الجحود أو جحود العاقلة فكان باب الاحتياط الطلب بكل لفظ يفهم منه طلب النقض من أن يقول حائطك هذه مخوف أو مائل فأهدمه حتى لا يسقط، وكذلك لو قال اشهدوا أني تقدمت حائطه هذا إلى هذا الرجل في هدم أيضاً. ولو قال ينبغي لك أن تهدمه فليس هذا يصح بطلب ولا إشهاد
And it is valid until the witnesses testify to three things: to the prior request for demolition, and that he died from falling upon him, and that the house belongs to so-and-so. And whatever wealth is lost due to it, its guarantee is upon him because the 'aqila does not bear wealth. The condition is the request for demolition from him, not the witnessing. Witnessing is only mentioned so that he can prove it upon denial or denial of the 'aqila. Therefore, the precautionary measure is to request with any expression that implies a request for demolition, such as saying: 'Your wall is dangerous or leaning, so demolish it so it does not fall.' Likewise, if he says: 'Witness that I have requested this wall from this man for demolition.' And if he says: 'You should demolish it,' this is not valid as a request nor as witnessing.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for holding the owner of a collapsing wall liable when it falls and causes damage. The key is the owner's prior request to demolish the wall. The witnesses must testify to three facts: (1) that the owner was asked to demolish it, (2) that the victim died from the collapse, and (3) that the wall belonged to the owner. The actual condition is the request itself, not the witnessing; witnessing is only for proof if the owner denies. Any clear request, such as 'your wall is dangerous, demolish it', suffices. A mere suggestion like 'you should demolish it' is not a valid request and does not establish liability.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'āqila): The clan or group responsible for paying blood money on behalf of a person who caused unintended death.
النقض (al-naqḍ): Demolition of a wall; the act of taking down a dangerous structure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the three things witnesses must testify to for the owner to be liable when a wall collapses?
A) The wall was leaning, the victim was a Muslim, and the owner was absent.
B) The owner was asked to demolish it, the victim died from the collapse, and the wall belonged to the owner.
C) The wall was built recently, the owner admitted fault, and there were no bystanders.
D) The wall was over ten cubits high, the victim was a pedestrian, and the owner had insurance.

Key Takeaway
For liability when a dangerous wall collapses, the essential condition is the owner being requested to demolish it; witnessing serves only as evidence, not a condition.

Conditions for a Valid Request to Demolish a Dangerous Wall
Explaining conditions for valid demolition request

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid recipients of a demolition request and state the conditions that make the warning legally effective in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A request to demolish is only valid if made to someone with authority to demolish and while that authority continues.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq specifies the procedural requirements for a legal warning (al-talab) before a dangerous wall collapses. The owner or guardian must be properly warned, and the warning must be directed to the right person. If the warning is invalid, the owner may not be liable for damages. Understanding these rules is crucial for determining liability in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify valid addressees
List the individuals who have authority over demolition (wilayat al-naqd) according to the text: owner, father, grandfather, guardian of minor, merchant slave, pawner of mortgaged house, and mukatib.
Each has a specific legal capacity to demolish.
2
Distinguish invalid addressees
Note that requests made to a resident (sakin), mortgagee (murtahin), or master (mawla) are not considered valid. Explain why they lack authority.
Their presence does not confer demolition authority.
3
Apply condition of continuous ability
The addressee must retain the ability to demolish until the wall falls. If the owner sells the property after the witnessing, he is freed from liability because he no longer has control.
The ruling: 'لو خرج عن ملكه بالبيع بعد الإشهاد برئ'.
4
Check timing of witnessing
The witnessing (ishhad) is only valid after the wall has actually leaned (become dangerous). Before that, there is no cause, so it is invalid.
The text: 'ولا يصح الإشهاد قبل أن يميل'.
5
Assess capacity of the requester
Both Muslims and dhimmis have equal right to request. Slaves and minors cannot make a valid request unless the master permits them to litigate, in which case they are treated as adults.
The text equates permission with capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشترط أن يكون طلب التفريع إلى من له ولاية النقض كالمالك والأب والجد والوصي في ملك الصغير والعبد التاجر كان عليه دين أو لا، وإلى الراهن في الدار المرهونة لأن القادر على الهدم وإلى المكاتب، ثم إن تلف حال بقاء الكتابة تجب عليه قيمته لتعذر الدفع وبعد العتق على عاقلة المولى، وبعد الحجر لا يجب على أحد لعدم قدرة المكاتب ولعدم الإشهاد على المولى، ولو تقدم إلى من يسكن أو للمرتهن أو للمولى لا يعتبر حتى لو سقط وأتلف شيئاً لا يضمن الساكن ولا المالك، ويشترط دوام القدرة إلى وقت السقوط حتى لو خرج عن ملكه بالبيع بعد الإشهاد برئ عن الضمان لعدم قدرته على النقض، ولا يصح الإشهاد قبل أن يميل لانعدام سببه وسوّى في المختصر بين أن يطالب بالنقض مسلم أو ذمي لأن حق المرور للكل بخلاف العبيد والصبيان لعدم قدرتهم على النقض إلا إذا أذن لهم المولى في الخصومة فحينئذ جاز طلبهم وإشهادهم لأنهم التحقوا بالبالغ
And it is a condition that the request be directed to the one who has authority over demolition, such as the owner, father, grandfather, or guardian over the property of a minor; and the merchant slave, whether he has debt or not; and to the pawner in a mortgaged house because he is able to demolish; and to the mukatib. Then if it is destroyed during the period of kitaba, its value is due from him because payment is difficult; after emancipation, it is due from the 'aqila of the master; after seclusion (hajr), it is not due from anyone because of the mukatib's inability and because there is no witnessing against the master. And if he requests from a resident, or the mortgagee, or the master, it is not considered; even if it falls and destroys something, the resident or owner is not liable. And it is a condition that the ability continues until the time of fall; so if it leaves his ownership by sale after the witnessing, he is free from liability because of his inability to demolish. And witnessing is not valid before it leans because the cause is non-existent. And the Mukhtasar equates between a Muslim and a dhimmi requesting demolition because the right of passage belongs to all, unlike slaves and boys because of their inability to demolish, unless the master gives them permission for litigation; then their request and witnessing are permissible because they become like adults.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage details the conditions for a valid request for demolition. The request must be made to someone who has legal authority to demolish the wall: the owner, father, grandfather, guardian of a minor, a merchant slave (even if in debt), the pawner of a mortgaged house, or a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission). If a request is made to a resident (non-owner), mortgagee, or master, it is invalid. The owner must retain ability to demolish until the wall falls; if he sells the property after the witnessing, he is freed from liability because he no longer has control. The witnessing is only valid after the wall has leaned (become dangerous), as there is no cause before. Both Muslims and dhimmis have equal right to request because of the public right of passage. Slaves and minors cannot give a valid request unless the master permits them to litigate, in which case they are treated as adults.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukātab): A slave who has made a contract (kitaba) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
الوصي (al-waṣiyy): Guardian appointed over the property of a minor or an incompetent person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
To whom must the request for demolition be made?
A) Any adult Muslim male.
B) The owner or legal guardian with authority to demolish.
C) The local judge or ruler.
D) The person who built the wall.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a demolition request depends on it being made to one with authority over the wall and the continuity of that authority until the wall falls.

Liability for a Wall Partly Weak and Partly Sound
Distinguishing liability when part of a wall is weak and part is sound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a partly weak wall falls and distinguish when the owner is liable for the sound part.

Core Idea
Owner is liable only if the weak part causes the sound part to fall; otherwise, no liability.

When a wall is partly weak and partly sound, the owner's liability for damage depends on causation. If the weak part collapses and pulls down the sound part, the owner is liable. But if the wall is long and the weak part remains standing while the sound part falls independently, the owner is not liable. This distinction is based on treating the two sections as separate walls when they are not structurally connected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two cases: (1) the weak part falls together with the sound part, killing someone; (2) the wall is long, the weak part weakens but does not fall, and the sound part falls independently.
The key is whether the sound part's fall is caused by the weak part's collapse.
2
Determine causation
In the first case, the weak part's fall caused the sound part to fall—hence owner is liable. In the second case, the weak part is separate (like a leaning wall), so the sound part falls on its own—no liability.
Causation is the decisive factor: if the weak part causes the fall, liability; otherwise, no liability.
3
Apply the rule
Apply the principle: if the wall is long enough that the weak and sound parts are effectively independent, treat them as two walls. The fall of a sound part without the weak part is 'hadar' (no liability).
The analogy to two separate walls clarifies the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان بعض الحائط صحيحاً وبعضه واه فأشهد عليه فسقط الواهي وغير الواهي وقتل إنساناً يضمن صاحب الحائط إلا أن يكون الحائط طويلاً بحيث وهى بعضه ولم يوه البعض فحينئذ يضمن ما أصاب الواهي ولا يضمن ما أصابه الذي لم يوه لأنه إذا كان كذلك صار بمنزلة حائطين أحدهما صحيح والآخر مائل وأشهد عليهم فلم يسقط المائل وسقط الصحيح فيكون هدراً.
And if some of the wall is sound and some weak, and he witnessed against him, and the weak part fell and also the non-weak part fell and killed a person, the owner is liable. Unless the wall is long such that some of it weakened and some did not weaken; then he is liable for what the weak part hit and not liable for what the non-weak part hit, because in that case it becomes like two walls: one sound and one leaning; if he witnessed against him and the leaning one did not fall but the sound one fell, then it is goalless (no liability).
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a wall is partly weak and partly sound. If both sections fall together and cause death, the owner is liable for the entire damage because the weak part caused the sound part to fall. However, if the wall is long and the weak section is separate, such that the two parts are effectively independent like two walls, then if the sound part falls without the weak part falling, the owner is not liable for the sound part because it is not considered dangerous. The key is whether the weak part's collapse caused the sound part to fall; if they are separate, each is treated independently.

Key Terms
واه (wāhin): Weak or dilapidated part of a wall that is likely to collapse.
هدر (hadar): Goalless; no blood money or liability is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always liable regardless.
B) When the wall is long and the weak part does not fall, so the sound part falls independently.
C) Only if the weak part was repaired.
D) If the victim was a non-Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Liability for a falling wall depends on whether the weak part caused the sound part to collapse; otherwise, the fall is without compensation.

Foundling's Leaning Wall: Liability of Bayt al-Mal
Ruling on foundling's wall causing death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that when a foundling's wall kills someone, the blood money is paid from Bayt al-Mal, and explain the reasoning based on the foundling's lack of 'aqila and inheritance to the public treasury.

Core Idea
A foundling's blood money liability falls on Bayt al-Mal because his inheritance returns there.

This lesson examines a specific case in diyat (blood money) involving a foundling (laqīṭ) who owns a leaning wall that collapses and kills a person. The Hanafi ruling is that the blood money is paid from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal). We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a foundling's leaning wall falling and causing death. The ruling is that the blood money (diyah) is wajib upon Bayt al-Mal.
2
Identify the liable party
Normally, the 'aqila (agnatic group) of the person whose property caused the death pays the diyah. However, a foundling has no known 'aqila.
3
Understand the reasoning
The text gives the reason: 'because his inheritance goes to Bayt al-Mal'. The public treasury ultimately inherits the foundling's wealth, so it also bears the liability for his torts.
4
Apply to a similar case
The same ruling applies to a person who was a disbeliever and becomes Muslim (and has no 'aqila).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه أيضاً : اللقيط له حائط مائل وأشهد عليه فسقط الحائط وأتلف إنساناً كان دية القتيل في بيت المال لأن ميراثه يكون لبيت المال، وكذا الكافر إذا أسلم.
And in it also: A foundling has a leaning wall and he witnessed against him, then the wall fell and destroyed a person; the blood money of the killed person is in the Bayt al-Mal because his inheritance goes to the Bayt al-Mal. And likewise a disbeliever when he becomes Muslim.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage addresses the case of a foundling (a child of unknown parentage) who owns a leaning wall that falls and kills someone. Since the foundling has no known 'aqila (agnatic group) to pay the blood money, and his inheritance ultimately goes to the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal), the blood money is paid from the Bayt al-Mal. Similarly, if a person who was a disbeliever becomes a Muslim and has a leaning wall, his blood money liability may also be from Bayt al-Mal if he has no 'aqila. The principle is that when there is no 'aqila, the state treasury bears the liability.

Key Terms
اللقيط (al-laqīṭ): A foundling child of unknown parentage, found abandoned.
بيت المال (Bayt al-Māl): The public treasury of the Islamic state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the foundling's own property.
B) From the 'aqila of the foundling.
C) From Bayt al-Mal (public treasury).
D) From the state's budget for orphans.

Key Takeaway
A foundling's blood money for his property causing death is paid by Bayt al-Mal due to his lack of 'aqila and inheritance escheat.

Person Falling from a Wall onto Another
Liability for accidental death from falling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a person who falls from a wall and kills another is liable for blood money according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Liability for falling depends on the victim's action: no liability if walking, full diyat if stationary.

In Hanafi tort law, accidental death from a fall is not automatically compensable. The ruling hinges on the victim's own conduct: a walker is exercising his right without transgression, so the fallen person is not liable. But if the victim is stationary—standing or sitting—the fallen person must pay blood money. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two cases: (1) the wall itself falls with the person, and (2) the person falls without the wall falling. Focus on the second scenario.
2
Determine liability in the second scenario
If the fallen person kills someone below, he is liable for blood money. But there is an exception based on the victim's state.
3
Distinguish the victim's state
If the victim was walking in the road, there is no liability on the fallen person. If the victim was stationary (standing or sitting), liability applies.
The key concept is transgression: the walker is not a transgressor, so the fallen person's act is not compensable.
4
Apply the rule
Given a scenario, check whether the victim was walking or stationary. Then state whether blood money is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان الرجل على حائط له والحائط مائل أو غير مائل فسقط الرجل بالحائط من غير فعله وأصاب إنساناً فقتله كان ضامناً لما هلك بالحائط إن كان أشهد عليه في نقضه ولا ضمان عليه فيما سواه . وإن كان هو الذي سقط من أعلى الحائط على إنسان من غير أن يسقط به الحائط وقتل أنساناً كان هو ضامناً دية المقتول، إن مات الساقط بمن كان في الطريق، فإن كان يمشي في الطريق فلا ضمان عليه لأنه غير متعد في المشي، وإن كان واقفاً في الطريق قائماً أو قاعداً كانت دية الساقط عليه .
And if a man is on his wall, and the wall is leaning or not leaning, and the man falls with the wall through no act of his own and hits a person and kills him, he is liable for what was destroyed by the wall if he had witnessed against him regarding its demolition, and there is no liability upon him for what is besides that. And if he is the one who fell from the top of the wall onto a person without the wall falling with him, and killed a person, then he is liable for the blood money of the killed person. If the one who fell died due to the person in the road, then if that person was walking in the road, there is no liability upon him because he is not transgressing in walking; and if he was standing in the road, standing or sitting, then the blood money is upon the one who fell.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two scenarios. First, if a person is on a wall and the wall falls (due to its own weakness) and the person falls with it, the person is only liable for damage caused by the wall if he had been asked to demolish it; otherwise no liability for the wall's damage. Second, if a person falls from a wall onto another person without the wall falling, he is liable for blood money. However, if the person who was hit was walking in the road, the fallen person is not liable because the walking person is not transgressing (the road is for walking). But if the person was standing (obstructing?), then the fallen person owes blood money. This illustrates how fault and transgression affect liability.

Key Terms
متعد (muta'addin): Transgressor; one who acts outside legal bounds, causing liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a person who falls from a wall and kills someone below not liable for blood money?
A) If the wall was leaning and he had witnessed demolition.
B) If the person below was walking in the road.
C) If the person below was standing still.
D) If the wall was not his property.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, the victim's behavior (walking vs stationary) determines the liability of the fallen person; walking implies no transgression, thus no liability.

Delay in Demolition of a Dangerous Wall
Rulings when a delay occurs in demolishing a dangerous wall

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid delays in demolition of a dangerous wall and state the liability consequences.

Core Idea
Only a judge’s delay can suspend liability; private delay does not protect the owner.

This passage from al-Jami' al-Saghir presents three scenarios involving a dangerous wall. It clarifies when the owner is liable for damage from the collapse and when he is excused. The key factor is who authorizes the delay: the judge or a private party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal duty
The owner of a leaning wall must demolish it to prevent harm. This duty is wajib. The passage examines when delay in fulfilling this duty results in liability for damage.
2
Distinguish the source of delay
The passage presents three scenarios: owner actively seeking demolition, owner obtaining delay from the judge, and owner obtaining delay from the neighbor. The liability outcome depends on who authorized the delay.
Only the judge's delay has legal effect; private delay does not change liability.
3
Analyze each outcome
Scenario 1: Owner seeks demolition but wall falls before he finds someone – no liability. Scenario 2: Judge grants extension – liability if wall falls during extension. Scenario 3: Neighbor grants extension – no liability during extension, but liability after extension ends.
The judge's delay places responsibility on the owner because he requested the delay.
4
Apply the overarching rule
The general rule: If the owner delays without a valid excuse (like actively seeking demolition), he is liable. Only a judge's authorization to delay temporarily suspends liability. Private agreement does not affect the legal duty.
This highlights the authority of the judge in such matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الجامع الصغير : أشهد عليه في حائط مائل له فذهب يطلب من يهدمه وكان في ذلك حتى سقط الحائط لا يضمن شيئاً. وفيه أيضاً رجل أشهد عليه في حائط مائل إلى دار رجل فسأل صاحب الحائط المائل من القاضي أن يؤجله يومين أو ثلاثة أو ما أشبه ذلك ففعل القاضي ذلك ثم . الحائط وأتلف شيئاً كان الضمان واجباً على صاحب الحائط، ولو وجد التأجيل من صاحب الدار فوقع الحائط في مدة التأجيل وأفسد شيئاً لا يجب الضمان ولو سقط الحائط بعد مدة التأجيل كان ضامناً وفيه أيضاً : رجل أشهد عليه في حائط مائل في الطريق الأعظم وطلب الحائط من القاضي أن يؤجله يوماً أو يومين أو ثلاثة ففعل القاضي ذلك الحائط المائل فأتلف شيئاً كان الضمان واجباً، وكذلك في هذه المسألة، ولو لم يؤخره القاضي ولكن أخرء الذي أشهد عليه لا يصح لا في حق غيره ولا في حق نفسه.
He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man witnessed against him regarding a leaning wall of his, then he went seeking someone to demolish it and he was in that (process) until the wall fell; he is not liable for anything. And in it also: A man witnessed against him regarding a leaning wall belonging to a man, so the owner of the leaning wall asked the judge to give him a two-day or three-day extension or something similar, and the judge did that, then the wall fell and destroyed something; the liability is wajib upon the owner of the wall. And if he obtained the extension from the owner of the house, and the wall fell during the extension period and spoiled something, liability is not obligatory. And if the wall fell after the extension period, he is liable. And in it also: A man witnessed against him regarding a leaning wall on the main road, and the owner of the wall asked the judge to give him a one-day, two-day, or three-day extension, and the judge did that, then the leaning wall fell and destroyed something; liability is wajib. And likewise in this issue. And if the judge did not delay him but the one who witnessed delayed him, it is not valid, neither in his right nor in the right of others.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage from al-Jami' al-Saghir presents several rulings on delay in demolition. First, if a man is asked to demolish his dangerous wall and he goes out to find someone to do it, but the wall falls while he is actively seeking help, he is not liable because he did not procrastinate. Second, if the owner of a leaning wall asks the judge for a few days' extension and the judge grants it, and the wall falls during that period, the owner is liable for damages. However, if the extension is given by the neighbor (the one who requested demolition), the owner is not liable if the wall falls during that period. If it falls after the extension, the owner is liable. Third, the same rule applies for a wall on a main road. Importantly, if the delay is granted by the requester (not the judge), it is not valid – it does not protect the owner from liability. This underscores that only a judge's delay can temporarily suspend liability; private agreement does not change the legal duty.

Key Terms
التأجيل (al-ta'jīl): Delay or extension of time granted by the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who must grant the delay for the owner to be exempt from liability during the delay period?
A) The neighbor who requested demolition.
B) The judge (qadi).
C) Any adult Muslim witness.
D) The owner himself.

Key Takeaway
Only a judge can authorize a delay that temporarily suspends liability; private extensions are legally ineffective.

Liability for Indirect Damage from Collapse: The Hanafi Dispute
Dispute between Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on liability for things knocked down by falling wall

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on liability for indirect damage from a collapsing wall and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Liability for collateral damage from a collapsing wall is wajib according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf limits it to the direct debris.

When a wall collapses after the owner is warned, a dispute arises over what damages he must compensate. Does liability extend only to the wall itself, or to everything it destroys? The three main Hanafi imams differ, and understanding this difference is key to applying the school's rulings on causation and liability.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling held by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani: the owner is liable for everything destroyed by the collapse of the wall.
This is the majority Hanafi view.
2
Identify the opposing view
State Abu Yusuf's position: the owner is only liable for the wall itself (al-naqḍ), not for other property knocked down, unless specifically warned about that property.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority within the school.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Understand Abu Yusuf's reasoning: the warning was only about the wall; other damage is not directly foreseeable and thus not compensable.
This reflects a narrower conception of legal causation.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the collapse of the wall is the direct cause of all subsequent damage; the warning encompasses all consequences.
They view the chain of causation as unbroken.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in a case where the wall falls and destroys a parked bicycle, Abu Yusuf would not require compensation for the bicycle, while the others would.
This affects fatwa in property damage claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي شرح الطحاوي: ولو أشهد على حائط فسقط فما سقط بنقضه فإنه يضمن في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف ما تلف بالنقض لا يضمن إلا إذا اشهد على النقض.
And in Sharh al-Tahawi: And if he witnessed regarding a wall and it fell, then whatever falls by its falling (i.e., parts of the wall itself), he is liable according to the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And Abu Yusuf said: what is destroyed by the falling (i.e., knocked down by the collapse) is not liable unless he witnessed regarding that specific falling object.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage records a dispute among the three main Hanafi imams. When a wall collapses and causes damage, there are two types of damage: (1) the wall itself falling (direct), and (2) objects that are knocked down by the falling wall (indirect). According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, if the owner was asked to demolish the wall and it falls, he is liable for everything that is destroyed by the wall's collapse, including both the wall itself and any property it damages. However, Abu Yusuf restricts liability to only the wall itself (i.e., the parts that fall) and holds that the owner is not liable for other objects that are knocked down by the falling wall unless he was specifically asked to secure those objects from potential damage. This reflects a difference in the scope of foreseeability and causation. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is typically that of Abu Hanifa, but the text does not explicitly state tarjih.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqḍ): What falls from the wall; the debris or the wall itself.
أشهد (ashhada): He caused to witness; i.e., gave a legal warning or notification.
تلف (talafa): Perished; destroyed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, when is the owner liable for property damaged by a falling wall?
A) Always, even without warning.
B) Only for the wall itself, unless the specific property was mentioned in the warning.
C) Never, because the owner had no control.
D) Only if the property was owned by a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
In intra-Hanafi disputes on liability for indirect damage, the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that all consequences of a warned hazard are compensable, while Abu Yusuf restricts liability to the immediate object warned.

Liability for a Tilting Wall After Demand to Demolish
Ruling statement with key conditional restrictions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a tilting wall and identify the conditions that trigger liability.

Core Idea
No liability for a falling wall unless the owner was formally asked to demolish it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key condition in the Hanafi law of damages. The owner of a tilting wall is not automatically liable if it falls and causes harm. Liability only attaches after a formal demand to demolish is made and ignored, and additionally depends on the victim's state. This lesson examines those conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal question
Who is liable when a tilting wall falls and causes death? The passage begins with a general rule and then restricts it.
2
Locate the restrictive phrase
The author writes 'قيد بقوله طولب بنقضه' – this is the key. Determine what it restricts (the preceding statement of liability).
The word 'قيد' indicates a condition.
3
Analyze the demand condition
What does 'طلب بنقضه' entail? A formal request to demolish. Without this, no liability.
The demand must be explicit and formal.
4
Examine the victim's state distinction
The passage contrasts a walking victim vs a stationary victim. Determine how this affects liability.
If walking, no liability; if stationary, liability arises.
5
Synthesize the complete rule
The owner is liable if (a) formally asked to demolish, (b) fails to act, (c) the wall falls and kills a person who was stationary in the road.
Both conditions must be met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يحدث الرجل في الطريق سقط هو ضامناً دية المقتول، إن مات الساقط بمن كان في الطريق، فإن كان يمشي في الطريق فلا ضمان عليه لأنه غير متعد في المشي، وإن كان واقفاً في الطريق قائماً أو قاعداً كانت دية الساقط عليه . قيد بقوله طولب بنقضه لأنه لو سقط وأتلف قبل أن يطالب بنقضه لا يضمن.
Chapter of what a man does in the road. It falls, he is liable for the diyah of the killed one. If the fallen died by someone in the road: if he was walking in the road, no liability because he is not transgressing in walking; if he was standing in the road, standing or sitting, the diyah of the fallen is on him. He restricted it by his saying: 'asked to demolish it', because if it falls and destroys before he is asked to demolish it, he is not liable.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq. The key condition for liability is that the owner was formally asked to demolish the tilting wall. If the wall falls and kills someone before any demand, there is no liability. The ruling further distinguishes based on the victim's state: if the victim was walking, the owner is not liable because the victim is not considered a transgressor (ghayr muta'addi). However, if the victim was standing or sitting, the owner is liable because the victim is stationary and the owner's negligence caused the harm. The restriction 'tuliba bi-naqdihi' is essential: only after a formal request does the owner become liable for failing to act.

Key Terms
سقط (saqata): The falling of the tilting wall
ضامن (dāmin): Liable for compensation (diyah)
دية (diyah): Blood money payable for wrongful death
طولب بنقضه (ṭūliba bi-naqḍihi): Formally asked to demolish the wall

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the owner of a tilting wall liable for the diyah of a person killed by its fall?
A) Whenever the wall falls and kills someone
B) Only if the owner had been formally asked to demolish it and failed
C) Only if the victim was walking
D) Only if the wall was newly built

Key Takeaway
The owner of a tilting wall is liable for diyah only if formally asked to demolish and fails, and the victim is stationary.

The Three Conditions for 'Aqila Liability in Wall-Fall Cases
Conditions for 'aqila liability and an illustrative case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions required for 'aqila liability and apply them to a case of accidental death from a falling wall.

Core Idea
For 'aqila to be liable, three facts must be proven: death from wall fall, ownership of wall, and valuation of diyah.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rules governing when the 'aqila (clan) must pay diyah for accidental death caused by a falling wall. The key is that the 'aqila's liability is contingent on specific proofs; even an admission by the owner does not shift liability to them. We will explore the three conditions from Sharh al-Tahawi and see them applied in a case from al-Muntaqa.

Guided Path
1
State the core ruling
Identify the main legal rule: the 'aqila are not liable for diyah unless three specific things are proven.
This is a conditional liability — the burden is on the claimant to prove all three.
2
List the three conditions
Extract the three requirements from the text: (1) that the deceased died from the wall's fall, (2) that the wall belonged to the accused, and (3) the valuation (taqwim) of the diyah.
If any one is denied, the 'aqila are off the hook.
3
Understand the effect of admission
Note that even if the wall owner confesses to all three, it does not bind the 'aqila — only his own wealth becomes liable.
The 'aqila's liability is separate; admission by the principal does not transfer obligation to the clan.
4
Apply to the al-Muntaqa case
Read the al-Muntaqa excerpt: a claimant alleges a house is his but another holds it; a tilting wall endangers. The holder must demolish it; after proof, if the wall kills, liability follows the same principle.
This case analogizes to the 'aqila conditions: ownership and causation must be proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي شرح الطحاوي ولو أنكرت العاقلة أن تكون الدار له لا عقل عليهم ولا يضمن حتى يشهدوا على التقويم عليه وعلى أنه مات من سقوط الحائط عليه وأن الدار له، فإذا أنكرت العاقلة واحداً من هذه الأشياء الثلاثة فلا تعقل ولو أقر رب الدار بهذا الأشهاد الثلاثة تلزم في ماله ولا تجب على العاقلة. وفي المنتقى : رجل ادعى داراً في يد رجل وفيها حائط مائل يخاف سقوطه من الذي يقدم إليه فيه ويشهد عليه بعد بينة المدعي قال : يؤخذ الذي في يده الدار بنقضه ويشهد عليه بميله هو بمنزلة دار ادعاها وأقام البينة لم يترك البينة فإنه يتقدم بنقضه الذي في يده ثم زكيت البينة ضمن تقدم له القيمة.
And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If the 'aqila deny that the house is his, no 'aql is upon them and he is not liable until they (the claimants) testify to the valuation against him and that he died from the fall of the wall upon him and that the house is his. If the 'aqila deny one of these three things, they do not pay 'aql. Even if the owner of the house confesses to these three testimonies, it is binding on his wealth and not obligatory on the 'aqila. And in al-Muntaqa: A man claimed a house in the hand of another man, and in it is a tilting wall feared to fall. From the one who proceeds to him in it and testifies against him after the plaintiff's evidence, he said: The one in whose hand is the house is taken to demolish it, and testimony is given against him of its tilt. It is like a house he claimed and he brought evidence; the evidence is not left; he proceeds to demolish it from the one in whose hand it is, then if the evidence is corroborated, he is liable and the value is given to him.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This section discusses the role of the 'aqila (the clan that pays diyah on behalf of a person for accidental killing). When a wall falls and kills someone, the 'aqila of the wall owner are liable. However, if the 'aqila deny that the house (and thus the wall) belongs to the accused, they are not liable unless three things are proven: (1) that the deceased died from the fall of the wall, (2) that the wall belonged to the accused, and (3) the valuation (taqwim) of the diyah. If any of these three is denied, the 'aqila are not required to pay. Even if the wall owner admits, it does not make the 'aqila liable; only the owner's wealth is liable. The al-Muntaqa case discusses a similar situation where a person claims a house in another's hand, and the wall is tilting; the holder of the house is ordered to demolish it.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The clan or group responsible for paying diyah on behalf of a person for accidental killing
عقل (ʿaql): Payment of diyah by the 'aqila
تقويم (taqwīm): Valuation of the diyah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the 'aqila deny that the house belongs to the accused wall owner?
A) They are still liable
B) They are not liable unless three specific things are proven
C) The owner's confession makes them liable
D) They must pay half the diyah

Key Takeaway
The 'aqila are only liable for diyah when all three conditions are proven; denial of any condition or even the owner's confession does not shift liability to them.

Liability for a Tilting Wall: Judge's Delay vs. Plaintiff's Delay
Distinguishes liability when delay is granted by judge versus by plaintiff

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish liability for damage caused by a tilting wall when a delay is granted by the judge versus when granted by the plaintiff.

Core Idea
Delay from the judge triggers liability; delay from the plaintiff absolves during the delay period.

When a tilting wall threatens damage, the owner may be ordered to demolish it. If the owner requests a delay, who bears liability if the wall falls? The Hanafi tradition, as recorded in al-Jami' al-Saghir, draws a sharp distinction between a delay granted by the judge and one granted by the plaintiff (the person who testified). Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining responsibility in property injury cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline case without delay
Read the first case: the owner is notified (ashhad) and goes looking for someone to demolish, but the wall falls before he can do so. He is not liable because he acted promptly and did not delay.
This shows that mere notification does not automatically impose liability; the owner must be at fault.
2
Analyze the case of judge-granted delay
Now read the case where the owner asks the judge for a delay and the judge grants it. If the wall falls during the delay, the owner is liable. The judge's permission does not exempt the owner from responsibility.
The judge's delay is only a procedural indulgence, not a guarantee against loss.
3
Analyze the case of plaintiff-granted delay
Next, the text considers when the plaintiff (the neighbor or the one who testified) grants the delay. If the wall falls during that period, the owner is not liable. The plaintiff has effectively waived his right to immediate action.
However, if the wall falls after the delay period expires, the owner becomes liable again.
4
Recognize the invalidity of plaintiff's delay without judge
Finally, the passage notes that if the plaintiff attempts to delay the owner without the judge's involvement, that delay is ineffective. It does not absolve the owner and is not valid even regarding the plaintiff himself.
The plaintiff cannot unilaterally alter the legal timeline; the judge's authority is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الجامع الصغير : أشهد عليه في حائط مائل له فذهب يطلب من يهدمه وكان في ذلك حتى سقط الحائط لا يضمن شيئاً. وفيه أيضاً رجل أشهد عليه في حائط مائل إلى دار رجل فسأل صاحب الحائط المائل من القاضي أن يؤجله يومين أو ثلاثة أو ما أشبه ذلك ففعل القاضي ذلك ثم [سقط] الحائط وأتلف شيئاً كان الضمان واجباً على صاحب الحائط، ولو وجد التأجيل من صاحب الدار فوقع الحائط في مدة التأجيل وأفسد شيئاً لا يجب الضمان ولو سقط الحائط بعد مدة التأجيل كان ضامناً وفيه أيضاً : رجل أشهد عليه في حائط مائل في الطريق الأعظم وطلب الحائط من القاضي أن يؤجله يوماً أو يومين أو ثلاثة ففعل القاضي ذلك الحائط المائل فأتلف شيئاً كان الضمان واجباً، وكذلك في هذه المسألة، ولو لم يؤخره القاضي ولكن أخرء الذي أشهد عليه لا يصح لا في حق غيره ولا في حق نفسه.
He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man was testified against regarding a tilting wall of his, so he went to seek someone to demolish it, and while doing so the wall fell; he is not liable for anything. And also in it: A man was testified against regarding a tilting wall leaning towards a man's house, so the owner of the tilting wall asked the judge to give him two or three days' delay or similar, and the judge did so, then the wall fell and destroyed something; the liability is obligatory upon the owner of the wall. And if the delay was granted by the owner of the house (the neighbor), and the wall fell during the delay period and damaged something, no liability is obligatory. And if the wall fell after the delay period, he is liable. And also in it: A man was testified against regarding a tilting wall in the main road, and the wall owner asked the judge to give him a day or two or three delay, and the judge did so, then the tilting wall fell and destroyed something; liability is obligatory. And likewise in this case. And if the judge did not delay him, but the one who testified against him delayed him, it is not valid neither in his right nor in his own right.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This section from al-Jami' al-Saghir (a core Hanafi text) presents several cases about delay granted to the owner of a tilting wall. The key distinction is whether the delay is granted by the judge (qadi) or by the plaintiff (the person who testified). If the judge grants a delay, the wall owner is liable if the wall falls during the delay period (because the owner is still under order to demolish but the judge allowed him time). However, if the plaintiff himself grants the delay, and the wall falls during that period, the owner is not liable because the plaintiff has effectively waived his right to demand immediate demolition. If the wall falls after the delay period expires, the owner becomes liable again. Additionally, a delay granted by the plaintiff without the judge's involvement is not legally effective and does not absolve the owner.

Key Terms
تأجيل (ta'jīl): Granting a delay or extension of time to demolish the wall
أشهد عليه (ashhada 'alayhi): Took testimonial evidence against him (i.e., formally notified him of the danger and required him to demolish)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the plaintiff (the neighbor) grants a delay to the owner of a tilting wall, and the wall falls during the delay period, who is liable?
A) The owner is liable
B) The plaintiff is liable
C) No one — the owner is not liable
D) Both are liable

Key Takeaway
The grantor of the delay determines liability: judicial delay holds the owner responsible; plaintiff's delay absolves the owner during the period.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Liability for Debris of a Tilting Wall
Presents cases from Nawadir and a dispute among Hanafi imams on debris liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on liability for debris of a tilting wall and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Debris from a fallen wall is either included in the original demand (Abu Hanifa) or demands separate notice (Abu Yusuf).

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents several nawadir cases on liability when a tilting wall falls and causes death. The key legal question is whether the owner's liability extends to the debris (naqd) of the wall after it falls, or only to the wall itself. The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that testimony against the wall covers its debris, while Abu Yusuf requires specific mention of the debris. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principles of cause and scope in tort liability.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
Identify what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue regarding liability for debris after a wall falls. According to them, testimony against the wall includes its debris, so the owner is liable for death caused by debris.
This is the majority view within the school.
2
Identify the opponent and his view
Recognize that Abu Yusuf disagrees. He holds that liability for debris only arises if testimony specifically mentioned the debris (ashhada ‘ala al-naqd). Otherwise, the owner is not liable for harm from debris.
Abu Yusuf views the debris as a separate entity after the fall.
3
Understand the basis of Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf's reasoning is that the debris is distinct from the wall; a demand to remove the wall does not automatically demand removal of the broken pieces. Thus, a separate testimony is needed.
This is a case of takhsis (specification) in the scope of the demand.
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Consider a scenario where a wall falls and a person dies from tripping on a brick. Under Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the owner's 'aqila pays diyah. Under Abu Yusuf, no liability unless the debris was explicitly mentioned in the testimony.
The practical consequence is significant – the outcome changes.
5
Consolidate with the cases from Nawadir
Review the specific cases (mosque, mukatab, father/mother, tripping on debris) to see how the rulings apply. Note which cases align with which imam's view.
The case of the man tripping on debris is explicitly attributed to Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نوادر ابن رستم : مسجد مائل حائطه فأشهد على الذي بناه. فإن وقع ذلك على رجل فقتله فالدية على العاقلة. ولو أشرع المكاتب كنيفاً أو جناحاً من حائط مائل إلى طريق المسلمين ثم أدى الكتابة وعتق ثم وقع ذلك على إنسان فقتله كان على المكاتب الأقل من دية المقتول ومن قيمته يوم قال الإشراع . في الكتاب : أن لو رجلاً أعتقه مولاه لعتاقة رجل وأبو عبد أشهد عليه في حائط مائل فلم ينقضه حتى عتق الأب ثم سقط الحائط وقتل إنساناً فديته على عاقلة الأب، ولو سقط قبل عتق الأب فالدية على عاقلة الأم بمثله ولو أشرع كنيفاً ثم عتق أبوه ثم وقع الكنيف على إنسان وقتله فالدية على عاقلة الأم . رجل أشهد عليه في حائط مائل فسقط في الطريق وعثر رجل بنقض الحائط ومات فديته على عاقلة صاحب الحائط وهذا قول محمد . وفي شرح الطحاوي: ولو أشهد على حائط فسقط فما سقط بنقضه فإنه يضمن في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف ما تلف بالنقض لا يضمن إلا إذا اشهد على النقض.
And in Nawadir Ibn Rustom: A mosque whose wall is tilting, so testimony was taken against the one who built it. If that falls on a man and kills him, the diyah is on the 'aqila. And if a mukatab extended a latrine or a canopy from a tilting wall into the Muslims' road, then he paid the kitaba and became free, then that fell on a person and killed him, the mukatab is liable for the lesser of the diyah of the killed and his value on the day of extending. In the book: That if a man's master emancipated him for the emancipation of a man, and Abu Abd was testified against regarding a tilting wall, and he did not demolish it until the father became free, then the wall fell and killed a man, his diyah is on the 'aqila of the father. And if it fell before the father's emancipation, the diyah is on the 'aqila of the mother likewise. And if he extended a latrine then his father became free, then the latrine fell on a man and killed him, the diyah is on the 'aqila of the mother. A man was testified against regarding a tilting wall, and it fell in the road, and a man tripped on the debris of the wall and died, his diyah is on the 'aqila of the wall's owner; this is the saying of Muhammad. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: And if testimony was taken regarding a wall and it fell, then what fell by its debris, he is liable according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf said: what is destroyed by the debris, he is not liable unless testimony was taken regarding the debris specifically.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage contains several cases from the Nawadir (rare transmissions) and then a clear intra-Hanafi dispute. The cases include: a mosque with a tilting wall, a mukatab (slave under contract) who extends a latrine or canopy and then is freed, a case involving a father and mother's 'aqila, and a case of a man tripping on debris. The final part is the dispute: When a wall falls and its debris causes death, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold the owner liable for damages caused by the debris as well, if he had been testified against regarding the wall. Abu Yusuf, however, limits the liability only to the direct fall of the wall itself; for the debris (the broken pieces), he would not be liable unless specifically testified against regarding the debris (i.e., the demand explicitly included removal of the debris).

Key Terms
نقض (naqḍ): Debris or broken pieces of a demolished or fallen wall
أشهد على النقض (ashhada 'alā al-naqḍ): Took testimony specifically regarding the debris (to demolish/remove it)
مكاتب (mukātib): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission (kitaba) to earn his freedom

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, when is the owner of a wall liable for death caused by the debris of the fallen wall?
A) Always, once testimony is taken against the wall
B) Only if testimony specifically mentioned the debris
C) Never, because debris is separate
D) Only if the debris was large

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school is divided on whether liability for a falling wall extends to its debris; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it does, Abu Yusuf requires separate testimony.

Liability for a Canopy (Janah) That Falls on a Public Road
Ruling on canopy fall liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the owner of a canopy projecting into a public road is liable for all deaths caused by its fall, including subsequent accidents involving the bodies.

Core Idea
The owner of a projecting canopy is liable for all deaths caused by its fall, including a second person tripping on the corpse.

This lesson examines a scenario where a canopy (janah) extends into a public road, falls, and causes a fatal chain of events. The Hanafi school holds the owner fully liable for both the initial death and the subsequent death of a person tripping over the corpse, as long as the chain is directly connected to the fall.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure and its location
The structure is a canopy (janah) that the owner built extending into the public road. This is an encroachment on public property.
The canopy is not on the owner's land.
2
Determine the cause of death
The canopy falls, its debris causes one person to trip and die. Then another person trips over the dead body and also dies. Both deaths are causally linked to the canopy's fall.
The second death is directly caused by the first death (the body on the road).
3
Apply the ruling on liability
The Hanafi ruling: the owner of the canopy is liable for the diyah (blood money) of both victims. The owner is responsible because the canopy was illegally placed in the road and is considered a source of harm.
The 'aqila (tribal surety) is not involved because this is a tort of direct causation (jinayat).
4
Compare with other scenarios
Contrast this with the case of a tilting wall where liability may shift depending on ownership and testimony. Here, the canopy's owner is always liable because the act of projecting into the road is wrongful.
This ruling is consistent with the principle that whoever causes harm through a wrongful act is liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسقوطه بلا طلب وإن مال إلى دار رجل فالطلب إلى ربها فإن أجله أو أبرأه صح حائط ضمان عليه ولا على عاقلة صاحب الحائط، ولو كان مكان الحائط جناح أخرجه إلى الطريق فوقع على الطريق فعثر إنسان بنقضه فمات وعثر رجل آخر بالقتيل ومات أيضاً فدية القتيلين جميعاً على صاحب الجناح. حائط مائل لرجل أشهد عليه في الحائط ثم إن صاحب الحائط وضع جرة لغيره على الحائط فسقط الحائط ورميت الجرة وأصابت إنساناً فقتلته فدية المقتول على صاحب الحائط، ولو عثر بالجرة وبنقضها أحد فلا ضمان على أحد. ولو باع الدار بعد الإشهاد عليه في الحائط ثم رد المشتري الدار بخيار رؤية أو بخيار رؤية أو بخيار شرط أو بخيار عيب بقضاء القاضي. وفي الخانية وغيره ثم سقط الحائط على إنسان وقتله فإنه لا ضمان عليه. وفي الخانية إلا بإشهاد مستقبل بعد الرد، ولو كان الخيار للبائع ثم سقط الحائط وأتلف شيئاً كان ضامناً لأن خيار البائع لا يبطل ولاية الإصلاح فلا يبطل الإشهاد، ولو أسقط البائع خياره وأوجب البيع بطل الإشهاد لأنه أزال الحائط عن ملكه، وفي اخراج الكنيف والجناح والميزاب لا يبطل الضمان بشيء من هذه الأشياء. وفي الكافي : لا ضمان على المشتري لأنه لم يشهد عليه في الهدم ، فإذا أشهد على المشتري بعد شرائه فهو ضامن. وفي شرح الطحاوي: ولو مال إلى سكة غير نافذة فالخصومة إلى واحد من أهل السكة، ولو مال إلى دار جاره فالخصومة إلى صاحب تلك الدار وإن مستعيراً أو مستأجراً فالإشهاد إلى السكان وليس إلى غيرهم.
By its fall without demand, and if it leans towards a man's house, the demand is to its owner; if he gives him a delay or absolves him, the wall is correct, he is liable, and not on the 'aqila of the wall's owner. And if instead of a wall there was a canopy he extended into the road, and it fell on the road, and a man tripped on its debris and died, and another man tripped on the dead body and also died, the diyah of both killed is upon the owner of the canopy. A man's tilting wall: he was testified against regarding the wall, then the wall's owner placed a jar of someone else on the wall, the wall fell and the jar was thrown and hit a man and killed him; the diyah of the killed is on the wall's owner. And if someone tripped on the jar and its debris, no liability on anyone. And if he sold the house after the testimony regarding the wall, then the buyer returned the house due to option of inspection or option of condition or option of defect by a judge's ruling, then the wall fell on a man and killed him: there is no liability on him. And in al-Khaniyya and others: except by a new testimony after the return. And if the option is for the seller, then the wall fell and destroyed something, he is liable because the seller's option does not invalidate the authority of repair, so the testimony does not become invalid. And if the seller dropped his option and confirmed the sale, the testimony becomes invalid because he removed the wall from his ownership. And in the matter of extending a latrine, canopy, and spout, liability is not invalidated by any of these things. And in al-Kafi: There is no liability on the buyer because he was not testified against regarding demolition. So if testimony is taken against the buyer after his purchase, he is liable. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If it leans towards a non-through lane, the claim is against any one of the lane's inhabitants; if it leans towards a neighbor's house, the claim is against the owner of that house, even if he is a borrower or lessee, so testimony is against the residents and not others.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This section covers various scenarios. First, for a canopy (جَنَاح) extended into the road, the owner is liable for all deaths caused by its debris and even for someone tripping on the dead body (as a sequence). For a jar placed on a tilting wall: if the wall falls and the jar kills someone, the wall owner is liable; but if someone later trips on the jar or its debris, no liability (since the jar is not part of the wall). Regarding sale: if the house is sold after testimony, and the buyer returns it due to an option (khiyar), the seller is not liable unless new testimony is taken after the return. If the option belongs to the seller, his liability continues because he retains ownership during the option period. Dropping the option and confirming the sale nullifies the testimony because the property is no longer his. Extending a latrine, canopy, or spout does not invalidate existing liability. The buyer is not liable unless specifically testified against after purchase. Finally, if the wall leans towards a non-through lane (sikka ghayr nafidha), any inhabitant can bring the claim; if towards a neighbor's house, the claim is against the owner or occupant.

Key Terms
جناح (janāḥ): A canopy or projecting structure extending from a house into the road

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A homeowner builds a wooden balcony over the public sidewalk. It collapses, killing a pedestrian. Another pedestrian trips over the body and dies. According to the Hanafi school, who is liable for the second death?
A) The municipality for not removing the body
B) No one, as it was an accident
C) The owner of the balcony
D) The first pedestrian's heirs for leaving the body

Key Takeaway
The owner of an illegal projecting structure is strictly liable for all deaths caused directly by its fall, including chain accidents.

Liability for a Jar Placed on a Tilting Wall
Ruling on jar on tilting wall liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that when a jar placed on a tilting wall falls and kills someone, the wall owner is liable, but if someone later trips on the jar debris, no liability arises.

Core Idea
The wall owner is liable for the jar's direct lethal hit, but not for subsequent tripping over the jar or its debris.

This lesson deals with a specific scenario: a tilting wall has already been testified against (for repair), then the owner places a jar belonging to someone else on top. The wall falls, the jar is thrown and kills a person. The Hanafi school holds the wall owner liable for that death. However, if someone later trips over the jar or its debris, no one is liable – because the jar is now a separate object.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
There is a tilting wall (hait ma'il). The owner has been notified (ishhad) about its danger. He then places a jar ('jar' as in a clay pot) of another person on top of the wall.
The jar belongs to someone else, but the wall owner placed it there.
2
Identify the cause of death
The wall falls, the jar is flung and hits a person, killing them. The direct cause of death is the jar hitting the person.
The jar is an object moved by the wall's fall.
3
Determine liability for the death
The wall owner is liable because he placed the jar on the wall, and the wall was already dangerous. His action of putting the jar there contributed to the harm.
Even though the jar belongs to another, the owner is the one who placed it.
4
Consider the second scenario (tripping)
Later, someone trips over the jar or its debris and dies. In this case, no liability on anyone.
The jar is now a separate object; the tripping is not directly caused by the wall owner's action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the jar scenario, if someone later trips over the jar debris and dies, who is liable?
A) The wall owner
B) The jar owner
C) No one
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Liability for an object placed on a dangerous wall is limited to the direct killing; subsequent accidents from the same object are not compensable.

Effect of Sale with Options on Liability for a Tilting Wall
Ruling on liability after sale with options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate how the Hanafi school distinguishes liability for a tilting wall after sale when options (khiyar) are involved, including the effect of dropping the option and the need for new testimony.

Core Idea
Liability for a tilting wall shifts after sale only if the buyer confirms possession without option; options (khiyar) that return ownership to seller keep liability on seller.

This lesson deals with the complex interplay of sale contracts and liability for a known dangerous wall. The Hanafi school provides detailed rules: if the buyer returns the house due to an option (khiyar al-ru'ya, al-shart, or al-'ayb), the seller's liability is not revived unless new testimony (ishhad) is taken against the seller after the return. If the seller retains an option, he remains liable. If the seller drops his option and confirms the sale, liability transfers to the buyer. This lesson clarifies these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Understand the pre-sale situation
The wall was tilting, and the seller was testified against (ishhad) requiring him to repair it. This testimony puts a liability on the owner to prevent harm.
Testimony (ishhad) is a legal notice that can serve as evidence in court.
2
Identify the type of option after sale
If the buyer returns the house due to an option (khiyar) – inspection, condition, or defect – the ownership reverts to the seller. However, the seller's liability from the old testimony is not automatically revived; a new testimony is needed.
The seller was not the owner at the time of the fall (during the buyer's possession), so the old testimony does not apply.
3
Determine liability when seller has option
If the seller retained an option (e.g., khiyar al-shart), he is still considered owner. He can repair the wall, so the old testimony remains effective. If the wall falls during his option period, he is liable.
The seller's option gives him ownership and control.
4
Consider the effect of dropping option
If the seller drops his option and confirms the sale, the wall becomes the buyer's property. The seller is no longer owner, so his old testimony is invalidated. The buyer does not inherit liability unless new testimony is taken against him.
The liability is tied to ownership, not to the wall itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This section covers various scenarios. First, for a canopy (جَنَاح) extended into the road, the owner is liable for all deaths caused by its debris and even for someone tripping on the dead body (as a sequence). For a jar placed on a tilting wall: if the wall falls and the jar kills someone, the wall owner is liable; but if someone later trips on the jar or its debris, no liability (since the jar is not part of the wall). Dropping the option and confirming the sale nullifies the testimony because the property is no longer his. Extending a latrine, canopy, or spout does not invalidate existing liability. The buyer is not liable unless specifically testified against after purchase. Finally, if the wall leans towards a non-through lane (sikka ghayr nafidha), any inhabitant can bring the claim; if towards a neighbor's house, the claim is against the owner or occupant.

Key Terms
خيار رؤية (khiyār ru'ya): Option of inspection after purchase
خيار شرط (khiyār sharṭ): Option stipulated in the contract for a specified period
خيار عيب (khiyār 'ayb): Option of defect after purchase
إشهاد (ishhad): Testimony or formal notice, often to demand repair

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller drops his option and finalizes the sale, and then the wall falls, who is liable?
A) The seller
B) The buyer
C) No one, because the testimony is invalidated
D) Both

Key Takeaway
Liability for a dangerous wall after sale hinges on ownership and the validity of testimony (ishhad); options affect who is considered owner and whether old testimony remains effective.
