Liability for a Wall Built Tilted from the Start
Commentary on the matn: liability for a wall built tilted from start, and related rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a wall built tilted from the start and apply the qiyas to similar public hazards.

Core Idea
Building a wall tilted from the start is an immediate transgression; liability arises without demand.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi tort case: a wall built leaning from the outset falls and causes harm. Does the owner need prior warning? The answer turns on the concept of ta'addi (transgression). We will see how the author analogizes to extending a canopy into the road, and explore an important intra-Hanafi difference on the degree of tilt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Locate the matn statement: 'if he built it tilting from the beginning, he is liable for what is destroyed by its fall without demand.' Note the key condition: initial tilt, not subsequent.
The ruling is daman (liability) without prior demand.
2
Understand the legal cause (illa)
The commentator explains the reason: 'because he transgressed by building.' The act of building a tilted wall is itself a transgression. Compare to extending a canopy, placing a stone, or digging a well in the road.
These analogies are qiyas: the common illa is creating a hazard in the public road.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi distinction
The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq made no distinction between slight and excessive tilt. But al-Muntaqa (by Imam Muhammad) distinguishes: slight tilt (inevitable) does not create liability; excessive tilt does. Note that some mashayikh reject this distinction.
This ikhtilaf affects whether liability attaches automatically or only for excessive tilt.
4
Apply the rule to related cases
The passage extends the same principle: a beam in the road, a wall blown by wind after complaint, and a canopy/spout extended into the road. Study each scenario and how liability depends on transgression and ownership admission by the 'aqila.
The 'aqila's admission of ownership triggers diyah liability; otherwise evidence is needed.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi position
The matn is absolute (no distinction), but the distinction in al-Muntaqa is considered for fatwa by later scholars. Recognize that the relied-upon position may vary depending on the strength of the riwayah.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah from the matn, but the nawadir (Muntaqa) offer a nuanced view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن بناه مائلاً ابتداء ضمن ما تلف بسقوطه بلا طلب) لأنه تعدى بالبناء فصار كشراع الجناح ووضع الحجر وحفر البئر في الطريق. أطلق المؤلف في الميلان ولم يفرق بين يسيره وفاحشه. وفي المنتقى : إن كان يسيراً وقت البناء لا يضمن لأن الجدار لا يخلو عن يسير الميلان وإن كان فاحشاً يضمن وإن كان لم يتقدم أحد يطلب منه النقض، ولو شغل الطريق بأن أخرج جذعاً فيها فهو على التفصيل، ومن المشايخ من لا يفصل في الجذع ولا في الميلان. وفي المنتقى قال محمد حائط مائل تقدم إلى صاحبه فيه فلم يهدمه حتى ألقته الريح فهو ضامن، وليس هذا كحجر وضعه إنسان على الطريق وقلبه الريح من موضع إلى موضع فعثر به إنسان فإنه لا يضمن . وإذا أقرت العاقلة أن الدار له ضمنوا الدية كما لو أقر بجناية خطأ وصدقته العاقلة في ذلك، وكذلك الجناح والميزاب يشرعه الرجل من داره في الطريق فوقع على إنسان ومات وأنكرت العاقلة أن تكون الدار له وقالوا: وإنما أمر رب الدار بإخراج الجناح فلا ضمان عليهم إلا أن تقام البينة أن الدار له وذلك لأن إخراج الجناح من الدار التي في يده إنما يوجب الضمان على العاقلة إذا أخرج من داره إلى الطريق لا بالبينة ولا بإقرار العاقلة كأن أقر رب الدار له وكذبته العاقلة لا يعقل. وفي قاضيخان : رجل تقدم إليه في حائط مائل له فلم ينقضه حتي وقع على حائط جاره وهدمه فهو ضامن من الحائط الجار
He (the author of the sharh) said: 'And if he built it tilting from the beginning, he is liable for what is destroyed by its fall without demand.' Because he transgressed by building, so it becomes like extending a canopy, placing a stone, and digging a well in the road. The author made absolute in the tilt and did not distinguish between slight and excessive. And in al-Muntaqa: If it was slight at the time of building, he does not guarantee, because a wall is not free of slight tilt; if it was excessive, he guarantees even if no one previously asked him to demolish. And if he occupied the road by extending a beam into it, it is on the detail. Some shaykhs do not differentiate in the beam nor in the tilt. And in al-Muntaqa, Muhammad said: A tilting wall: complaint was made to its owner about it, and he did not demolish it until the wind threw it; he is liable. And this is not like a stone placed by a person on the road and the wind turned it from place to place and a man tripped on it; he does not guarantee. And if the 'aqila admit that the house is his, they are liable for the diyah, just as if he confessed to an accidental injury and the 'aqila confirmed him in that. Likewise, the canopy and spout that a man extends from his house into the road, and it falls on a man and dies, and the 'aqila deny that the house is his and say: 'The owner of the house only ordered the extension of the canopy,' so no liability on them unless evidence is established that the house is his. This is because the extension of the canopy from the house in his hand only obligates liability on the 'aqila if he extended from his house into the road; not by evidence nor by the 'aqila's admission, as if the house owner admitted and the 'aqila denied, they do not pay. And in Qadikhan: A man complained to him about his tilting wall, and he did not demolish it until it fell on his neighbor's wall and demolished it; he is liable for the neighbor's wall.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This is a direct commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It explains that if someone builds a wall tilting from the start, he is liable even without prior demand because the act of building itself is a transgression (ta'addi). The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) notes that the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq did not distinguish between slight and excessive tilt. However, al-Muntaqa does: a slight tilt (which is unavoidable) does not create liability; excessive tilt does. The case is analogized (qiyas) to extending a canopy, placing a stone, or digging a well in the road — all are transgressions. The passage also discusses a beam in the road, a wall blown by wind, and the 'aqila's liability if they admit ownership. It concludes with a case from Qadikhan: if a tilting wall falls on a neighbor's wall and demolishes it, the owner is liable for the neighbor's wall as well.

Key Terms
تعدى (ta'addā): Transgression or exceeding lawful bounds; creating a danger that leads to liability
شراع الجناح (shirā' al-janāḥ): Extending a canopy (into the road)
جذع (jidh'): A beam or log extended into the road
أقرت العاقلة (aqarrat al-ʿāqila): The 'aqila confessed or admitted ownership

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal reason (illa) for liability when a wall is built tilted from the beginning?
A) The wall fell on a human
B) The act of building is a transgression (ta'addi)
C) The owner was asked to demolish
D) The wall was built without permission

Key Takeaway
A wall built tilted from the start creates immediate liability due to transgression; no prior demand is necessary, though some Hanafi authorities distinguish slight from excessive tilt.

Diyat — Blood Money

Liability for Leaning Walls and Projecting Structures
Compiles rulings on liability when a leaning wall or projecting beam causes harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a building owner is liable for harm caused by a leaning wall or projecting structure, and apply the Hanafi rules on transgression (taʿaddī) and bearing witness (ishhād).

Core Idea
Liability for structural hazards hinges on transgression: building leaning, extending beams, or failing to act after warning.

Welcome to this lesson on the Hanafi framework for liability arising from structures that intrude into public or private space. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq compiles several sub-rulings on leaning walls and projecting beams, illustrating when the owner or his ʿāqila must pay blood-money (diya) for resulting deaths or injuries. You will learn to distinguish cases where liability is automatic (e.g., building a wall leaning from the start) from those requiring prior warning, and to apply the key concept of taʿaddī (transgression) that forms the basis of the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core liability rule
Locate the statement that a wall built leaning from the start makes the owner liable for its fall even without prior demand. Note the phrase 'liability without demand' and the reasoning: because he transgressed by the construction.
This rule is in the matn: 'If he built it leaning from the beginning, he is liable for what is destroyed by its falling without demand.'
2
Analyze the proof (taʿlīl)
The proof is rational: building a leaning wall is an act of transgression (taʿaddī), analogous to spreading a sail in the road, placing a stone, or digging a well. These acts remove the structure from normal safeguards, so the owner bears strict liability.
The author compares to three examples: a ship's sail (janāḥ), a stone, and a well.
3
Distinguish slight vs. excessive leaning
From al-Muntaqā, a distinction is introduced: if the leaning was slight at construction, there is no liability because walls always lean a bit; if excessive, liability applies even without demand. Understand that the ruling is not absolute but depends on the degree.
The author of Kanz al-Daqāʾiq did not distinguish, but the commentary clarifies with the two positions.
4
Examine the effect of demand and delay
If someone demanded demolition of a leaning wall, and the owner delayed until the wind toppled it, he is liable. Compare this with the case of a stone placed on the road: if the wind moves the stone and someone trips, there is no liability—because the stone's placement is not a continuing transgression like a wall that remains.
This shows the principle that liability persists as long as the hazard remains under the owner's control.
5
Apply the ʿāqila and procedural rules
When the ʿāqila acknowledge the owner's house, they pay diya; if they deny, evidence is required. Also, if a wall was witnessed against and then the house is sold, the buyer is not liable unless a fresh witness is made after purchase. These rules protect procedural fairness and the chain of liability.
The ishhād (bearing witness) serves as a formal warning that transfers liability to subsequent owners only if renewed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات باب ما يحدث الرجل في الطريق
بسقوطه بلا طلب وإن مال إلى دار رجل فالطلب إلى ربها فإن أجله أو أبرأه صح حائط ضمان عليه ولا على عاقلة صاحب الحائط، ولو كان مكان الحائط جناح أخرجه إلى الطريق فوقع على الطريق فعثر إنسان بنقضه فمات وعثر رجل آخر بالقتيل ومات أيضاً فدية القتيلين جميعاً على صاحب الجناح. حائط مائل لرجل أشهد عليه في الحائط ثم إن صاحب الحائط وضع جرة لغيره على الحائط فسقط الحائط ورميت الجرة وأصابت إنساناً فقتلته فدية المقتول على صاحب الحائط، ولو عثر بالجرة وبنقضها أحد فلا ضمان على أحد. ولو باع الدار بعد الإشهاد عليه في الحائط ثم رد المشتري الدار بخيار رؤية أو بخيار شرط أو بخيار عيب بقضاء القاضي. وفي الخانية وغيره ثم سقط الحائط على إنسان وقتله فإنه لا ضمان عليه. وفي الخانية إلا بإشهاد مستقبل بعد الرد، ولو كان الخيار للبائع ثم سقط الحائط وأتلف شيئاً كان ضامناً لأن خيار البائع لا يبطل ولاية الإصلاح فلا يبطل الإشهاد، ولو أسقط البائع خياره وأوجب البيع بطل الإشهاد لأنه أزال الحائط عن ملكه، وفي اخراج الكنيف والجناح والميزاب لا يبطل الضمان بشيء من هذه الأشياء. وفي الكافي : لا ضمان على المشتري لأنه لم يشهد عليه في الهدم ، فإذا أشهد على المشتري بعد شرائه فهو ضامن. وفي شرح الطحاوي: ولو مال إلى سكة غير نافذة فالخصومة إلى واحد من أهل السكة، ولو مال إلى دار جاره فالخصومة إلى صاحب تلك الدار وإن مستعيراً أو مستأجراً فالإشهاد إلى السكان وليس إلى غيرهم.

قال رحمه الله : (وإن بناه مائلاً ابتداء ضمن ما تلف بسقوطه بلا طلب) لأنه تعدى بالبناء فصار كشراع الجناح ووضع الحجر وحفر البئر في الطريق. أطلق المؤلف في الميلان ولم يفرق بين يسيره وفاحشه. وفي المنتقى : إن كان يسيراً وقت البناء لا يضمن لأن الجدار لا يخلو عن يسير الميلان وإن كان فاحشاً يضمن وإن كان لم يتقدم أحد يطلب منه النقض، ولو شغل الطريق بأن أخرج جذعاً فيها فهو على التفصيل، ومن المشايخ من لا يفصل في الجذع ولا في الميلان. وفي المنتقى قال محمد حائط مائل تقدم إلى صاحبه فيه فلم يهدمه حتى ألقته الريح فهو ضامن، وليس هذا كحجر وضعه إنسان على الطريق وقلبه الريح من موضع إلى موضع فعثر به إنسان فإنه لا يضمن . وإذا أقرت العاقلة أن الدار له ضمنوا الدية كما لو أقر بجناية خطأ وصدقته العاقلة في ذلك، وكذلك الجناح والميزاب يشرعه الرجل من داره في الطريق فوقع على إنسان ومات وأنكرت العاقلة أن تكون الدار له وقالوا: وإنما أمر رب الدار بإخراج الجناح فلا ضمان عليهم إلا أن تقام البينة أن الدار له وذلك لأن إخراج الجناح من الدار التي في يده إنما يوجب الضمان على العاقلة إذا أخرج من داره إلى الطريق لا بالبينة ولا بإقرار العاقلة كأن أقر رب الدار له وكذبته العاقلة لا يعقل. وفي قاضيخان : رجل تقدم إليه في حائط مائل له فلم ينقضه حتي وقع على حائط جاره وهدمه فهو ضامن من الحائط الجار
Chapter of Blood-Money: Chapter on What a Man Causes in the Road. By its falling without demand, and if it leans into a man's house, the demand is to its owner; if he grants time or acquits him, it is valid; a wall is liability upon him and not upon the 'aqila of the owner of the wall. And if instead of the wall there was a projecting beam that he extended into the road and it fell on the road, and a person tripped over its debris and died, and another person tripped over the dead body and also died, the blood-money for both killed is upon the owner of the beam. A leaning wall of a man, he had witnesses called against him concerning the wall, then the owner of the wall placed a jar belonging to someone else on the wall, and the wall fell and the jar was thrown and hit a person and killed him: the blood-money of the killed is upon the owner of the wall. And if someone tripped over the jar and its debris, there is no liability upon anyone. And if he sold the house after bearing witness against him concerning the wall, then the buyer returned the house by option of inspection, or by option of condition, or by option of defect by a judge's ruling, as in al-Khaniyya and others, then the wall fell on a person and killed him: there is no liability upon him. And in al-Khaniyya: except by a fresh bearing of witness after the return. And if the option was for the seller, then the wall fell and destroyed something, he is liable because the seller's option does not void the capacity of repair, so the bearing of witness is not voided. And if the seller dropped his option and made the sale binding, the bearing of witness is voided because he removed the wall from his ownership. In the matter of bringing out a privy, a projecting beam, and a spout, the liability is not voided by any of these things. And in al-Kafi: There is no liability on the buyer because he was not witnessed against regarding the demolition; when he bears witness against the buyer after his purchase, he is liable. In Sharh al-Tahawi: And if it leans into a non-through alley, the dispute is with one of the alley's residents; and if it leans into the house of his neighbor, the dispute is with the owner of that house, even if he is a borrower or tenant; the bearing of witness is to the residents and not to others. He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And if he built it leaning from the beginning, he is liable for what is destroyed by its falling without demand) because he transgressed by the construction, so it became like a sail of a projecting beam, and placing a stone, and digging a well in the road. The author was absolute regarding leaning and did not distinguish between slight and excessive. In al-Muntaqa: If it was slight at the time of building, he is not liable because a wall is not free from slight leaning; and if it was excessive, he is liable even if no one had previously demanded its demolition. And if he occupied the road by extending a beam into it, it is according to the detail; some scholars do not distinguish regarding the beam nor regarding the leaning. In al-Muntaqa, Muhammad said: A leaning wall, someone went to its owner concerning it, and he did not demolish it until the wind threw it down: he is liable. And this is not like a stone that a person placed on the road and the wind turned it from place to place, and a person tripped over it, for he is not liable. And if the 'aqila acknowledge that the house is his, they guarantee the blood-money, just as if he confessed to a mistaken injury and the 'aqila verified him in that. Likewise, the projecting beam and spout that a man extends from his house into the road, and it falls on a person and dies, and the 'aqila deny that the house is his and say: 'The owner of the house only ordered the bringing out of the beam,' there is no liability on them unless evidence is established that the house is his. And that is because bringing out the beam from the house in his possession only imposes liability on the 'aqila if he brought it out from his house into the road; not by evidence nor by the acknowledgment of the 'aqila. If the owner of the house acknowledged for him and the 'aqila denied him, they do not bear responsibility. In Qadi Khan: A person went to him concerning a leaning wall of his, so he did not demolish it until it fell on the wall of his neighbor and demolished it: he is liable for the neighbor's wall.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage type is a commentary expanding on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by stating the overarching rule: if a wall leans into a public road or private property and falls without being demanded to be fixed, the owner is liable. The 'aqila (kins group) of the owner are not liable. The author then presents several sub-cases: (1) If there is a projecting beam (جناح) that falls, causing two deaths by tripping, the full blood-money for both is on the beam's owner. (2) If a jar placed on a leaning wall falls and kills someone, the wall owner is liable, but if someone independently trips over the jar or debris, there is no liability. (3) The effect of sale and option: if a wall was witnessed against, then the house is sold, the buyer is not liable unless a fresh bearing of witness occurs after the sale. If the seller retains an option, the prior witness remains effective because the seller retains authority over the wall. (4) Matters like privies, beams, and spouts do not void pre-existing liability. (5) If the wall leans into a private alley or neighbor's house, the dispute is with the affected party. The matn then focuses on a wall built leaning from the start: the owner is liable because he transgressed (تعدى) by his act, analogized to spreading a sail in the road, placing a stone, or digging a well. The author notes a distinction from al-Muntaqa: slight leaning unavoidable and thus no liability; excessive leaning always liable. After demand, if the owner delays demolition and the wall falls even by wind, he is liable. The rule differs from a stone moved by wind which does not impose liability. The passage also covers cases where the 'aqila acknowledge or deny ownership of the house from which the beam was extended, affecting liability. Finally, it states that if a person fails to demolish a leaning wall after demand and it damages a neighbor's wall, he is liable for that damage.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The group of male agnates (ʿaṣaba) who are responsible for paying blood-money (diya) for unintentional homicide or injury caused by their member.
جناح (janāḥ): A projecting beam or structure from a building that extends into the public road, similar to a sailing ship's yardarm.
إشهاد (ishhād): The act of bearing witness or having witnesses called; in this context, a formal warning to the owner about a hazardous wall.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man builds a wall that leans significantly into the public road. Before anyone demands its demolition, the wall falls and kills a passerby. According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, is the owner liable?
A) No, because no one demanded the demolition.
B) Yes, because he built it leaning from the start, which is a transgression.
C) Yes, but only if the ʿāqila acknowledge the house is his.
D) No, because the wind caused it to fall.

Key Takeaway
The owner of a leaning wall or projecting beam is liable for harm caused if the structure was built leaning excessively or if a warning was ignored; liability is rooted in the principle of transgression (taʿaddī), and procedural safeguards like ishhād and ownership proof affect who pays.

Joint Liability for a Falling Shared Wall
States the ruling when a wall shared among multiple owners falls and kills a person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joint liability when a shared wall falls and kills a person, and apply the principle of ishhād to determine who pays blood-money.

Core Idea
When a shared wall falls after warning (ishhād) to one owner, all owners are jointly liable for the full blood-money.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʼiq explains the liability for death caused by a falling wall owned by multiple partners. It establishes that a formal warning (ishhād) against one partner does not isolate liability; all owners remain jointly responsible. The commentary also explores related scenarios involving debris and cascading wall collapses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling for a shared wall
Locate the first sentence: 'A wall between five; witnesses were called against one of them, then it fell on a man: the five pay the blood-money.' This is the primary ruling.
The warning (ishhād) is against only one owner, yet all five are liable.
2
Understand the role of ishhād
Ishhād is a formal warning that establishes that the owners knew of the danger. Without it, liability may differ.
Here the warning was against one, but the ruling treats all owners as responsible for joint property.
3
Examine the parallel case of a shared house
A house shared by three; one digs a well or builds a wall that kills; that person pays two-thirds of the diya. Note the proportionality.
The two-thirds reflects the transgressor's share beyond his own one-third ownership.
4
Trace liability for subsequent damage
The passage discusses debris: if someone trips over debris after the wall falls, liability shifts to the tripping person's 'aqila. Also, if a first wall falls on a second wall and the second falls on a person, only the first owner is liable.
This clarifies that liability does not automatically follow the original falling object; it depends on causation.
5
Note the alternative opinion from al-Kafi
Al-Kafi holds that the first wall owner is liable for both the first and second fall's damages. This shows intra-Hanafi variation.
The main text's view is that the second wall's owner is not liable if the first wall caused the second to fall.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حائط بين خمسة أشهد على أحدهم فسقط على رجل ضمن خمسة الدية دار بين ثلاثة حفر أحدهم فيها بئراً أو بنى حائطاً فعطب به رجل ضمن ثلثي الدية .
ويكون ربها بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه قيمة حائطه والنقض ،له وإن شاء أخذ النقض وضمنه النقصان، ولو أراد أن يجبره على البناء كما كان ليس له ذلك. وفي الكافي : وما تلف بوقوع الأول والثاني فعلى مالك الأول ، ولم يذكر محمد رحمه الله قيمة الحائط ؛ حكي عن الشيخ الإمام شمس الأئمة الحلواني قال : تقوم الدار وحيطانها محيطة بها، وكذلك قال في المنتقى : أنا أرسل دابته في زرع غيره وأفسد ضمن قيمة الزرع وطريق معرفة قيمته أن تقوم الأرض مع الزرع الثابت فيضمن حصة الزرع، وإذا ضمن قيمة حائطه كان النقض للضامن، فلو جاء إنسان وعثر بنقض الحائط فالضمان على عاقلته المتقدم عليه، وهذا على قول محمد، وإن عثر بنقض الحائط الثاني قيل يضمن صاحب الحائط الأول، ولو أن الحائط الأول حين وقع على الحائط الثاني وهدمه وقع الحائط الثاني على رجل وقتله لا ضمان على صاحب الحائط الثاني وإنما الضمان على عاقلة صاحب الحائط الأول .
A wall between five; witnesses were called against one of them, then it fell on a man: the five pay the blood-money. A house among three; one of them dug a well in it or built a wall, and a man was killed by it: he pays two-thirds of the blood-money. And its owner has the option: if he wishes, he may claim the value of his wall and the debris belong to him; and if he wishes, he may take the debris and claim the deficiency. And if he wished to force him to rebuild as it was, he does not have that. And in al-Kafi: And what is destroyed by the fall of the first and second is upon the owner of the first. And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, did not mention the value of the wall. It is narrated from the Shaykh al-Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani that he said: The house and its walls are valued circumscribing it. And likewise it is said in al-Muntaqa: I sent his animal into another's crop and it caused damage, he guarantees the value of the crop, and the way to know its value is to value the land with the standing crop, so he guarantees the share of the crop. And if he guarantees the value of his wall, the debris belong to the guarantor. So if a person came and tripped over the debris of the wall, the liability is upon his 'aqila who were previously approached (with a demand). This is according to Muhammad's view. And if he tripped over the debris of the second wall, it is said the owner of the first wall is liable. And had the first wall, when it fell on the second wall and demolished it, and the second wall fell on a man and killed him, there is no liability on the owner of the second wall; rather the liability is on the 'aqila of the owner of the first wall.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling regarding a wall shared by five owners. When witnesses have been called against one of them (i.e., a warning about the wall's dangerous condition), and the wall falls and kills a person, all five owners are jointly liable for the full blood-money. The author then presents a parallel case: a house shared by three people, where one of them digs a well or builds a wall that causes a death; that individual pays two-thirds of the blood-money (because he is a transgressor in two-thirds of the act, as his share is one-third but he acted without permission of the others). The commentary then discusses various situations: if the wall falls and damages another object, the owner of the first wall is liable for both. The method of valuing a wall is given: the house and walls are valued as a whole, then the wall's value is derived. If a person trips over the debris after the wall has fallen, the liability is on his own 'aqila (not on the original wall owner) because the debris is now considered a separate object. The passage also clarifies a chain of falling walls: if the first wall falls on a second wall, causing it to fall on a person, the first wall's owner is solely liable; the second wall's owner is not liable.

Key Terms
أشهد على أحدهم (ushhida ʿalā aḥadihim): Witnesses were called against one of the partners, meaning a formal warning about the wall's dangerous condition was made before that partner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wall is shared equally among five people. After a warning (ishhād) against one of them, the wall falls and kills someone. According to the passage, who pays the blood-money?
A) Only the one who was warned.
B) All five partners jointly.
C) The 'aqila of the warned partner.
D) The owner of the land where the wall stood.

Key Takeaway
Joint owners of a wall are jointly and severally liable for blood-money when the wall falls after a warning (ishhād) to any one of them.

The Right to Demand Removal for Leaning Walls and the Validity of Acquittal
Ruling with brief commentary (sharh).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who has the right to demand removal of a leaning wall and explain the effect of granting time or acquittal.

Core Idea
A wall leaning into private property gives only the owner the right to demand removal; acquittal or deferment is valid for private rights but not public roads.

When a wall leans into a neighbor's airspace, the property owner's private right is violated. This lesson clarifies who can demand rectification, and whether the wall owner can be released from liability by the affected party. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between private and public rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the affected property right
Recognize that the leaning wall encroaches on the airspace of the neighbor's house. This is a private right (ḥaqq khāṣṣ).
Only the owner of the invaded property has the right to demand removal.
2
Determine who can demand
The text states 'al-ṭalab ilā rabbihā' – the claim is upon its owner. If someone else lives there (e.g., tenant), they cannot demand directly but can notify the owner.
Tenants do not have the right of khuṣūma over the airspace.
3
Analyze the validity of acquittal and deferment
If the owner grants time (ajjalah) or acquits (abra'a) the wall owner, it is valid. The wall owner is not liable if the wall falls within that period.
This contrasts with a public road where only the judge can such actions.
4
Extend the ruling to similar cases
The phrase 'into a man's house' is an example; the same applies if the upper part leans into the lower or vice versa.
The ruling is not restricted to the specific wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن مال إلى دار رجل فالطلب إلى ربها لأن الحق له على الخصوص، وإذا كان يسكنها غيره كان له أن يطالبه لأن له المطالبة بإزالة ما شغل .هواها قال رحمه الله : ( فإن أجله أو أبرأه صح) بخلاف الطريق إن أجله صاحب الدار أو أبرأه جاز تأجيله وإبراؤه حتى لو سقط في الإبراء وقبل مضي المدة في التأجيل لا يضمن لأن الحق له على ما ذكرناه بخلاف ما إذا مال للطريق العام فأجله القاضي أو من أشهد عليه أو أبرأه لا يصح التأجيل والإبراء لما ذكرنا وقوله «إلى دار رجل» مثال وليس بقيد حتى لو مال العلو إلى الأسفل أو الأسفل إلى العلو فالحكم كذلك؛ كذا في قاضيخان قال رحمه الله : (حائط بين خمسة أشهد على أحدهم فسقط على رجل ضمن خمسة الدية دار بين ثلاثة حفر أحدهم فيها بئراً أو بنى حائطاً فعطب به رجل ضمن ثلثي الدية) وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : يضمن النصف في الصورتين لأن التلف بنصيب من أشهد عليه يعتبر وبنصيب من لم يشهد عليه ،هدر، وفي الحفر باعتبار ملكه غير متعد باعتبار ملك شريكه متعد وكانا قسمين فانقسما نصفين عليهما. وللإمام أن الموت حصل بعلة واحدة وهي القتل فيضاف التلف إلى العلة الواحدة ثم يقسم على أربابها بقدر الملك. فإن قيل : الواحد من شيئاً . من الحائط فكيف يصح تقدمه إليه؟ قلنا: إن لم يتمكن من هدم نصيبه يتمكن من اصلاحه بالمرافعة إلى الحاكم وبه يحصل الغرض وهو إزالة الضرر. وفي المحيط قال : يقدر على هدم نصيبه بحكم الحاكم ومطالبة الباقين بالنقض فيكون قادراً على النقض بهذا الطريق. ولم يذكر الفرق للإمام بين المسألتين حيث يضمن خمس الدية وفي الحائط، ويضمن ثلث الدية فيما إذا حفر وبنى في دار والفرق بينهما أن كل حجر وضعه أو حفره فهو متعدٍ في ثلثي الوضع والحفر وليس متعدياً في الثلث فلهذا يضمن الثلثين.
الشركاء لا يقدر أن يهدم .
He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And if it leans into a man's house, the demand is upon its owner because the right is specifically his. And if someone else resides in it, he may demand because he has the right to demand removal of what occupies its air.) He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him, said: (Then if he grants time or acquits him, it is valid) unlike the road. If the owner of the house grants time or acquits him, his granting time and acquittal are permissible, so that even if the wall falls during the period of acquittal or before the expiry of the time in the case of deferment, he is not liable because the right is his, as we mentioned. Unlike when it leans into a public road: if the judge grants time or the person who bore witness or acquits him, the deferment and acquittal are not valid for the reason we mentioned. And his statement 'into a man's house' is an example and not a restriction, so that even if the upper part leans into the lower or the lower into the upper, the ruling is the same. Thus in Qadi Khan. He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him, said: (A wall between five; witnesses were called against one of them, then it fell on a man: the five pay the blood-money. A house among three; one of them dug a well in it or built a wall, and a man was killed by it: he pays two-thirds of the blood-money.) And this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. And the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He pays half in both cases, because the destruction caused by the share of the one witnessed against is considered, and by the share of the one not witnessed against is null. And in digging, considering his own property is not transgression, considering the partner's property is transgression, and they are two halves, so they are divided into halves upon them. For Imam (Abu Hanifa), death occurred by one cause, which is killing, so the destruction is attributed to the single cause, then divided among its owners according to ownership. If it is said: The one from something of the wall — so how can a demand be made against him? We reply: If he is not able to demolish his share, he is able to rectify it by taking the matter to the judge, and thereby the objective is achieved, which is removal of harm. And in al-Muhit: He is able to demolish his share by the judge's ruling and by demanding the remaining owners to remove the debris, so he is able to demolish in this way. And he (Imam) did not mention a difference between the two issues: he guarantees one-fifth of the blood-money in the wall case, and two-thirds of the blood-money in the case of digging and building in the house. The difference between them is that every stone he placed or pit he dug, he is a transgressor in two-thirds of the placement and digging, and not a transgressor in one-third; therefore he guarantees two-thirds. The partners are not able to demolish.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage contains three distinct legal units tied together by the theme of liability for harmful constructions. First, the matn 'If it leans into a man's house, the demand is upon its owner' establishes that only the property owner whose airspace is invaded can demand rectification. Tenants or lessees cannot demand, but they can notify the owner. If the owner grants time or formally acquits the wall-owner of liability, that is valid and removes future liability—unlike a public road where only the judge has that authority. The scope is broad: any leaning into an adjoining space, whether upper into lower or vice versa, is covered. Second, the matn on the shared wall (repeated from earlier) is now used as the basis for an intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that when a wall owned by five partners falls, all five jointly pay the full blood-money (each one-fifth), and when a house shared by three partners has one partner dig a well or build a wall that kills, that partner pays two-thirds. The Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that in both cases the liable party pays only half. Their reasoning: the destruction caused by the share of the partner who was warned (or who did the act) is considered, while the share of the other partners is null—the death is attributed to the transgressor's portion only. Imam's reasoning: the death is a single cause (the collapse), so the loss is attributed entirely to that cause, then divided among all owners proportionally. A question is raised: if one partner cannot physically demolish his share without the others, how can he be held liable for not demolishing? The answer: he can seek a judge's order to compel the others or to demolish the entire wall. Finally, the author explains the difference between the two cases: in the wall case, the partner is a transgressor in the entire wall (since it is a single structure), so he shares liability equally with the others; in the digging/building case, the partner is a transgressor only in his own action, so he is liable for two-thirds (since his share is one-third but he acted without consent for the other two-thirds).

Key Terms
خصومة (khuṣūma): The dispute or legal claim; the right to file a complaint against the owner of the leaning structure.
أبرأه (abra'ahu): He acquitted him, i.e., released him from liability for future harm from the leaning structure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wall leans into a private house. The owner grants the wall owner one year to fix it. During that year the wall falls and damages the house. Who is liable?
A) The wall owner is liable because the fall was not due to the deferment.
B) No one is liable because the owner granted valid time.
C) The judge must decide liability.
D) The wall owner is liable only if negligent.

Key Takeaway
Only the property owner can demand removal or waive liability for a leaning wall; private rights are distinct from public rights.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Liability for Shared Walls: Single Cause vs. Proportional Shares
Intra-Hanafi dispute with talil reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Two Sahibayn in apportioning liability when a shared wall falls or a partner digs a well, and state the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa treats the collapse as a single cause (ʿilla wāḥida) leading to joint liability by shares; the Sahibayn treat only the warned/transgressing partner's share as actionable.

When a wall owned by several partners falls and kills someone, or a partner in a shared house digs a well that causes death, how is the blood-money divided? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ: the Imam holds all partners liable in proportion to ownership, while the Two hold only the warned partner is liable for half. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the two positions
Two cases: (a) a wall shared among five falls after a warning to one partner; (b) a house shared among three, one partner digs a well or builds a wall that kills. Imam says: five pay 1/5 each in (a), partner pays 2/3 in (b). Sahibayn say: in both, the liable party pays 1/2.
The scenario involves a warning (ishhād) to one partner in the wall case.
2
Analyze Imam's dalil: single cause
Imam argues that death occurred by a single cause (al-mawt ḥaṣala bi-ʿilla wāḥida), so the loss is attributed to that cause and then divided among owners by ownership shares. In the wall, all five are liable; in the digging case, the digger is liable for 2/3 because he transgressed in 2/3 of his action (his share minus partners' shares).
The 'single cause' principle is key: the collapse is one event, not parceled out.
3
Analyze Sahibayn's dalil: proportional transgression
The Two hold that the destruction caused by the share of the warned/acting partner is considered, while the other shares are irrelevant. In the wall, only the warned partner's share counts, so he pays half. In the digging, the digger's act is half transgression (his share) and half not (partners' shares), so he pays half.
They consider the act divisible by ownership shares, not as a single cause.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Imam, in the wall case each of five pays 1/5; under Sahibayn only the warned partner pays 1/2. In the digging case, Imam's 2/3 vs Sahibayn's 1/2.
The difference is significant for actual distribution of diya.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text presents Imam's position first and elaborates its reasoning, indicating it is the mufta bihi. The Sahibayn's view is mentioned but not adopted as the relied-upon position.
Later Hanafi works generally follow Imam on this issue.

علة واحدة (ʿilla wāḥida): A single cause; in legal reasoning, the collapse is treated as one cause, not split by shares.
أشهد عليه (ushhida ʿalayhi): Witnesses were called against him; a formal warning that the structure is dangerous, establishing negligence.

Liability for a Collapsing Wall or Well: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Diya
States ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on diya for collapsing wall and well

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diya for a warned partner in a shared wall or well, and explain the difference between the views of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Liability is proportional to ownership share, not to the number of warned partners.

This lesson examines a specific case from the Hanafi law of torts (diyat): when a shared structure like a wall or well causes death after one co-owner is warned. The passage presents the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and the opposing view of his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding the reasoning behind each position deepens your appreciation of Hanafi methodology in property and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases: a wall shared by five partners where one is warned, and a house among three where one digs a well or builds a wall resulting in death. Note the ownership shares: 1/5 for the wall, 1/3 for the well.
Warning ('ashhada) is a prerequisite for liability in the wall case but not explicitly for the well case.
2
State the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa
Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the warned partner pays 1/5 of the diya for the wall and 2/3 of the diya for the well. These fractions are proportional to his ownership share (1/5 and 1/3 respectively) with an adjustment for transgression in the well case.
The matn says 'wa hadha 'inda al-imam' indicating this is his view.
3
State the ruling of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani rule that the warned partner pays 1/2 of the diya in both cases. They argue that only the warned partner's share is considered transgressive; the other partners' shares are disregarded.
Their reasoning: the transgression and non-transgression become two halves, so liability is half.
4
Compare the rationales
Imam relies on the principle that death is caused by a single event, so liability is divided by ownership. The Sahibayn focus on the transgression inherent in the warned partner's action, which they see as half the total.
Imam's view is preferred (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (حائط بين خمسة أشهد على أحدهم فسقط على رجل ضمن خمسة الدية دار بين ثلاثة حفر أحدهم فيها بئراً أو بنى حائطاً فعطب به رجل ضمن ثلثي الدية) وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : يضمن النصف في الصورتين لأن التلف بنصيب من أشهد عليه يعتبر وبنصيب من لم يشهد عليه هدر، وفي الحفر باعتبار ملكه غير متعد باعتبار ملك شريكه متعد وكانا قسمين فانقسما نصفين عليهما. وللإمام أن الموت حصل بعلة واحدة وهي القتل فيضاف التلف إلى العلة الواحدة ثم يقسم على أربابها بقدر الملك. فإن قيل : الواحد من شيئاً من الحائط فكيف يصح تقدمه إليه؟ قلنا: إن لم يتمكن من هدم نصيبه يتمكن من اصلاحه بالمرافعة إلى الحاكم وبه يحصل الغرض وهو إزالة الضرر. وفي المحيط قال : يقدر على هدم نصيبه بحكم الحاكم ومطالبة الباقين بالنقض فيكون قادراً على النقض بهذا الطريق. ولم يذكر الفرق للإمام بين المسألتين حيث يضمن خمس الدية وفي الحائط، ويضمن ثلث الدية فيما إذا حفر وبنى في دار والفرق بينهما أن كل حجر وضعه أو حفره فهو متعدٍ في ثلثي الوضع والحفر وليس متعدياً في الثلث فلهذا يضمن الثلثين.
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (A wall between five, when one of them is warned and it falls on a man, he pays one-fifth of the diya. A house between three, one of them digs a well or builds a wall and a man is killed by it, he pays two-thirds of the diya.) This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He pays half in both cases because the destruction is considered according to the share of the one warned, and the share of those not warned is disregarded. Regarding digging, considering his ownership he is not transgressing, but considering his partner's ownership he is transgressing, so there are two halves, so they are divided into two halves. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, death occurred due to a single cause, which is killing, so the destruction is attributed to the single cause and then divided among the owners according to ownership. If it is said: He cannot remove his share from the wall, how can he be warned? We say: If he cannot demolish his share, he can rectify it by referring to the judge, and thereby the purpose of removing harm is achieved. In al-Muhit, it says: He can demolish his share by the judge's decree and by demanding the others to remove the rubble, so he is able to remove the rubble in this way. The difference between the two cases for Imam Abu Hanifa is not mentioned, where he pays one-fifth of the diya for the wall, and two-thirds of the diya for digging and building in a house. The difference is that every stone he placed or dug is transgression in two-thirds of the placement and digging, and not transgression in one-third, so he pays two-thirds.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability when a shared structure causes death. The matn states that if a wall shared by five partners falls after warning one partner, that partner pays 1/5 of the diya. Similarly, if one of three partners digs a well or builds a wall in a shared house and it kills someone, that partner pays 2/3 of the diya. Imam Abu Hanifa's reasoning: death is caused by a single act (the fall or the well), so the liability is divided proportionally to ownership. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that only the share of the warned partner is considered; the other partners' shares are disregarded, leading to half liability in both cases. They base this on the idea that the warned partner's action is transgressive only with respect to his own share. The commentary then addresses an objection: how can a single partner be effectively warned if he cannot demolish his share alone? The answer is that he can seek a judge's order to compel the other partners to participate in removal. Finally, the text explains the distinction: in the wall case, each stone placement is transgressive in 2/3 of placement (since he owns 1/5, but the transgression is considered differently), leading to 1/5 diya; in the well case, the transgression is in 2/3 of the digging, so he pays 2/3.

Key Terms
أشهد على أحدهم (ashhada 'ala ahadihim): The partner was warned (by the judge or authorities) to demolish or repair the leaning wall.
الدية (al-diyah): The blood money paid to the heirs of the deceased, fully or in shares.
متعدٍ (muta'addin): Transgressive, legally blameworthy in causing harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a wall shared by five partners falls on a man after only one partner is warned, what fraction of the diya does the warned partner pay?
A) One-fifth
B) One-half
C) Two-thirds
D) Full diya

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, liability for a shared structure causing death is proportional to the warned partner's ownership share, with nuances depending on the nature of the structure and transgression.

Owner's Options for Fallen Wall & Wall Leaning into Private House
Rulings on owner's options after wall collapse and on leaning wall into private house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the owner's two options after a wall collapse, explain why he cannot force rebuilding, and distinguish between private and public rights in demanding removal of a leaning wall.

Core Idea
Private property rights grant flexibility in compensation and waiver, unlike public rights requiring judicial authority.

When a wall collapses or leans into a private house, Islamic law gives the affected owner specific rights. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains his options for compensation and who may demand removal of a leaning wall. Understanding these distinctions is essential for property law and liability.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two options after collapse
The owner of the collapsed wall can either claim the full value of the wall (with the rubble going to the payer) or take the rubble and claim compensation for the deficiency. Note that he cannot force the liable party to rebuild.
2
Understand the valuation method
According to al-Halwani, the valuation considers the house with its surrounding walls. This is relevant when determining the deficiency.
3
Identify the private right in a leaning wall
If a wall leans into a private house, the right to demand removal belongs exclusively to the house owner (or the tenant). They can give the wall owner time or discharge him from liability.
4
Contrast with public road
If the wall leans into a public road, only the judge or designated authority can grant time or discharge, because the right belongs to the public.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
The same logic applies if an upper floor leans into a lower floor or vice versa – the right remains private.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكون ربها بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه قيمة حائطه والنقض له وإن شاء أخذ النقض وضمنه النقصان، ولو أراد أن يجبره على البناء كما كان ليس له ذلك. وفي الكافي : وما تلف بوقوع الأول والثاني فعلى مالك الأول، ولم يذكر محمد رحمه الله قيمة الحائط؛ حكي عن الشيخ الإمام شمس الأئمة الحلواني قال : تقوم الدار وحيطانها محيطة بها، وكذلك قال في المنتقى. قال رحمه الله : (وإن مال إلى دار رجل فالطلب إلى ربها لأن الحق له على الخصوص، وإذا كان يسكنها غيره كان له أن يطالبه لأن له المطالبة بإزالة ما شغل هواه قال رحمه الله : (فإن أجله أو أبرأه صح) بخلاف الطريق إن أجله صاحب الدار أو أبرأه جاز تأجيله وإبراؤه حتى لو سقط في الإبراء وقبل مضي المدة في التأجيل لا يضمن لأن الحق له على ما ذكرناه بخلاف ما إذا مال للطريق العام فأجله القاضي أو من أشهد عليه أو أبرأه لا يصح التأجيل والإبراء لما ذكرنا وقوله «إلى دار رجل» مثال وليس بقيد حتى لو مال العلو إلى الأسفل أو الأسفل إلى العلو فالحكم كذلك؛ كذا في قاضيخان
And the owner has the choice: if he wishes, he may claim the value of his wall and the rubble belongs to him; if he wishes, he may take the rubble and claim the deficiency. And if he wants to force him to rebuild as it was, he does not have that right. In al-Kafi: Whatever is lost by the fall of the first and second is upon the owner of the first. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) did not mention the value of the wall. It is narrated from al-Shaykh al-Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani that he said: The house and its surrounding walls are valued. And likewise it is said in al-Muntaqa. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And if it leans towards a man's house, the claim is to its owner because the right is specifically his. And if someone else is living there, he may claim from him because he has the right to demand removal of what occupies his air. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (If he gives him time or discharges him, it is valid.) Unlike the road: if the house owner gives time or discharges, the postponement and discharge are valid, even if it falls during the discharge or before the expiry of the time in postponement, he is not liable because the right is his as we mentioned. Unlike if it leans towards the public road: if the judge gives time, or anyone who is warned, or discharges, the postponement and discharge are not valid for the reason we mentioned. His saying 'to a man's house' is an example and not a restriction, so even if the upper part leans towards the lower or the lower towards the upper, the ruling is the same. Thus in Qadikhan.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents two related rulings. First, when a leaning wall collapses, the owner whose wall was damaged has an option: he can either claim the full value of the wall (including the rubble) from the liable party, with the rubble then belonging to the payer, OR he can take the rubble himself and claim compensation for the decrease in value. He cannot compel the liable party to rebuild the wall. The text then discusses the valuation: according to al-Kafi, loss from the first and second fall is on the first owner; al-Halwani advises valuing the house with its surrounding walls. Second, the passage addresses a wall leaning into a private house, not a public road. The right to demand removal belongs specifically to the house owner (or the tenant). The owner can give the wall owner time to fix it or even discharge him from liability. This is effective because the right is his personal property. In contrast, if the wall leans into a public road, only the judge or the designated authority can give time or discharge, because the right belongs to the public. The ruling applies similarly when an upper floor leans onto a lower floor. This distinction is based on whether the right is private (individual ownership) or public.

Key Terms
ربها بالخيار (rabbuha bil-khiyar): The owner of the wall has the option to choose between two forms of compensation.
النقض (al-naqd): The rubble or materials of the collapsed wall.
أجله أو أبرأه (ajjalahu aw abra'ahu): Gave him time (postponement) or discharged him from liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wall leans into a private house and the owner gives the wall owner time to repair, then the wall falls after the time but before the deadline, is the wall owner liable?
A) Yes, because he is always liable for the collapse
B) No, because the discharge was valid
C) Yes, because the wall leaned into private property
D) No, because the judge must approve the postponement

Key Takeaway
Private property rights allow the owner to choose compensation after wall collapse and to waive liability for a leaning wall, whereas public rights require judicial authority.

Wall and Well Liability: Intra-Hanafi Differences and Key Principles
Expands on wall and well cases, quoting al-Jami' al-Saghir and detailing rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on liability for falling shared walls and wells, and apply the principle that placing objects on one's own property without transgression incurs no liability.

Core Idea
Liability for falling shared walls is proportional to ownership share, but the Sahibayn count only the warned share.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq compiles several scenarios on liability when a shared wall or well causes death. It reveals a clear intra-Hanafi difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the extent of liability. Additionally, a key principle is affirmed: there is no liability for objects placed on one's own property unless transgression occurs. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the rules of diyat in shared structures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Main Disputed Cases
Locate the case of a wall shared by five (each owning a fifth) where one partner is warned, and the case of a well dug in a shared house by one of three partners. These are the core examples where Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ.
The passage cites al-Jami' al-Saghir as the source of the disagreement; al-Asl has no recorded difference.
2
Contrast Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on Liability Amount
State Abu Hanifa's position: the warned partner pays a share proportional to his ownership (1/5 for the wall, 1/3 for the well). Then state the Sahibayn's position: the warned partner pays half (1/2) in both cases, because only the warned share counts.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the amount of diya: 1/5 or 1/3 vs. 1/2.
3
Apply the Principle of Non-Liability for Acts on Own Property
From al-Sighnaqi: if a man places an object on his own wall (even if leaning) and it falls and kills, there is no liability because he is not transgressing on his own property. This is a key principle that limits liability to cases of transgression.
This principle overrides assumptions that any falling object from one's property creates liability.
4
Derive the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The difference in the amount of diya affects how much the 'aqila of the warned partner must pay. Under Abu Hanifa, it is proportional to ownership; under Sahibayn, it is half regardless of how small the warned share. This matters in multi-party ownership.
The passage also mentions that the disagreements are specific to al-Jami' al-Saghir, implying later works may have settled on one view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله «حائط بين خمسة ودار بين ثلاثة مثال وليس بقيد وفي الظهيرية : والحائط إذا كان مشتركاً بين اثنين فأشهد على أحدهما فهو بمنزلة ما لو أشهد على أحد الورثة. وفي المنتقى : رجل مات وترك داراً وعليه من الدين ما يستغرق قيمتها وفيها حائط مائل إلى الطريق ولا وارث للميت غير هذا الابن فالتقدم في حائطه إليه، وإن كان لا يملكها فإن وقع التقدم بعد التقدم إليه كانت الدية على عاقلة الأب دون عاقلة الابن، فإن كان الحائط المائل بين خمسة نفر أخماساً وتقدم إلى أحدهم بالنقض ثم سقط على إنسان فإنه يضمن المتقدم إليه خمس الدية ويجب على عاقلته ويهدر أربعة أخماس وهو حصة شركائه وهو قول أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد بأن الشريك الحاضر المتقدم إليه يضمن نصف الدية فتجب ذلك على عاقلته ويهدر النصف، ذكر المسألة في الجامع الصغير على هذا الوجه، وذكر هذه المسألة في الأصل ولم يذكر فيها خلافاً. قال في الجامع الصغير أيضاً : إذا كانت الدار بين ثلاثة نفر حفر أحدهم في هذه الدار المشتركة بئراً ووقع فيها إنسان ومات قال : على عاقلة الحافر عند أبي حنيفة ثلث دية المقتول، وعلى قول أبي يوسف ومحمد يجب على الحافر نصف الدية وهذه المسألة مذكورة في الأصل من غير خلاف، والخلاف في هاتين المسألتين من خصائص الجامع الصغير . وفي السغناقي : وإذا وضع الرجل على حائطه شيئاً فوقع ذلك الشيء فأصاب إنساناً فلا ضمان عليه فيه لأنه وضعه على ملكه وهو لا يكون متعدياً فيما يحدثه في ملكه. سواء كان الحائط مائلاً أو غير مائل. وفي المنتقى : ولو أن رجلاً بنى حائطاً مائلاً بين رجلين اثلاثاً تقدم إلى صاحب الثلث فيه ثم سقط على رجل وقتله صرعاً فعليه ثلث الدية بلا خلاف وهو بمنزلة حمار حمل عليه إنسان عشرة أقفزة وحمل الآخر عليه خمسة أقفزه وكل ذلك بغير إذن المولى فمات الحمار من ذلك تجب القيمة اثلاثاً وهو بمنزلة رجل أخذ بنفس إنسان وأخذ آخر بنفسه الآخر فمات المأخوذ من ذلك وهناك يجب الضمان كذا هنا هذا إذا وقع الحائط على حر، ولو وقع الحائط على عبد إن قتله غماً فإن قيمته عليها اثلاثاً وإن جرحته الحائط ومات العبد من الجراحة فالجراحة عليهما أثلاثاً والنفس عليهما نصفين، فإن جرحه الحائط ثم مات من الغم والجراحة فإن الجراحة عليهما اثلاثاً نصف ما بقي من النفس وهو حصة الغم بينهما أثلاثاً أيضاً، والنصف الآخر وهو حصة الجراحة بينهما نصفين. عن أبي حنيفة في حائط مائل لرجلين أشهد عليهما وحائط مائل لرجل أشهد عليه سقطا على إنسان فقتلاه فنصف الدية على الرجل الذي له الحائط ونصف الدية على رجلين. وروى الحسن بن زيادة فسقطا على الرجلين فماتا فالدية عليهما مطلقاً. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : إن مات من جرح حرحه الحائط فالدية عليهم أثلاثاً، وإن مات من ثقلهما فالدية عليهما نصفين ولا يضمن إذا لم يكن متعدياً، فأما إذا وضع في ملكه عرضاً حتى خرج طرفه منه إلى الطريق إن سقط فأصاب الطرف الخارج منه
And his saying 'a wall between five and a house between three' is an example and not a restriction. In al-Zahiriyyah: A wall if it is shared between two and one is warned, it is like warning one of the heirs. In al-Muntaqa: A man died leaving a house and debts covering its value, and in it a wall leaning to the road, and the deceased has no heir except this son, then the warning regarding the wall is to him, even if he does not own the house. If the warning is given to him and then it falls on a man, the diya is on the 'aqilah of the father, not the son. If the leaning wall is between five fifths and one partner is warned to remove the rubble, then it falls on a man, the warned one pays one-fifth of the diya on his 'aqilah, and four-fifths are waived, which is the share of his partners. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: The present partner who is warned pays half the diya, so it is obligatory on his 'aqilah, and half is waived. This issue is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir in this manner, and it is mentioned in al-Asl without disagreement. He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir also: If the house is between three people, one of them digs a well in this shared house, and a person falls into it and dies, he said: According to Abu Hanifa, the diya is one-third on the 'aqilah of the digger. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, half of the diya is obligatory on the digger. This issue is mentioned in al-Asl without disagreement, and the disagreement in these two issues is specific to al-Jami' al-Saghir. In al-Sighnaqi: If a man places something on his wall and that thing falls and hits a person, there is no liability on him because he placed it on his property and he is not transgressing in what he does on his property, whether the wall is leaning or not. In al-Muntaqa: If a man built a leaning wall between two men in thirds, and he warned the owner of the third, then it falls on a man and kills him, he must pay one-third of the diya without disagreement. It is like a donkey on which one man loaded ten qafiz and another loaded five qafiz without the owner's permission, and the donkey died from that, the value is obligated in thirds. It is like a man grabbing one person and another grabbing another person, and the grabbed one dies from that, liability is obligated. Similarly here. This is if the wall falls on a free man. If the wall falls on a slave, if it kills him by pressure, his value is upon them in thirds. If the wall wounds him and he dies from the wound, the wound amount is on them in thirds and the blood money for the person is half. If the wall wounds him and then he dies from pressure and the wound, then the wound amount is on them in thirds, half of what remains of the blood money is the share of pressure between them in thirds also, and the other half is the share of the wound between them half. From Abu Hanifa regarding a leaning wall belonging to two men, both warned, and a leaning wall of a man warned, they both fall on a man and kill him: half of the diya is on the man who owns the wall, and half of the diya is on the two men. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated: if they fall on two men and both die, the diya is upon them absolutely. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: If he dies from a wound inflicted by the wall, the diya is upon them in thirds. If he dies from their weight, the diya is upon them half. There is no liability if he is not transgressing. As for if he places on his property a piece of wood such that its end comes out into the road, and it falls and hits the external end.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage compiles multiple scenarios related to liability for falling walls and wells, with intra-Hanafi differences. It begins by noting that the previous examples (wall between five, house between three) are not exclusive. It then addresses: (1) A wall shared by two; warning one is like warning an heir. (2) A deceased debtor's house with a leaning wall; the sole heir is warned even before inheriting; if it falls, diya is on the father's 'aqilah, not the son's. (3) A wall between five fifths: Abu Hanifa says the warned partner pays 1/5; Sahibayn say 1/2 (citing al-Jami' al-Saghir which records the disagreement, while al-Asl has no dispute). (4) A well dug by one of three partners: Abu Hanifa says 1/3; Sahibayn say 1/2. (5) If someone places an object on his own wall and it falls, he is not liable because he is on his own property, unless it leans into the road (end protruding). (6) A wall owned in thirds: only the 1/3 owner is warned; he pays 1/3 diya without dispute, analogized to overloading a donkey or two persons grabbing which cause death. (7) Detailed calculations for a slave: if killed by pressure, value in thirds (since wall is in thirds); if wounded then dies, wound compensation in thirds and blood money half; if combination, complex division. (8) Two separate walls (one owned by one man, one jointly by two) both fall and kill: half diya on the single owner, half on the two jointly. (9) If two walls fall on two victims, diya on both sets. (10) Sahibayn differentiate: if death from wound, diya in thirds; if from weight, half. No liability if no transgression. The passage ends with a rule about placing a beam that protrudes into the road: if it falls and hits the external part, liability may apply.

Key Terms
ثقل (thiqal): Weight or pressure of the wall causing death.
عاقلة ('aqila): The group responsible for paying diya (typically male relatives).
جرح (jarh): Wound caused by the wall, distinguishing cause of death.
إشهاد (ishhad): Warning given to a partner to remove a dangerous structure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man places a heavy object on his own wall (which is not leaning) and it falls and kills someone, is he liable?
A) Yes, because he caused the death
B) No, because he placed it on his property and is not transgressing
C) Yes, if the wall was leaning
D) No, only if the object was placed without permission

Key Takeaway
For shared structures, the liable party pays diya proportional to ownership share according to Abu Hanifa, and placing objects on one's own property incurs no liability unless there is transgression.

Liability for a Collapsing Shared Wall: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on proportional diyah liability after notice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on proportional liability for harm caused by shared property and apply the differing positions to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Warning a co-owner about a danger does not shift full liability; only the warned owner's share is at stake according to Abu Hanifa.

When shared property causes harm, who pays the blood money? This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute recorded in Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir, where Abu Hanifa and his two students differ on the liability of a co-owner who was warned about a dangerous wall but failed to act. The disagreement hinges on the extent of responsibility after notice, with practical implications for the 'aqila's payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Note the two cases: a leaning wall shared among five owners, and a well dug in a shared house by one of three owners. In each, a warning or action by one owner leads to a death.
The warning (tugaddim) is a formal call to demolish or secure the danger.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and principle
Abu Hanifa holds that the warned owner bears only his proportional share of the blood money (e.g., 1/5 for the wall or 1/3 for the well). The rest is waived because the other owners were not warned or did not act.
This reflects a principle of no liability without notice or fault beyond one's ownership share.
3
State Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the warned owner guarantees half the blood money. They likely consider the owner's presence and ability to prevent harm as creating greater responsibility.
The Sahibayn's view is more strict: the warned owner's omission triggers liability for a larger portion.
4
Note the source discrepancy
The text notes that in al-Asl (the core book), Imam Muhammad reported the issue without mention of any difference. The dispute appears only in al-Jami' al-Saghir. This suggests the disagreement may be a later refinement in transmission.
This source hierarchy affects which view is considered stronger in the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, the warned owner's 'aqila pays only 1/5 (or 1/3); under Sahibayn, they pay half. This significantly affects the financial burden on the 'aqila.
Later Hanafi fatwa often prefers Abu Hanifa's view to avoid excessive liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الحائط المائل بين خمسة نفر أخماساً وتقدم إلى أحدهم بالنقض ثم سقط على إنسان فإنه يضمن المتقدم إليه خمس الدية ويجب على عاقلته ويهدر أربعة أخماس وهو حصة شركائه وهو قول أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد بأن الشريك الحاضر المتقدم إليه يضمن نصف الدية فتجب ذلك على عاقلته ويهدر النصف، ذكر المسألة في الجامع الصغير على هذا الوجه، وذكر هذه المسألة في الأصل ولم يذكر فيها خلافاً. قال في الجامع الصغير أيضاً : إذا كانت الدار بين ثلاثة نفر حفر أحدهم في هذه الدار المشتركة بئراً ووقع فيها إنسان ومات قال : على عاقلة الحافر عند أبي حنيفة ثلث دية المقتول، وعلى قول أبي يوسف ومحمد يجب على الحافر نصف الدية وهذه المسألة مذكورة في الأصل من غير خلاف، والخلاف في هاتين المسألتين من خصائص الجامع الصغير.
If a leaning wall is shared in fifths between five persons and one of them is called upon to demolish it, then it falls on a person, the one called upon guarantees one-fifth of the blood money, and it is upon his 'aqila, and four-fifths are waived, that being the share of his partners. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say: the present partner who was called upon guarantees half the blood money, so that is obligatory upon his 'aqila, and half is waived. He mentioned the issue in al-Jami' al-Saghir in this manner, and he mentioned this issue in al-Asl and did not mention any difference in it. He also said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If a house was between three persons, and one of them dug a well in this shared house, and a person fell into it and died, he said: Upon the 'aqila of the digger, according to Abu Hanifa, one-third of the blood money of the killed. And according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, half the blood money is obligatory upon the digger. This issue is mentioned in al-Asl without difference, and the difference in these two issues is specific to al-Jami' al-Saghir.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents two intra-Hanafi disputes regarding liability when a shared property causes harm. In the first case, a shared leaning wall between five owners is at risk of collapsing. One owner is warned (tugaddim) to demolish it but does not, and the wall falls on a person. Abu Hanifa holds that the warned owner guarantees only his proportional share (one-fifth of the blood money), because the other owners were not warned. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the warned owner guarantees half the blood money, because he is present and should have taken action, and thus his liability is greater. The second case is similar: a well dug in a shared house by one of three owners. Abu Hanifa says the digger guarantees one-third (his share), while the Sahibayn say half. The text notes that these issues are recorded in the Jami' al-Saghir of Imam Muhammad, but in al-Asl (the original book) no difference is mentioned. This suggests that the difference may be a later development in Imam Muhammad's transmission. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) would typically be the one in al-Asl, but later Hanafi jurists may prefer one view.

Key Terms
تقدم إليه بالنقض (tugaddima ilayhi bi-l-naqd): The act of warning or calling upon a co-owner to demolish a leaning wall to prevent harm; in this context, it triggers liability if the wall collapses.
عاقلة (al-aqila): The group of paternal male relatives (asaba) who are responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of a tribesman who kills accidentally.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money; compensation paid to the victim's heirs in cases of homicide or bodily harm where retaliation (qisas) is not applicable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a shared leaning wall between five owners, what is the liability according to Abu Hanifa for the owner who was warned to demolish it but it collapsed and killed someone?
A) Full diyah
B) Half diyah
C) One-fifth diyah
D) No liability

Key Takeaway
In shared property, liability for accidental death after notice is proportional to ownership or half, depending on whether one follows Abu Hanifa or Sahibayn.
