Liability for Objects Placed on a Wall That Fall and Cause Harm
Detailed Hanafi ruling on liability for falling objects from a wall.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a person is liable for harm caused by an object falling from his wall, and apply the Hanafi distinction between excessive and non-excessive lean.

Core Idea
No liability unless transgression—excessive lean or protrusion into public space.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq details when a person must compensate for harm caused by an object falling from his wall. The default rule is no liability because the wall is on his own property. But if he transgresses, such as by building an excessively leaning wall or placing a beam that protrudes into the road, he becomes liable. The passage also covers shared ownership scenarios and the division of blood money.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule and its basis
The text states that placing something on one's own wall does not incur liability, even if the wall is leaning. Understand the basis: acting on one's own property is not transgression.
2
Recognize the conditions for liability
Liability arises when the wall is excessively leaning (mayl fahish) or when an object protrudes into public space. These constitute transgression.
Excessive lean is defined by custom (urf); what builders guard against is fahish.
3
Understand proportionate liability in shared walls
For a wall owned in thirds, each owner is liable for his share. The text analogizes to loading a donkey without permission.
4
Distinguish between death by compression and wound
The division of blood money for a slave differs: for compression, liability in thirds; for wound, halves. This applies to the joint owner scenario.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a wall with slight lean and a beam placed lengthwise, no liability. If the beam protrudes, liability arises.
Also note the intra-Hanafi disagreement on absolute no liability for excessive lean.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السغناقي : وإذا وضع الرجل على حائطه شيئاً فوقع ذلك الشيء فأصاب إنساناً فلا ضمان عليه فيه لأنه وضعه على ملكه وهو لا يكون متعدياً فيما يحدثه في ملكه. سواء كان الحائط مائلاً أو غير مائل. وفي المنتقى : ولو أن رجلاً بنى حائطاً مائلاً بين رجلين اثلاثاً تقدم إلى صاحب الثلث فيه ثم سقط على رجل وقتله صرعاً فعليه ثلث الدية بلا خلاف وهو بمنزلة حمار حمل عليه إنسان عشرة أقفزة وحمل الآخر عليه خمسة أقفزه وكل ذلك بغير إذن المولى فمات الحمار من ذلك تجب القيمة اثلاثاً وهو بمنزلة رجل أخذ بنفس إنسان وأخذ آخر بنفسه الآخر فمات المأخوذ من ذلك وهناك يجب الضمان كذا هنا هذا إذا وقع الحائط على حر، ولو وقع الحائط على عبد إن قتله غماً فإن قيمته عليها أثلاثاً وإن جرحته الحائط ومات العبد من الجراحة فالجراحة عليهما أثلاثاً والنفس عليهما نصفين، فإن جرحه الحائط ثم مات من الغم والجراحة فإن الجراحة عليهما أثلاثاً نصف ما بقي من النفس وهو حصة الغم بينهما أثلاثاً أيضاً، والنصف الآخر وهو حصة الجراحة بينهما نصفين. عن أبي حنيفة في حائط مائل لرجلين أشهد عليهما وحائط مائل لرجل أشهد عليه سقطا على إنسان فقتلاه فنصف الدية على الرجل الذي له الحائط ونصف الدية على رجلين. وروى الحسن بن زيادة فسقطا على الرجلين فماتا فالدية عليهما مطلقاً. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : إن مات من جرح حرحه الحائط فالدية عليهم أثلاثاً، وإن مات من ثقلهما فالدية عليهما نصفين ولا يضمن إذا لم يكن متعدياً، فأما إذا وضع في ملكه عرضاً حتى خرج طرفه منه إلى الطريق إن سقط فأصاب الطرف الخارج منه حتى لو كان الحائط ماثلاً وكان ممكن وضع الجذع عليه طولاً لم يخرج شيء منه إلى الطريق ثم سقط ذلك الجذع على إنسان ومات فإنه لا يضمن هكذا ذكر في الكتاب وأطلق الجواب اطلاقاً. وفي مشايخنا من قال هذا إذا كان الحائط مائلاً إلى الطريق ميلاً يسيراً غير فاحش فأما إذا مال ميلاً فاحشاً فإنه يضمن وذلك لأن الميلان إذا كان غير فاحش بحيث يوجب ذلك القدر وقت البناء يكون وجوده وعدمه بمنزلة لأن الجدار قلما يخلو عن قليل ميلان يكون له إلى الطريق فإما إذا كانا ميلاً فاحشاً بحيث يحترز منه عند البناء في الأصل فإنه يضمن إذا سقط ذلك على إنسان إن لم يتقدم إليه بالرفع لأنه متى وضع الجذع طولاً على الحائط المائل فيعتبر بما لو شغل الهواء بغر واسطة، ولو شغل هواء الطريق بواسطة بأن أخرج الجذع عن الحائط فسقط فأصاب إنساناً كذا هذا. ومنهم من قال الجواب فيه كما أطلقه محمد لا يضمن في الحالين، ولو كان الوضع بعد ما تقدم إليه في الحائط ثم سقط الجذع فأصاب إنساناً يقول بأنه يضمن؛ كذا المنتقى والله تعالى أعلم.
In al-Sighnaqi: If a man places something on his wall and that thing falls and hits a person, there is no liability upon him for it because he placed it on his property and he is not transgressing in what he does on his property, whether the wall is leaning or not. And in al-Muntaqa: If a man built a leaning wall between two men as thirds, and the owner of one-third was called upon regarding it, then it fell on a man and killed him instantly, he owes one-third of the blood money without disagreement. It is like a donkey on which one man loaded ten qafiz and another loaded five qafiz without the owner's permission and the donkey died from that; the value is due in thirds. And it is like a man who seized a person and another seized his other side and the seized one died; liability is due there, so here too. This is if the wall fell on a free person. If the wall fell on a slave and killed him by compression, its value is upon them in thirds; if the wall wounded him and the slave died from the wound, the wound is upon them in thirds and the life is upon them in halves; if the wall wounded him then he died from compression and wound, then the wound is upon them in thirds, half of the remainder of the life is the share of compression between them also in thirds, and the other half is the share of the wound between them in halves. From Abu Hanifa: about a leaning wall belonging to two men who were warned, and a leaning wall belonging to one man who was warned, they both fell on a man and killed him: half the blood money is on the man who owns the wall and half on the two men. And al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated: they fell on two men and they both died, the blood money is upon them absolutely. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: If the wall wounded him and he died from the wound, the blood money is upon them in thirds; if he died from their weight, the blood money is upon them in halves. And there is no liability if he is not transgressing. As for if he placed on his property a beam such that its end protrudes onto the road and it falls and hits the protruding end, even if the wall is leaning and it is possible to place the beam lengthwise without anything protruding onto the road, then that beam falls on a person and he dies, he is not liable. Thus he mentioned in the book and gave the answer absolutely. Among our shaykhs, some said: this is if the wall leans toward the road slightly, not excessively. As for if it leans excessively, he is liable. This is because if the lean is not excessive such that it was present at the time of construction, its existence and non-existence are similar, since a wall is rarely free of a slight lean toward the road. But if the lean is excessive such that one would guard against it at the time of construction originally, he is liable if it falls on a person if he was not preceded by a warning to remove it. Because when he places a beam lengthwise on a leaning wall, it is considered like occupying the air without a barrier. And if he occupies the air of the road with a barrier by extending the beam from the wall and it then falls and hits a person, it is similar. Some said: the answer is as Muhammad gave it: no liability in both cases. And if the placement was after he was warned about the wall, then the beam falls and hits a person, he says he is liable. So in al-Muntaqa. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This lengthy passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq provides detailed Hanafi rulings on liability when something placed on a wall falls and causes harm. The general rule: if a person places an object on his own wall and it falls without his transgression (e.g., normal use), he is not liable, whether the wall is leaning or not. However, if the wall is excessively leaning (mil fahish), he is liable because such a lean indicates negligence. Also, if the object protrudes into the public road (e.g., a beam extending beyond the wall), he is liable because he is occupying public space without right. The passage also covers cases involving shared walls: if a leaning wall belongs to two owners and they are both warned, liability is divided. There is also a distinction between death by compression (ghamm) and by wound (jurh): for a slave, the value is divided differently depending on the cause. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ from Abu Hanifa in some details. The passage also mentions an analogy to loading a donkey and to two persons seizing a victim, to illustrate proportional liability. The relied-upon position is that the person is not liable unless he is transgressing (e.g., excessive lean or protrusion).

Key Terms
ميل فاحش (mayl fahish): Excessive leaning of a wall such that it was avoidable at construction; triggers liability if it falls and causes harm.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to compensate; in this context, payment of blood money or value of slave.
تقدم إليه (tugaddima ilayhi): Warning given to a person to remove a dangerous condition; warning triggers liability if harm occurs thereafter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man places a beam on his wall that does not protrude and the wall has only a slight lean (ghayr fahish), and the beam falls and kills a person, is the man liable?
A) Yes, because the wall is leaning.
B) No, because there is no transgression.
C) Only if he was warned before.
D) Only if the victim is a free person.

Key Takeaway
A person is not liable for objects falling from his wall unless he transgresses by excessive lean or protrusion into public space.

Rider's Liability for Animal-Caused Injuries: Front vs Hind Parts
Hanafi ruling on rider liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a rider's liability for injuries caused by his animal's front and hind parts, and apply the condition of safety on the road.

Core Idea
A rider is liable for front parts but not hind kicks — avoidability determines responsibility.

In this passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, we learn the precise rule for liability when a rider's animal injures someone. The key distinction is between actions of the animal's front and hind parts: front actions are controllable and thus the rider is liable; hind kicks are generally unpredictable and exempt unless the rider stops the animal. This ruling is based on the principle that passage on Muslim roads is permitted only when safety is maintained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a rider causing injury through his animal. The ruling: the rider is liable for compensation (diyah or daman) when the animal's front parts cause damage.
The ruling is for the rider (rakib), not the owner if not riding.
2
Distinguish front vs hind actions
Front actions include: stepping (ita'), biting (kadm), striking with foreleg (khabt), and collision (sadm). Hind actions: kicking with hind leg or tail (nafh). The rider is liable for all front actions, but not for hind kicks unless he stops the animal on the road.
The terms are defined in al-Ayni's commentary.
3
Apply the condition of safety
The underlying principle is that using a public road is permissible only with safety. Since front actions are avoidable, the rider must prevent them; hind kicks are not easily avoided except by not stopping the animal.
This principle is stated explicitly in the passage.
4
Note the exception on private property
If the rider is on his own property, he is only liable for ita' (stepping) because it is considered a direct action of the rider himself, similar to digging a well. Other injuries are not actionable.
This is a qualification mentioned at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها وغير ذلك
لما فرغ رحمه الله تعالى من بيان أحكام جناية الإنسان شرع في بيان جناية البهيمة، ولا شك في تقدم جناية الإنسان على البهيمة؛ كذا في النهاية ويرد عليه أنه لم يفرغ من بيان جناية مطلقاً بل بقي منها جناية المملوك، ولا شك أنه من الإنسان فيقدم على البهيمة وكان يقدم على جناية البهيمة؛ كذا في غاية البيان قال رحمه الله : (ضمن الراكب ما أوطأت دابته بيد ورجل أو رأس أو كدمت أو خبطت أو صدمت لا ما نفحت برجل أو ذنب إلا إذا أوقفها في الطريق والأصل في هذا الباب أن المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح بشرط السلامة لأنه تصرف في حقه وفي حق غيره من وجه لكونه مشتركاً بين كل الناس إذ الإباحة مقيدة بالسلامة، والاحتراز عن الإيطاء والكدم والصدم والخبط ممكن لأنه ليس من ضرورة السير، وقيدناه بشرط السلامة وفي العيني على الهداية : الكدم بمقدم الإنسان، والخبط باليد والصدم هو أن تطلب الشيء بجسدك ولا يمكن الاحتراز عن النفحة أيضاً لأنه يمكن الاحتراز عن الإيقاف وهو المراد بقوله إذا إذا أوقفها في الطريق أطلق فيما ذكره وهو مقيد بأن يكون في غير ملكه أما إذا كان في ملكه لا يضمن إلا في الإيطاء وهو راكبها لأنه فعل منه مباشرة حتى يحرم به عن الميراث وتجب عليه الكفارة بشرط التعدي فصار كحفر البئر،
Chapter on injury caused by animals and injury to them, and other matters. When the author (may Allah have mercy on him) finished explaining the rulings of injury caused by humans, he began explaining injury caused by animals. There is no doubt about the precedence of human injury over animal injury; so in al-Nihaya. It is objected that he did not finish explaining injury absolutely, but there remains the injury of a slave, and there is no doubt that it is from humans so it precedes animal injury, and he should have preceded it over animal injury; so in Ghayat al-Bayan. He said: The rider is liable for what his animal steps on with its foreleg, hind leg, or head, or what it bites, strikes with its hand, or collides with, but not for what it kicks with its hind leg or tail, unless he stops it in the road. The principle in this chapter is that passing on the road of the Muslims is permissible on condition of safety, because it is a disposition concerning his right and the right of others in one respect, since it is shared among all people. Indeed, permissibility is conditioned by safety. Avoiding stepping, biting, colliding, and striking is possible because it is not a necessity of travel. We conditioned it on safety. In al-Ayni on al-Hidaya: Al-Kadm is with the front part of the animal, al-khabt is with the hand, al-sadm is when it seeks something with its body. It is not possible to guard against the nahla (hind kick) except by avoiding stopping the animal, which is what is meant by his saying 'if he stops it in the road'. He gave an absolute statement, and it is restricted to when it is not on his property. If it is on his property, he is not liable except for stepping (ita'), because he is riding it, as it is a direct action of his, so much so that it precludes him from inheritance and requires kaffarah with the condition of transgression; thus it becomes like digging a well.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on animal injuries in Hanafi fiqh. The author notes the order of topics: human injuries first, then animal. There is a brief scholarly note that the injury caused by a slave should have been placed before animal injuries since it is also human. The core ruling: the rider (rakib) is liable for damage caused by his animal's front parts—its forelegs, hind legs (when walking/stepping), head, biting, striking with forelegs, and colliding—because these are actions he can control. However, he is not liable for kicks from the hind legs or tail (nafh), as these are typically less predictable, unless he stops the animal in the road, making the kick foreseeable. The legal principle (asl) underlying this is that passage on Muslim roads is permitted only if safety is maintained. Since the rider can avoid injury from the animal's front movements, they are considered his responsibility; hind kicks are generally unavoidable except by not stopping. The ruling is absolute regarding location: if the rider is on his own property, he is only liable for stepping (ita') because it is a direct action; other injuries are not actionable. This ruling has consequences: if a rider causes death by stepping (ita'), he may be barred from inheritance and must pay kaffarah if he was transgressing.

Key Terms
الكدم (al-kadm): Biting with the front of the mouth; considered a front action for which the rider is liable.
الخبط (al-khabt): Striking with the foreleg; a front action creating liability.
الصدم (al-sadm): Collision with the body; the animal intentionally bumping something.
النفح (al-nafh): Kicking with the hind leg or tail; not subject to liability unless the rider stops the animal.
الإيطاء (al-ita'): Stepping with the foot; a front action that creates full liability including possible inheritance bar and kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For which of the following actions is the rider NOT liable if his animal injures a person on the road?
A) The animal steps on a child's foot (ita')
B) The animal bites a passerby (kadm)
C) The animal kicks with its hind leg (nafh)
D) The animal collides with a pedestrian (sadm)

Key Takeaway
A rider is liable for damage from front parts but not hind kicks, unless he stops the animal — because avoidability determines responsibility.

Rider's Liability for Animal Actions and the Beam Dispute
States rider’s liability and an intra-Hanafi dispute on beam liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a rider's liability for his animal's actions, identify the exceptions, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a beam on a leaning wall.

Core Idea
The rider is liable for avoidable harm; kicks are exempt unless the animal is stopped.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a rider's liability for harm caused by his animal while passing on a public road. It distinguishes between actions the rider can control (trampling, biting) and those he generally cannot (kicking). It then expands to an analogous case of a beam on a leaning wall, where Hanafi scholars differ on the conditions for liability. Understanding these distinctions is key to applying the principle that the right to pass is conditional on safety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and its Exceptions
Read the opening: the rider is liable for awta'at (trampling) and other direct actions, but not for nafahat (kicks) unless he stopped the animal. Note the specific actions listed and the single exception.
The exception for kicks applies only when the animal is moving; stopping the animal makes the rider fully responsible.
2
Understand the Underlying Principle
The commentary explains the reasoning: passing on the road is permissible only if harm is avoided. Trampling and biting are avoidable by the rider; kicks are often unavoidable while moving. This is a qiyas (analogy) drawn from the principle of liability for direct avoidable harm.
The text also analogizes to extending a spout and to the beam case, reinforcing the same principle.
3
Analyze the Beam Dispute
The passage presents a case: a beam placed lengthwise on a leaning wall falls and kills someone. It states Muhammad's absolute view of no liability, but other mashayikh distinguish: slight lean = no liability; excessive lean = liability unless previously ordered to fix. Examine the reasoning behind each view.
Muhammad's view: the beam did not extend into the road; the fall is due to the wall's condition. The mashayikh: excessive lean is a novelty one should guard against.
4
Determine the Practical Consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Muhammad's view, no liability in either case; under the mashayikh's view, liability for excessive lean unless warned. Note that the text reports both without a final tarjih.
This means a mufti may choose either view based on circumstances; some prefer the stricter view for public safety.

Source Text

كتاب الديات باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها وغير ذلك باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها وغير ذلك ضمن الراكب ما أوطأت دابته بيد ورجل أو رأس أو كدمت أو خبطت أو صدمت لا ما نفحت برجل أو ذنب إلا إذا أوقفها في الطريق وإن أصابت بيدها أو رجلها حصاة [؟] حتى [؟] شيئاً فإنه يضمن وكان الجواب فيه كالجواب في اخراج الميزاب، وكذلك لو كان الحائط ماثلاً وكان ممكن وضع الجذع عليه طولاً . لم يخرج شيء منه إلى الطريق ثم سقط ذلك الجذع على إنسان ومات فإنه لا يضمن هكذا ذكر في الكتاب وأطلق الجواب اطلاقاً. وفي مشايخنا من قال هذا إذا كان الحائط مائلاً إلى الطريق ميلاً يسيراً غير فاحش فأما إذا مال ميلاً فاحشاً فإنه يضمن وذلك لأن الميلان إذا كان غير فاحش بحيث يوجب ذلك القدر وقت البناء يكون وجوده وعدمه بمنزلة لأن الجدار قلما يخلو عن قليل ميلان يكون له إلى الطريق فإما إذا كانا ميلاً فاحشاً بحيث يحترز منه عند البناء في الأصل فإنه يضمن إذا سقط ذلك على إنسان إن لم يتقدم إليه بالرفع لأنه متى وضع الجذع طولاً على الحائط المائل فيعتبر بما لو شغل الهواء بغر واسطة، ولو شغل هواء الطريق بواسطة بأن أخرج الجذع عن الحائط فسقط فأصاب إنساناً كذا هذا. ومنهم من قال الجواب فيه كما أطلقه محمد لا يضمن في الحالين، ولو كان الوضع بعد ما تقدم إليه في الحائط ثم سقط الجذع فأصاب إنساناً يقول بأنه يضمن؛ كذا المنتقى والله تعالى أعلم.
Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injuries by Animals and Injuries upon Them and Other Matters. Chapter on Injuries by Animals and Injuries upon Them and Other Matters. The rider is liable for what his animal tramples with its hoof, hand, or head, or bites, or strikes, or collides, but not for what it kicks with its leg or tail, unless he stopped it in the road. And if it strikes with its hand or leg a pebble [?] until [?] something, then he is liable. And the answer regarding it is like the answer regarding extending a spout. Likewise, if the wall was leaning and it was possible to place the beam lengthwise on it, and nothing of it extended into the road, then the beam fell on a person and died, then he is not liable. Thus it is mentioned in the book and the answer is given absolutely. Among our scholars are those who said: This is when the wall leans towards the road slightly, not excessively. But if it leans excessively, then he is liable. Because if the lean is not excessive such that that amount was necessitated at the time of building, its existence and non-existence are equivalent, since a wall is rarely free from a slight lean towards the road. But if it leans excessively such that one would guard against it at the time of building originally, then he is liable if it falls on a person, unless he was previously ordered to remove it. Because when he places the beam lengthwise on the leaning wall, it is considered like occupying the air without an intermediary. And if he occupied the air of the road with an intermediary by extending the beam from the wall and it fell and hit a person, then it is like this. And among them are those who said: The answer regarding it is as Muhammad gave it absolutely: he is not liable in both cases. And if the placement was after the prior order [to fix the wall] and then the beam fell and hit a person, they say he is liable. Thus in al-Muntaqa, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by stating the general Hanafi ruling: a rider is liable for harm caused by his animal's hooves, hands, head, bites, strikes, or collisions, but not for kicks with the leg or tail unless he stopped the animal in the road (making it stationary and thus fully under his control). The commentary then explains that the rule is based on the principle that passing on the road is permitted conditionally on safety; the rider can avoid trampling etc. but cannot easily avoid a kick unless he stops. The text then provides an analogous case: extending a beam from a leaning wall. If the wall leans slightly, the beam placement is like the default, and no liability arises for a fall. But if it leans excessively, the builder is liable if he didn't remove it after being ordered. A minority among Hanafi scholars (some mashayikh) hold that Muhammad's absolute statement applies, i.e., no liability in either case. However, if the person was previously told to fix the wall, then liability applies.

Key Terms
نفحت (nafahat): To kick with the hind leg (by a horse or similar animal).
أوطأت (awta'at): To trample with the hoof of a riding animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which case is a rider NOT liable for his animal's action?
A) When the animal tramples with its hoof.
B) When the animal kicks with its leg while moving.
C) When the animal bites someone.
D) When the animal collides with a person.

Key Takeaway
The rider's liability for his animal depends on control: avoidable actions (trampling, biting) create liability; unavoidable kicks do not unless the animal is stopped.

Principles of Animal Liability and Specific Scenarios
Explains the underlying principle of animal liability and applies it to various scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of animal liability and apply it to determine liability in various scenarios based on conditions such as location and permission.

Core Idea
Passing on the road is permissible only with safety – avoidable harm creates liability.

The passage establishes a foundational principle: the road is shared, and passing is conditional on safety. From this, the Hanafi school derives detailed rules for when a rider is liable for harm caused by his animal. The key is distinguishing between avoidable actions (like trampling or biting) and unavoidable ones (like kicking), and also considering where the animal is stopped and with whose permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational principle
Locate the statement: 'المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح بشرط السلامة'. This is the basis for all subsequent rulings.
The principle links permissibility to a condition of safety, making any unnecessary harm a violation.
2
Classify animal actions as avoidable or unavoidable
The text lists actions: al-kadm (biting), al-khabt (striking with foreleg), al-sadm (collision) – these are avoidable and incur liability. Al-nafhat (kick with leg or tail) is unavoidable unless the animal is stopped.
Avoidability is key: actions not inherent to movement impose liability.
3
Apply location and permission conditions
If the animal is in the rider's own property, liability is limited (only for direct trampling). In another's property without permission, the rider is liable for all harm if he brought it in. Joint property is like one's own.
The condition of permission modifies liability even when avoidable harm occurs.
4
Extend to special spaces: mosque, market, wide road
The mosque is treated like the road. If the Imam designates a place for animals, no liability. Similarly, stopping in a livestock market or wide road is permitted without extra liability.
Public benefit and customary permission (urfi) limit liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها وغير ذلك لما فرغ رحمه الله تعالى من بيان أحكام جناية الإنسان شرع في بيان جناية البهيمة، ولا شك في تقدم جناية الإنسان على البهيمة ؛ كذا في النهاية ويرد عليه أنه لم يفرغ من بيان جناية مطلقاً بل بقي منها جناية المملوك، ولا شك أنه من الإنسان فيقدم على البهيمة وكان يقدم على جناية البهيمة ؛ كذا في غاية البيان قال رحمه الله : (ضمن الراكب ما أوطأت دابته بيد ورجل أو رأس أو كدمت أو خبطت أو صدمت لا ما نفحت برجل أو ذنب إلا إذا أوقفها في الطريق والأصل في هذا الباب أن المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح بشرط السلامة لأنه تصرف في حقه وفي حق غيره من وجه لكونه مشتركاً بين كل الناس إذ الإباحة مقيدة بالسلامة، والاحتراز عن الإيطاء والكدم والصدم والخبط ممكن لأنه ليس من ضرورة السير، وقيدناه بشرط السلامة وفي العيني على الهداية : الكدم بمقدم الإنسان، والخبط باليد والصدم هو أن تطلب الشيء بجسدك ولا يمكن الاحتراز عن النفحة أيضاً لأنه يمكن الاحتراز عن الإيقاف وهو المراد بقوله إذا إذا أوقفها في الطريق أطلق فيما ذكره وهو مقيد بأن يكون في غير ملكه أما إذا كان في ملكه لا يضمن إلا في الإيطاء وهو راكبها لأنه فعل منه مباشرة حتى يحرم به عن الميراث وتجب عليه الكفارة بشرط التعدي فصار كحفر البئر، وفي المباشرة لا يشترط ذلك، وإن كان ذلك في ملك غيره فإن كان غيره تسبب فيه بإذن مالكه فهو كما لو كان في ملكه فإن كان بغير إذن مالكه فإن دخلت الدابة من غير أن يدخلها مالكها ولم يكن معها لم يضمن شيئاً، فإن أدخلها هو ضمن الجميع سواء كان معها أو لم يكن معها لوجود التعدي بالإدخال في ملك الغير والملك المشترك كملكه الخاص به فيما ذكرنا من الأحكام والمسجد كالطريق فيما ذكرناه من الأحكام، ولو جعل الإمام موضعاً لوقوف الدواب عند باب المسجد فلا ضمان فيما يحدث من الوقوف فيه، وكذا إيقاف الدواب في سوق الدواب لأنه مأذون فيه من جهة السلطان وكذا إذا أوقفها في طريق متسعة لا يضر وقوفها بالناس فلا يحتاج فيه إلى إذن الإمام بخلاف ما إذا كانت غير متسعة .
Chapter on Injuries by Animals and Injuries upon Them and Other Matters. When the author (may Allah have mercy on him) finished explaining the rulings on injuries caused by humans, he began explaining injuries by animals. There is no doubt that human injuries take precedence over animal injuries; thus in al-Nihayah. But it is objected that he did not finish the discussion of human injuries absolutely, for there remains the injury by a slave, and there is no doubt that it is from a human and should precede animal injuries; thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (The rider is liable for what his animal tramples with its hoof, hand, or head, or bites, or strikes, or collides, but not for what it kicks with its leg or tail, unless he stopped it in the road. And the principle in this chapter is that passing in Muslim roads is permissible conditionally on safety, because it is an action concerning his right and the right of others in a way, since the road is shared among all people. Permission is restricted by safety, and avoiding trampling, biting, colliding, and striking is possible because they are not necessary for movement. And we conditioned it with safety. And in al-'Ayni on al-Hidayah: al-kadm is with the front of the mouth, al-khabt is with the hand, al-sadm is when one seeks something with one's body. And one cannot avoid the kick either because one can avoid stopping, which is what he means by his saying 'unless he stopped it in the road.' He spoke absolutely in what he mentioned, but it is conditioned that it be in other than his property. As for if it is in his property, he is not liable except for trampling, and he is riding it, because it is a direct action of his such that it prevents inheritance and requires expiation on condition of transgression, so it becomes like digging a well. And in direct action, that condition is not required. And if that is in the property of another, if the other caused it with the owner's permission, it is as if in his own property. If it was without the owner's permission, and the animal entered without the owner bringing it in and he was not with it, he is not liable for anything. If he brought it in, he is liable for everything, whether he was with it or not, because of transgression by entering into another's property. And joint property is like his own property in what we mentioned of rulings. And the mosque is like the road in what we mentioned of rulings. And if the Imam designates a place for animals to stand at the door of the mosque, there is no liability for what occurs from standing there. Likewise, stopping animals in the livestock market because it is permitted by the authority. Likewise, if he stops them in a wide road that does not harm people by their stopping, then permission of the Imam is not needed, unlike when it is not wide.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the sharh, beginning with a note on the order of topics: why animal injuries are mentioned after human injuries, with a critique about the omission of slave injuries. The commentator then explains the principle that passing on the road is permissible only with safety, so the rider must avoid harm. The conditions are detailed: the rider is liable for the animal's foreleg actions (trampling, biting, etc.) as they are avoidable; but he is not liable for kicking (nafhat) because it is too quick, unless he stopped the animal (making it under his control). The liability also depends on whose property the animal is in: in the rider's own property, he is only liable for trampling (direct act); in another's property without permission, he is liable if he brought it in; otherwise not. Joint property is treated like one's own. The mosque is like the road: no liability for stopping in a designated area or a wide road. These are all applications of the same principle.

Key Terms
الكدم (alkadm): Biting with the front of the mouth (by an animal).
الخبط (alkhabt): Striking with the hand (foreleg) of the animal.
الصدم (alsadm): Collision: the animal intentionally rams something with its body.
النفحة (alnafhat): Kicking with the leg or tail, considered an unavoidable action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition that affects liability according to the passage?
A) Whether the animal was on a wide road.
B) Whether the rider stopped the animal.
C) Whether the animal was male or female.
D) Whether the animal was in the owner's property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of animal liability hinges on the distinction between avoidable and unavoidable harm, conditioned by the location and permission of stopping.

Liability for Animal Kicks and the Linguistic Scope of 'Nafhat'
Rulings on animal liability and linguistic clarification of nafhat.

Learning Objective
State the conditions of liability for animal injuries in the scenarios from al-Khulasa, and explain why the term 'nafhat' must be interpreted as a general metaphor for striking.

Core Idea
Liability for animal kicks depends on location and permission; 'nafhat' is a metaphor for any striking motion.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two key contributions: first, it details the rulings of liability (daman vs hadar) in various scenarios from the Khulasa, involving tied animals and rider-goader situations. Second, it resolves an apparent contradiction in a matn by reinterpreting the word 'nafhat' as a general metaphor, not limited to hoof kicks. Understanding this shows the depth of Hanafi legal analysis beyond mere black-letter rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
There are two main sets: (a) tied animals, (b) rider and goader. For each, note the conditions that trigger liability or excuse it.
The tied animal case distinguishes between movement within the tie and freeing itself.
2
Analyze liability conditions for tied animal
If the animal moves about in its tie and causes damage, the owner is liable (mudman) for any part of the body used. If the animal frees itself and then causes damage, it is hadar (no liability).
Key distinction: injury while tied vs. after escaping.
3
Analyze liability conditions for rider-goader
If the rider permits the goading, liability for resulting injuries (other than kicks) is shared; if not permitted, the goader alone is liable. Kicks (nafhat) are always jubbar (no liability) unless the rider is on another's property and orders the goading.
Exceptional case: rider standing in another's property changes the rule.
4
Parse the linguistic problem with 'nafhat'
The matn mentions both leg and tail strikes under 'nafhat', but commentators restrict nafhat to hoof kicks. Identify the contradiction: if nafhat is only leg, mentioning tail adds nothing but conflict.
The author argues that 'nafhat' must be a general metaphor for striking.
5
Apply the metaphorical interpretation
The correct interpretation is to take 'nafhat' as a general metaphor (majaz) for any striking motion, making the mention of both leg and tail consistent and meaningful.
This reflects Hanafi linguistic pragmatics: resolving textual ambiguity via majaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة : دابة مربوطة في غير ملكه فإن ذهب وحل الرباط فقد زالت الجناية فما عطب به من ذلك فهو هدر فلو جالت الدابة في رباطها فما أصاب شيئاً وأتلفه فهو مضمون، سواء ضربت بيدها أو برجلها أو برأسها، فلو ربطها في مكان فذهبت إلى مكان آخر فما أصابت في ذلك المكان فهو هدر. وفيها أيضاً الراكب إذا كانت الدابة تسير به فنخسها رجل فألقت الراكب ؛ إن كان الراكب أذن له في النخس لا يجب على الناخس شيء، وإن كان بغير إذنه ضمن الدية، وإن ضربت الناخس فمات فدمه هدر، وإن أصابت رجلاً آخر بالذنب أو الرجل أو كيفما أصابت إن كان بغير إذن الراكب فالضمان على الناخس، وإن كان بإذنه فالضمان عليهما إلا في النفخة بالرجل أو الذنب فإنه جبار إلا إذا كان الراكب واقفاً بغير ملكه فأمر رجلاً فنخسها فنفحت برجلها فالضمان عليهما، وإن كان بغير إذنه فالضمان على الناخس ولا كفارة عليه فيما نفحت برجلها . قال عامة الشراح : نفحت الدابة إذا ضربت بحافرها. قال في النهاية ومثل هذا في الصحاح والمغرب واقتفى أثره صاحب الكفاية ومعراج الداراية. أقول : كون المذكور في الصحاح كذا ممنوع إذا لم يعتبر فيه كون الضرب بحد الحافر بل قال فيه ونفحت الناقة ضربت برجلها ثم أقول : بقي إشكال في عبارة الكتاب أن الذي يظهر مما ذكر في كتب اللغة ومما ذكره الشراح ههنا أن لا تكون النفحة إلا بالرجل فيلزم أن لا يصح قوله ولا يضمن بالنفحة ما نفحت برجلها أو ذنبها» لأنه يقتضي أن تكون النفحة بالذنب أيضاً بل يلزم أيضاً استدراك قوله (برجالها لأن الضرب بالرجل كان داخلاً في مفهوم النفحة : لا يقال ذكر الرجل محمول على التأكيد وذكر الذنب على التحديد لأنا نقول : اعتبار التأكيد والتحديد معاً بالنظر إلى كلمة واحدة في موضع واحد متعذر للتنافي بينهما كما لا يخفى على الفطن، بل التأويل الصحيح أن تحمل النفحة المذكور في عبارة الكتاب على مطلق الجمع بطريق عموم المجاز فيصح ذكر الرجل والذنب كليهما بلا إشكال فتأمل . وهو قال رحمه الله : ( وإن أصابت بيدها أو رجلها حصاة أو نواة أو أثار غباراً أو حجراً البحر الرائق/ ج ۹/ م ٩
And in al-Khulasa: An animal tied in other than its owner's property: if it goes and the knot is untied, the injury ceases, so whatever is damaged by that is free of liability (hadar). But if the animal moves about in its tie and hits something and destroys it, it is guaranteed (mudman), whether it struck with its hand, leg, or head. If it was tied in one place and went to another place, whatever it hits in that other place is free of liability. And in it also: The rider, when the animal is moving, and a man goads it and it throws the rider: if the rider permitted the goading, nothing is owed by the goader; if without permission, he guarantees the blood-money (diyah). If the goader is struck and dies, his blood is wasted (hadar). If it hits another person with its tail or leg or however it hits: if without the rider's permission, liability is on the goader; if with his permission, liability is on both of them, except for the kick with the leg or tail, which is free of liability (jubbar), unless the rider was standing in someone else's property and ordered a man to goad it and it kicked with its leg, then liability is on both. And if without his permission, liability is on the goader, and there is no expiation upon him for what it kicked with its leg. The generality of the commentators said: “Nafahat” of the animal is when it strikes with its hoof. He said in al-Nihayah, and similar is in al-Sihah and al-Mughrib, and followed by the author of al-Kifayah and Mi'raj al-Dirayah. I say: The claim that it is mentioned in al-Sihah like this is not acceptable, because he did not consider the striking with the edge of the hoof, but rather he said: “Nafahat” of the she-camel is when it strikes with its leg. Then I say: There remains a problem in the wording of the book: what appears from what is mentioned in language books and from what the commentators state here is that nafhat is only with the leg, so his saying “He is not liable for nafhat when it kicks with its leg or its tail” would be invalid, because it requires that nafhat be also with the tail, and it also requires the addition of his saying “with its leg” because striking with the leg was already included in the concept of nafhat. It cannot be said that the mention of the leg is for emphasis and the mention of the tail for specification, because we say: considering emphasis and specification together with reference to one word in one place is impossible due to mutual contradiction, as is not hidden from the intelligent. Rather, the correct interpretation is to take nafhat mentioned in the book's wording as meaning absolute striking (jami') by way of general metaphor (majaz), so the mention of both leg and tail is correct without problem. So reflect. And he (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And if it strikes with its hand or leg a pebble or a date-stone or raises dust or a stone... al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, vol. 9, p. 9
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage provides additional rulings from al-Khulasa on animals tied outside their owner's property, and on the case of a rider and a goader. It then delves into a linguistic clarification of the word 'nafhat'. The author argues that the common definition (striking with the hoof) is too narrow; rather, nafhat in the context of the ruling should be interpreted metaphorically to include both leg and tail strikes, resolving an apparent contradiction in the matn. This is a prime example of how meticulous Hanafi scholars are in resolving apparent textual inconsistencies.

Key Terms
نفحت (nafahat): Linguistically, kicking with the hind leg; but the author argues it can be interpreted metaphorically as any striking motion.
جبار (jubbar): Free of liability, no compensation required.
مضمون (mudman): Guaranteed, i.e., liability attaches and compensation is owed.
هدر (hadar): Wasted, i.e., no compensation is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's linguistic analysis, what is the correct interpretation of 'nafhat' in the matn?
A) It means striking with the hoof only.
B) It means striking with the leg only.
C) It is a general metaphor for any striking motion, including tail.
D) It is a mistake in the matn.

Key Takeaway
Liability for animal kicks in Hanafi fiqh is highly contextual, and the definition of 'nafhat' must be interpreted metaphorically to maintain textual consistency.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Liability for Stones and Excrement: Avoidable vs Unavoidable Harm
Hanafi ruling on small/large stones and animal excrement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for liability when an animal causes harm through stones or excrement, and identify when the owner is liable based on foreseeability and transgression.

Core Idea
Avoidable harm creates liability; unavoidable harm does not.

Animals naturally produce small stones, dust, urine, and dung. The law must distinguish between inevitable accompaniments of travel and preventable acts. This lesson clarifies the boundary: when the owner stops the animal for an unnecessary reason, he becomes liable for resulting harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and harm
Determine what the animal did (e.g., kick up a stone, defecate, urinate) and what damage resulted (e.g., eye injury, property loss).
The harm must be directly caused by the animal's action.
2
Assess foreseeability
Is the harm something the owner could have prevented? Small stones and dust are unavoidable; large stones are preventable. Similarly, natural excretion during movement is unavoidable, but stopping specifically for that purpose is necessary.
The key is whether the owner had control over the situation.
3
Evaluate transgression (ta'addi)
Did the owner act improperly? Stopping the animal for a non‑essential reason is transgressive; stopping for the animal to relieve itself is not.
Transgression triggers liability regardless of the harm's nature.
4
Apply the ruling
If the harm was unavoidable and no transgression, no liability. If preventable (large stone) or transgressive (unnecessary stopping), liability attaches.
The ruling is wajib compensation when liable.

لأن التحرز عن الحجارة الصغار والغبار متعذر الدابة لا يخلو عنه وعن الكبار من الحجارة ممكن، وإنما يكون ذلك عادة . هداية الراكب فيضمن. وفي الذخيرة : قيل لو عنف الدابة فأثارت حجراً صغيراً أو كبيراً يضمن. في الطريق فأمر رجلاً أن ينخس دابته فنخسها فقتلت رجلاً فدية الرجل الأجنبي على الناخس والراكب جميعاً ودم الآمر بالنخس ،هدر، ولو سارت عن موضعها ثم نفحت من فور النخس فالضمان على الناخس دون الراكب، ولو لم فالضمان على الراكب والسائق جميعاً. وفيه أيضاً : رك�ب دابة بأمر أبيه ثم إن الصبي الراكب أمر صبياً فنخسها فالقول فيه إذا كان مأذوناً كالقول في الكبير، وإن كان لم يؤذن له في ذلك فأمر صبياً حتى نخسها فسارت ونفحت من النخسة فعلى الناخس الضمان ولا شيء على الراكب، وإن أمر بذلك ووطئت إنساناً فقتلته وكان سيرها من النخسة فالدية على عاقلة الناخس ولا يرجعون بذلك على عاقلة الراكب. وفيه أيضاً : رجل ركب دابة والناخس نصفين. رجل أذن رجلاً أن يدخل داره وهو راكب فدخلها راكباً فوطئت دابته على شيء كان ضامناً، له، إن كان سائقاً أو قائداً فلا ضمان. أدخل بعيراً برحله فوقع عليه المتعلم فقتله فقد اختلف المشايخ فمنهم غير متعلم فحكمه حكم متعلم . وفي الفتاوى : ربط حماره في أرضه ليأكل علفاً فجاء حمار رجل فعقره فجعله معيوباً عيباً فاحشاً قال : لا يرجع بنقصان العيب على صاحب الحمار . قلت قال القاضي بديع الدين : إن كان صاحبه معه يضمن وإلا فلا يضمن قال رحمه الله : (وما ضمنه الراكب ضمن السائق والقائد) أي كل شيء يضمنه الراكب يضمنان لأنهما سببان كالراكب في غير الايطاء فيجب عليهما الضمان بالتعدي فيه كالراكب. وقوله وما ضمنه الراكب ضمنه السائق والقائد يطرد وينعكس في الصحيح. وذكر القدوري أن السائق يضمن النفحة بالرجل لأنه بمرأى عينه فيمكنه الاحتراز عنها مع السير وغائبة عن بصر الراكب والقائد فلا يمكنهما الاحتراز عنها بخلاف الكدم والصدم. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : يضمنون كلهم النفحة والحجة عليه ما ذكرنا . وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام) (الرجل) جبار» ومعناه النفحة بالرجل.
Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them and Other Matters. Or a date pit or it raised dust or a small stone and it gouged out an eye: he is not liable; if large, he is liable. If it defecated or urinated in the road, he is not liable for what is damaged thereby if he stopped it for that purpose; if he stopped it for something else, he is liable. Because protecting against small stones and dust is impossible, as animals are never free from them, while protection against large stones is possible, and it is customary. The guidance of the rider: he is liable. In al-Dhakhirah: it was said if he violently spurred the animal so it raised a small or large stone, he is liable. In al-Zahiriyyah: if he stopped an animal in the Muslims' road and did not tie it, and it went to another place and damaged something, there is no liability on its owner, like this in al-Kubra. Every animal, whether wild or otherwise, is liable as long as it has not changed from its normal state. If a man rode his animal on the road and struck it and restrained it with the bridle, so it struck with its leg or tail, nothing is upon him. In al-Sughnaqi: and of this type is what they said about one who drives an animal loaded with wheat, and it damages something on the road, life or property: it is in several aspects: if the driver, leader, or rider says 'Go away' and [the person] heard this speech and did not leave, it is two aspects: if he stayed in his place not moving despite ability to move or did not find another place to go, so he remained there until his clothes were torn - in this first aspect, the owner of the animal is not liable; in the second aspect, he is liable. If he did not say 'Go away', the rider is liable. In al-Fatawa: a man drove a donkey loaded with firewood; the driver said in Persian 'koseet or yarzeh' and the stationary person did not hear until the wood hit him and tore his garment, or he could not manage to step aside due to shortness of time: he is liable. If he heard and could [move] but did not move, he is not liable. An example of this: one who places a donkey on the road with clothes on it, and a rider came and ran over and tore the clothes: if the rider saw the donkey and [intentionally] set [the animal upon it?], he is liable; if he did not see it, he should not be liable. Clothes on the road so people pass over them without seeing them: no liability. Likewise, a man sat on the road and a person fell upon him so he did not avoid him, and the seated man died: the [fallen] is not liable. Then the one who drove the donkey, if he did not call out 'O Lord' or 'if you wish' until the wood caught on a man's garment and tore it: he is liable if the donkey walked to the owner of the garment; if [the man] walked to the donkey while it was standing still, or did not move away, he is not liable. If [the animal] jumped due to goading upon a man and killed him, or it trampled a man and killed him, the liability is on the goader, not the rider. In al-Kafi: his blood-money is on the 'aqilah of the goader; like this in al-Dhakhirah. He (the author) said (may Allah have mercy on him): (If it defecated or urinated in the road, he is not liable for what is damaged by it if he stopped it for that purpose; if he stopped it for something else, he is liable) because the movement of the animal is never free from dung and urine, so he cannot avoid it, so he is not liable for what is damaged by it when it defecated or urinated while moving, and likewise if he stopped it for that purpose because some animals do not do that except while standing. That is meant by his statement 'if he stopped it for something else and it urinated or defecated and a person was damaged by it, he is liable because he is transgressing in stopping as it is not necessary for travel and it is also more harmful than travel because it is longer [in duration], so it is not like [travel]'. That is meant by his statement 'if he stopped it for that purpose and if he stopped it for something else, he is liable'. In al-Muntaqa: a man standing with his animal... Then the text continues with additional scenarios: rider liable, driver and leader liable, and the rider must give kaffarah but not them; two boys collided in the road; a man ordered another to goad his animal so he goaded it and it killed a man: the blood-money of the foreign man is on the goader and the rider jointly, and the blood of the orderer is wasted. If it moved from its place then kicked due to the goading, the liability is on the goader, not the rider. If it did not move and kicked the goader and another man, killing both, the blood-money of the foreigner is on the goader and rider, and half the blood-money of the goader is on the rider. If the rider did not stop the animal on the road but it became stubborn and stopped, so he and another goaded it to move, and it kicked a man: nothing is on them. Also in it: a man hired from another an animal to go on it for his need, and its owner followed him; he may drive it. If the rider stopped in the road at a gathering and the animal became stubborn, so the owner of the animal goaded it, struck it, or drove it, and the animal kicked while standing and killed a man: the liability is on the rider and driver jointly. Also in it: he rode an animal by order of his father, then the boy rider ordered a boy to goad it: the ruling is if he was permitted, it is like the adult; if he was not permitted, and he ordered a boy to goad it, and it moved and kicked due to the goading: the liability is on the goader, nothing on the rider. If he ordered that and it trampled a man and killed him, and its movement was from the goading: the blood-money is on the 'aqilah of the goader, and they do not recourse to the 'aqilah of the rider. Also in it: a man rode an animal belonging to another that its owner had stopped on the road while its owner was absent, so the owner of the animal ordered a man to goad it, and it kicked a man or kicked the orderer: the blood-money is on the goader. If the orderer stopped it on the road then ordered a man to goad it, and it killed a man: the blood-money is on the orderer and goader half each. A man gave permission to another to enter his house while he was riding, and he entered riding, and his animal trampled something: he was liable for it; if he was driving or leading, no liability. He brought a camel with its saddle and a student fell upon it and was killed: the shaykhs differed; some said no liability on the owner of the student; some said if the owner of the student brought it without permission of the house owner, he is liable; if he entered with permission, no liability; this is adopted by al-Faqih Abu al-Layth and upon it is the fatwa. In Fatawa al-Khulasah: if the camel was not trained, its ruling is like the trained one. In al-Fatawa: he tied his donkey in his land to eat fodder, and another man's donkey came and gored it, making it severely defective: he said: he does not recoup the defect depreciation from the owner of the donkey. I (the commentator) say: al-Qadi Badi' al-Din said: if its owner was with it, he is liable; otherwise not. He (the author) said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And what the rider is liable for, the driver and leader are liable for) meaning everything the rider is liable for, they are liable because they are causes like the rider except in trampling, so liability for transgression is obligatory on them like the rider. And his statement 'what the rider is liable for, the driver and leader are liable for' applies universally and conversely in the correct view. Al-Quduri mentioned that the driver is liable for the kick with the leg because it is before his eyes, so he can avoid it while moving, and it is absent from the sight of the rider and leader, so they cannot avoid it, unlike biting and collision. Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: all of them are liable for the kick. The proof against him is what we mentioned. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'The foot (of an animal) is without liability (jubar)' meaning the kick with the leg.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq on the rulings regarding liability for damages caused by animals. The author first addresses a scenario from the matn: if an animal kicks up a small stone or dust that blinds an eye, the owner is not liable because such small debris is unavoidable. But if the stone is large, liability attaches because it could have been prevented. Next, it covers animal excrement: if the animal defecates or urinates while moving, or is stopped specifically for that purpose, no liability arises as this is natural. However, if the owner stops the animal for an unrelated reason and it then relieves itself causing harm, he is liable for transgression. Then various subsidiary cases are examined: situations where a driver or rider says 'go away' to a pedestrian, Persian phrases, goading causing kicks or trampling, hired animals, children as riders, and animals trespassing. The ruling consistently distinguishes between foreseeable harm (large stone, unnecessary stopping) and unavoidable harm (small stone, normal excretion). A key principle is that the rider's liability extends to the driver and leader except in trampling where the driver is more liable because he can see the legs. The passage ends with a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds all equally liable for a kick; the Hanafi reliance on the hadith 'al-rajulu jubar' (the foot is free of liability) limits liability to the driver who can see and avoid the kick. The relied-upon positions (mufta bihi) are indicated, e.g., the view of Abu al-Layth regarding bringing a trained camel into a house with permission.

Key Terms
النخس (al-nakhas): Goading or spurring an animal to move, often with a stick or prod.
النفحة (al-nafhah): A kick or strike with the animal's leg, particularly from a horse or donkey.
الرجل جبار (al-rajulu jubar): A prophetical saying meaning that injury caused by an animal's foot is not compensable; the harm is 'wasted' (jubar).
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The male paternal relatives of the killer who share payment of blood-money (diyah) in cases of unintentional killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, why is an owner not liable when his horse kicks up a small pebble that hits a pedestrian?
A) Because the pebble is too small to cause significant harm.
B) Because small stones are unavoidable, so there is no transgression.
C) Because the pedestrian should have been more careful.
D) Because the owner can claim the animal was not under control.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for animal-caused harm hinges on foreseeability and transgression: unavoidable harms bear no liability, but avoidable or transgressive acts do.

Diyat — Blood Money
advanced
10 min
Goading and the Kick Dispute
Hanafi ruling on goading and dispute on kick liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who is liable when an animal kicks after being goaded, and explain the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on the interpretation of 'al-rajulu jubar'.

Core Idea
The goader is liable for the animal's immediate reaction; the driver pays for the kick, not the rider.

Goading an animal can trigger an immediate kick. Who bears responsibility? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the goader, the rider, the driver, and the leader based on the hadith 'al-rajulu jubar'. This lesson unpacks the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds all three equally liable for a kick.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles: goader, rider, driver, leader
The passage differentiates four roles: the goader (nakhis) who spurs the animal, the rider (rakib) who sits on the animal, the driver (sa'iq) who walks behind and drives, and the leader (qa'id) who leads from the front.
Each has different ability to see and control the animal's legs.
2
State the Hanafi rule for goading
If the animal jumps or kicks due to the goading, the goader is liable for any resulting harm, not the rider. The blood-money is on the 'aqilah of the goader.
The goader is the immediate cause.
3
State the Hanafi rule for the kick (nafha)
The driver is liable for a kick because he can see the legs and can avoid it. The rider and leader are not liable because the kick is out of their sight, based on the hadith 'al-rajulu jubar'.
The hadith is interpreted narrowly by Hanafis.
4
Present the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the rider, driver, and leader all equally liable for the kick because they all have control over the animal.
His proof is general causation.
5
Analyse the Hanafi response and tarjih
The Hanafi response uses the hadith and the impossibility for the rider/leader to see the legs. The tarjih is for the Hanafi position based on the explicit text and rational necessity.
The practical difference is who pays blood-money.

كتاب الديات باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها وغير ذلك
أو نواة أو أثار غباراً أو حجراً صغيراً ففقأ عيناً لم يضمن ولو كبيراً ضمن فإن راثت أو بالت في الطريق لم يضمن ما عطب به إن أوقفها لذلك وإن أوقفها لغيره ضمن
لأن التحرز عن الحجارة الصغار والغبار متعذر الدابة لا يخلو عنه وعن الكبار من الحجارة ممكن، وإنما يكون ذلك عادة . هداية الراكب فيضمن. وفي الذخيرة : قيل لو عنف الدابة فأثارت حجراً صغيراً أو كبيراً يضمن. وفي الظهيرية : لو أوقف دابة في طريق المسلمين ولم يربطها فسارت إلى مكان آخر وأتلفت شيئاً فلا ضمان على صاحبها كذا في الكبرى. وكل بهيمة من سبع أو غيره فهو ضامن ما لم يتغير عن حاله، وإذا سار الرجل على دابته في الطريق فضربها وكبحها باللجام فضربت برجلها أو بذنبها لم يكن عليه شيء. وفي السغناقي ومن هذا الجنس ما قالوا فيمن ساق دابة عليها وقر من الحنطة فأتلفت شيئاً من الطريق نفساً أو مالاً فهو على وجوه : إما إن قال السائق أو القائد أو الراكب اليك فإن سمع هذه المقالة ولم يذهب فهو على وجهين : إما مكانه إن لم يبرح من مع القدرة على المكان أو لم يجد مكاناً آخر ليذهب فمكث في مكانه ذلك حتى تخرق ثيابه ففي هذا الوجه الأول لا يضمن صاحب الدابة وفي الوجه الثاني يضمن، وإن لم يقل إليك ركب الدابة ضمن وفي الفتاوى رجل ساق حماراً عليه وقر حطب فقال السائق بالفارسية (كوسيت أو يرثة فلم يسمع الواقف حتى أصابه الحطب فخرق ثوبه أو لكن لم يتهيأ له ينتحي عن الطريق لقصر المدة ضمن وإن سمع وتهيأ ولم ينتقل لا يضمن، ونظير هذا من أقام حماراً على الطريق وعليه ثياب فجاء راكب وكر شلا وخرق الثياب، إن كان الراكب يبصر الحمار وأرسون يضمن وإن لم يبصر ينبغي أن لا يضمنوا . الثياب على الطريق فجعل الناس يمرون عليه وهم لا يبصرون لا يضمن، وكذا رجل جلس على الطريق فوقع عليه إنسان فلم يرده فمات الجالس لا يضمن ثم الذي ساق الحمار إذا كان لا ينادي يا رب أي لو شئت حتى تعلق الحطب بثوب رجل فتخرق يضمن إن مشى الحمار إلى صاحب الثوب، وإن مشى إلى الحمار وهو يراه أو لم يتباعد عليه لا يضمن. ولو وثب من نخسه على رجل فقتله أو وطئت رجلاً فقتلته فالضمان على الناخس دون الراكب. وفي الكافي : فديته على عاقلة الناخس؛ كذا في الذخيرة. قال رحمه الله : (فإن راثت أو بالت في الطريق لم يضمن ما عطب به إن أوقفها لذلك وإن أوقفها لغيره ضمن) لأن سير الدابة لا يخلو عن روث وبول فلا يمكنه التحرز عنه فلا يضمن ما تلف به فيما إذا راثت أو بالت وهي تسير، وكذا إذا أوقفها لذلك لأن من الدواب من لا يفعل ذلك إلا واقفاً وهو المراد بقوله «وإن أوقفها لغيره فبالت أو راثت فعطب به إنسان ضمن لأنه متعد في الإيقاف إذ هو ليس من ضروريات السير وهو أكثر ضرراً أيضاً من السير لكونه أدوم منه فلا يلحق به وهو المراد بقوله «وإن أوقفها لذلك وإن أوقفها لغيره ضمن وفي المنتقى : رجل واقف على دابته
ضمنه الراكب ضمن السائق والقائد وعلى الراكب الكفارة لا عليهما ولو اصطدم
صبي
في الطريق فأمر رجلاً أن ينخس دابته فنخسها فقتلت رجلاً فدية الرجل الأجنبي على الناخس والراكب جميعاً ودم الآمر بالنخس ،هدر، ولو سارت عن موضعها ثم نفحت من فور النخس فالضمان على الناخس دون الراكب، ولو لم تسر ونفحت الناخس ورجلاً آخر وقتلتهما فدية الأجنبي على الناخس والراكب ونصف دية الناخس على الراكب ولو لم يوقفها الراكب على الطريق ولكن حرنت فوقفت فنخسها هو وغيره لتسير فنفحت إنساناً فلا شيء عليهما. وفيه أيضاً : رجل اكترى من آخر دابة ليذهب عليها في حاجة له فاتبعه صاحبها فله أن يسوقها، فإن وقف الراكب في الطريق على أهل مجلس فحرنت فنخسها صاحب الدابة أو ضربها أو ساقها فنفحت الدابة وهي واقفة فقتلت إنساناً فالضمان على الراكب والسائق جميعاً. وفيه أيضاً : رك�ب دابة بأمر أبيه ثم إن الصبي الراكب أمر صبياً فنخسها فالقول فيه إذا كان مأذوناً كالقول في الكبير، وإن كان لم يؤذن له في ذلك فأمر صبياً حتى نخسها فسارت ونفحت من النخسة فعلى الناخس الضمان ولا شيء على الراكب، وإن أمر بذلك ووطئت إنساناً فقتلته وكان سيرها من النخسة فالدية على عاقلة الناخس ولا يرجعون بذلك على عاقلة الراكب. وفيه أيضاً : رجل ركب دابة رجل قد أوقفها ربها في الطريق وربها وغاب فأمر رب الدابة رجلاً حتى نخسها فنفحت رجلاً أو نفحت الآمر فديته على الناخس، وإن كان الأمر أوقفها في الطريق ثم أمر رجلاً حتى نخسها فقتلت رجلاً فديته على الأمر والناخس نصفين. رجل أذن رجلاً أن يدخل داره وهو راكب فدخلها راكباً فوطئت دابته على شيء كان ضامناً، له، إن كان سائقاً أو قائداً فلا ضمان. أدخل بعيراً برحله فوقع عليه المتعلم فقتله فقد اختلف المشايخ فمنهم من قال لا ضمان على صاحب المتعلم، وقال بعضهم إن أدخل صاحب المتعلم بغير إذن صاحب الدار فعليه الضمان وإن كان دخلها بإذنه فلا ضمان وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث وعليه الفتوى وفي فتاوى الخلاصة ولو كان البعير غير متعلم فحكمه حكم متعلم . وفي الفتاوى : ربط حماره في أرضه ليأكل علفاً فجاء حمار رجل فعقره فجعله معيوباً عيباً فاحشاً قال : لا يرجع بنقصان العيب على صاحب الحمار . قلت قال القاضي بديع الدين : إن كان صاحبه معه يضمن وإلا فلا يضمن قال رحمه الله : (وما ضمنه الراكب ضمن السائق والقائد) أي كل شيء يضمنه الراكب يضمنان لأنهما سببان كالراكب في غير الايطاء فيجب عليهما الضمان بالتعدي فيه كالراكب. وقوله وما ضمنه الراكب ضمنه السائق والقائد يطرد وينعكس في الصحيح. وذكر القدوري أن السائق يضمن النفحة بالرجل لأنه بمرأى عينه فيمكنه الاحتراز عنها مع السير وغائبة عن بصر الراكب والقائد فلا يمكنهما الاحتراز عنها بخلاف الكدم والصدم. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : يضمنون كلهم النفحة والحجة عليه ما ذكرنا . وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام) (الرجل) جبار» ومعناه النفحة بالرجل.
Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury to Them and Other Matters. Or a date pit or it raised dust or a small stone and it gouged out an eye: he is not liable; if large, he is liable. If it defecated or urinated in the road, he is not liable for what is damaged thereby if he stopped it for that purpose; if he stopped it for something else, he is liable. Because protecting against small stones and dust is impossible, as animals are never free from them, while protection against large stones is possible, and it is customary. The guidance of the rider: he is liable. In al-Dhakhirah: it was said if he violently spurred the animal so it raised a small or large stone, he is liable. In al-Zahiriyyah: if he stopped an animal in the Muslims' road and did not tie it, and it went to another place and damaged something, there is no liability on its owner, like this in al-Kubra. Every animal, whether wild or otherwise, is liable as long as it has not changed from its normal state. If a man rode his animal on the road and struck it and restrained it with the bridle, so it struck with its leg or tail, nothing is upon him. In al-Sughnaqi: and of this type is what they said about one who drives an animal loaded with wheat, and it damages something on the road, life or property: it is in several aspects: if the driver, leader, or rider says 'Go away' and [the person] heard this speech and did not leave, it is two aspects: if he stayed in his place not moving despite ability to move or did not find another place to go, so he remained there until his clothes were torn - in this first aspect, the owner of the animal is not liable; in the second aspect, he is liable. If he did not say 'Go away', the rider is liable. In al-Fatawa: a man drove a donkey loaded with firewood; the driver said in Persian 'koseet or yarzeh' and the stationary person did not hear until the wood hit him and tore his garment, or he could not manage to step aside due to shortness of time: he is liable. If he heard and could [move] but did not move, he is not liable. An example of this: one who places a donkey on the road with clothes on it, and a rider came and ran over and tore the clothes: if the rider saw the donkey and [intentionally] set [the animal upon it?], he is liable; if he did not see it, he should not be liable. Clothes on the road so people pass over them without seeing them: no liability. Likewise, a man sat on the road and a person fell upon him so he did not avoid him, and the seated man died: the [fallen] is not liable. Then the one who drove the donkey, if he did not call out 'O Lord' or 'if you wish' until the wood caught on a man's garment and tore it: he is liable if the donkey walked to the owner of the garment; if [the man] walked to the donkey while it was standing still, or did not move away, he is not liable. If [the animal] jumped due to goading upon a man and killed him, or it trampled a man and killed him, the liability is on the goader, not the rider. In al-Kafi: his blood-money is on the 'aqilah of the goader; like this in al-Dhakhirah. He (the author) said (may Allah have mercy on him): (If it defecated or urinated in the road, he is not liable for what is damaged by it if he stopped it for that purpose; if he stopped it for something else, he is liable) because the movement of the animal is never free from dung and urine, so he cannot avoid it, so he is not liable for what is damaged by it when it defecated or urinated while moving, and likewise if he stopped it for that purpose because some animals do not do that except while standing. That is meant by his statement 'if he stopped it for something else and it urinated or defecated and a person was damaged by it, he is liable because he is transgressing in stopping as it is not necessary for travel and it is also more harmful than travel because it is longer [in duration], so it is not like [travel]'. That is meant by his statement 'if he stopped it for that purpose and if he stopped it for something else, he is liable'. In al-Muntaqa: a man standing with his animal... Then the text continues with additional scenarios: rider liable, driver and leader liable, and the rider must give kaffarah but not them; two boys collided in the road; a man ordered another to goad his animal so he goaded it and it killed a man: the blood-money of the foreign man is on the goader and the rider jointly, and the blood of the orderer is wasted. If it moved from its place then kicked due to the goading, the liability is on the goader, not the rider. If it did not move and kicked the goader and another man, killing both, the blood-money of the foreigner is on the goader and rider, and half the blood-money of the goader is on the rider. If the rider did not stop the animal on the road but it became stubborn and stopped, so he and another goaded it to move, and it kicked a man: nothing is on them. Also in it: a man hired from another an animal to go on it for his need, and its owner followed him; he may drive it. If the rider stopped in the road at a gathering and the animal became stubborn, so the owner of the animal goaded it, struck it, or drove it, and the animal kicked while standing and killed a man: the liability is on the rider and driver jointly. Also in it: he rode an animal by order of his father, then the boy rider ordered a boy to goad it: the ruling is if he was permitted, it is like the adult; if he was not permitted, and he ordered a boy to goad it, and it moved and kicked due to the goading: the liability is on the goader, nothing on the rider. If he ordered that and it trampled a man and killed him, and its movement was from the goading: the blood-money is on the 'aqilah of the goader, and they do not recourse to the 'aqilah of the rider. Also in it: a man rode an animal belonging to another that its owner had stopped on the road while its owner was absent, so the owner of the animal ordered a man to goad it, and it kicked a man or kicked the orderer: the blood-money is on the goader. If the orderer stopped it on the road then ordered a man to goad it, and it killed a man: the blood-money is on the orderer and goader half each. A man gave permission to another to enter his house while he was riding, and he entered riding, and his animal trampled something: he was liable for it; if he was driving or leading, no liability. He brought a camel with its saddle and a student fell upon it and was killed: the shaykhs differed; some said no liability on the owner of the student; some said if the owner of the student brought it without permission of the house owner, he is liable; if he entered with permission, no liability; this is adopted by al-Faqih Abu al-Layth and upon it is the fatwa. In Fatawa al-Khulasah: if the camel was not trained, its ruling is like the trained one. In al-Fatawa: he tied his donkey in his land to eat fodder, and another man's donkey came and gored it, making it severely defective: he said: he does not recoup the defect depreciation from the owner of the donkey. I (the commentator) say: al-Qadi Badi' al-Din said: if its owner was with it, he is liable; otherwise not. He (the author) said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And what the rider is liable for, the driver and leader are liable for) meaning everything the rider is liable for, they are liable because they are causes like the rider except in trampling, so liability for transgression is obligatory on them like the rider. And his statement 'what the rider is liable for, the driver and leader are liable for' applies universally and conversely in the correct view. Al-Quduri mentioned that the driver is liable for the kick with the leg because it is before his eyes, so he can avoid it while moving, and it is absent from the sight of the rider and leader, so they cannot avoid it, unlike biting and collision. Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: all of them are liable for the kick. The proof against him is what we mentioned. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'The foot (of an animal) is without liability (jubar)' meaning the kick with the leg.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq on the rulings regarding liability for damages caused by animals. The author first addresses a scenario from the matn: if an animal kicks up a small stone or dust that blinds an eye, the owner is not liable because such small debris is unavoidable. But if the stone is large, liability attaches because it could have been prevented. Next, it covers animal excrement: if the animal defecates or urinates while moving, or is stopped specifically for that purpose, no liability arises as this is natural. However, if the owner stops the animal for an unrelated reason and it then relieves itself causing harm, he is liable for transgression. Then various subsidiary cases are examined: situations where a driver or rider says 'go away' to a pedestrian, Persian phrases, goading causing kicks or trampling, hired animals, children as riders, and animals trespassing. The ruling consistently distinguishes between foreseeable harm (large stone, unnecessary stopping) and unavoidable harm (small stone, normal excretion). A key principle is that the rider's liability extends to the driver and leader except in trampling where the driver is more liable because he can see the legs. The passage ends with a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds all equally liable for a kick; the Hanafi reliance on the hadith 'al-rajulu jubar' (the foot is free of liability) limits liability to the driver who can see and avoid the kick. The relied-upon positions (mufta bihi) are indicated, e.g., the view of Abu al-Layth regarding bringing a trained camel into a house with permission.

Key Terms
النخس (al-nakhas): Goading or spurring an animal to move, often with a stick or prod.
النفحة (al-nafhah): A kick or strike with the animal's leg, particularly from a horse or donkey.
الرجل جبار (al-rajulu jubar): A prophetical saying meaning that injury caused by an animal's foot is not compensable; the harm is 'wasted' (jubar).
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The male paternal relatives of the killer who share payment of blood-money (diyah) in cases of unintentional killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the driver liable for a kick (nafha) but not the rider?
A) Because the driver has greater control over the animal.
B) Because the driver can see the animal's legs and avoid the kick, while the rider cannot.
C) Because the driver is considered the owner.
D) Because the hadith 'al-rajulu jubar' applies only to the driver.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for an animal's kick depends on ability to see and avoid: the driver pays, the rider and leader are exempt by the hadith 'al-rajulu jubar'.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Liability for Animal Injury: Rider, Driver, and Leader
Commentary on liability for harm caused by animals involving rider, driver, and leader

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between direct and indirect liability for animal harm and apply the ruling to cases involving a rider, driver, and leader.

Core Idea
Liability follows direct causation: rider pays for trampling; driver pays for kicks; leader shares rider's liability.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks a crucial distinction in Hanafi tort law: the difference between direct (mubashir) and indirect (sabab) causation in animal injuries. Understanding who is liable for what—and why—requires careful attention to the roles of rider, driver, and leader, and the type of harm caused. The commentary also records a significant inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding liability for kicks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three roles and types of harm
In this passage, three persons are associated with an animal: the rider (raakib), the driver (saaiq), and the leader (qa'id). Types of harm include trampling (i'taa'), kicking (nafha), biting (kadm), and bumping (sadm). Your first task is to match each role to the type of harm for which they are liable according to the Hanafi school.
The key distinction: the rider is liable for trampling (due to his weight), the driver for kicks (due to his ability to see and avoid), and the leader jointly with the rider in non-trampling harms.
2
Understand the Hanafi distinction: mubashir vs. sabab
The commentary explains that the rider is a direct cause (mubashir) because the harm results from his weight on the animal, which is a direct act. The driver and leader are indirect causes (sabab) because their action (driving or leading) does not directly produce the harm but sets conditions for it. This distinction has significant legal consequences: direct causation triggers full liability including kaffarah and inheritance restrictions, while indirect causation only triggers financial liability.
The rule: 'al-mubashir yadman wa al-musabbib la yadman' (the direct cause is liable, the indirect cause is not) is a key Hanafi maxim, but it has exceptions when the cause works by itself.
3
Follow the qiyas with the pit analogy
The author uses an analogy: digging a pit (hafr) does not alone cause death; someone must fall in. Thus the digger is not liable for a death unless he also threw the victim in. This illustrates that an indirect cause (sabab) that does not work by itself does not entail liability alongside a direct cause. The same applies to the driver: his driving does not alone cause trampling; the rider's weight does. Therefore, the driver is not liable for trampling.
The exception: if the cause works by itself—like the rider's weight in trampling—then the indirect cause (driver) shares liability.
4
Examine the Shafi'i dispute and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i held that all three (rider, driver, leader) are equally liable for the animal's kick (nafha). The Hanafi response relies on the hadith 'al-rijalu jubbar' (the leg is free) meaning the kick is not subject to diyah. Furthermore, they argue that the driver can see the kick and avoid it, while the rider and leader cannot, so only the driver is liable. This shows the Hanafi emphasis on practical ability to avoid harm.
The practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, only the driver pays diyah for a kick; the rider and leader do not. In the Shafi'i school, all three pay jointly.
5
Apply to the caravan cases from al-Asl
The passage includes detailed scenarios from al-Asl about a caravan of camels with multiple leaders and drivers. These cases illustrate how liability is distributed based on control and direct action. For example, a leader is liable for what his animal tramples in front, but not necessarily for what an animal behind him does. Work through these scenarios to see how the rules are consistently applied.
Key principle: liability follows the person in actual control of the animal at the time of harm.
