ضمنه الراكب ضمن السائق والقائد وعلى الراكب الكفارة لا عليهما ولو اصطدم صبي في الطريق فأمر رجلاً أن ينخس دابته فنخسها فقتلت رجلاً فدية الرجل الأجنبي على الناخس والراكب جميعاً ودم الآمر بالنخس هدر ولو سارت عن موضعها ثم نفحت من فور النخس فالضمان على الناخس دون الراكب ولو لم تسر ونفحت الناخس ورجلاً آخر وقتلتهما فدية الأجنبي على الناخس والراكب ونصف دية الناخس على الراكب ولو لم يوقفها الراكب على الطريق ولكن حرنت فوقفت فنخسها هو وغيره لتسير فنفحت إنساناً فلا شيء عليهما. وفيه أيضاً : رجل اكترى من آخر دابة ليذهب عليها في حاجة له فاتبعه صاحبها فله أن يسوقها فإن وقف الراكب في الطريق على أهل مجلس فحرنت فنخسها صاحب الدابة أو ضربها أو ساقها فنفحت الدابة وهي واقفة فقتلت إنساناً فالضمان على الراكب والسائق جميعاً. وفيه أيضاً : ركب دابة بأمر أبيه ثم إن الصبي الراكب أمر صبياً فنخسها فالقول فيه إذا كان مأذوناً كالقول في الكبير وإن كان لم يؤذن له في ذلك فأمر صبياً حتى نخسها فسارت ونفحت من النخسة فعلى الناخس الضمان ولا شيء على الراكب وإن أمر بذلك ووطئت إنساناً فقتلته وكان سيرها من النخسة فالدية على عاقلة الناخس ولا يرجعون بذلك على عاقلة الراكب. وفيه أيضاً : رجل ركب دابة رجل قد أوقفها ربها في الطريق وربها غائب فأمر رب الدابة رجلاً حتى نخسها فنفحت رجلاً أو نفحت الآمر فديته على الناخس وإن كان الأمر أوقفها في الطريق ثم أمر رجلاً حتى نخسها فقتلت رجلاً فديته على الأمر والناخس نصفين. رجل أذن رجلاً أن يدخل داره وهو راكب فدخلها راكباً فوطئت دابته على شيء كان ضامناً له إن كان سائقاً أو قائداً فلا ضمان. أدخل بعيراً برحله فوقع عليه المتعلم فقتله فقد اختلف المشايخ فمنهم من قال لا ضمان على صاحب المتعلم وقال بعضهم إن أدخل صاحب المتعلم بغير إذن صاحب الدار فعليه الضمان وإن كان دخلها بإذنه فلا ضمان وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث وعليه الفتوى وفي فتاوى الخلاصة ولو كان البعير غير متعلم فحكمه حكم متعلم . وفي الفتاوى : ربط حماره في أرضه ليأكل علفاً فجاء حمار رجل فعقره فجعله معيوباً عيباً فاحشاً قال : لا يرجع بنقصان العيب على صاحب الحمار . قلت قال القاضي بديع الدين : إن كان صاحبه معه يضمن وإلا فلا يضمن قال رحمه الله : (وما ضمنه الراكب ضمن السائق والقائد) أي كل شيء يضمنه الراكب يضمنان لأنهما سببان كالراكب في غير الإيطاء فيجب عليهما الضمان بالتعدي فيه كالراكب. وقوله وما ضمنه الراكب ضمنه السائق والقائد يطرد وينعكس في الصحيح. وذكر القدوري أن السائق يضمن النفحة بالرجل لأنه بمرأى عينه فيمكنه الاحتراز عنها مع السير وغائبة عن بصر الراكب والقائد فلا يمكنهما الاحتراز عنها بخلاف الكدم والصدم. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : يضمنون كلهم النفحة والحجة عليه ما ذكرنا . وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام) (الرجل) جبار» ومعناه النفحة بالرجل.

قال رحمه الله : (وعلى الراكب الكفارة لا (عليهما أي لا على السائق والقائد ومراده في الإيطاء لأن الراكب مباشر فيه لأن التلف بثقله وثقل دابته تبع فإن سير الدابة مضاف إليه العلة وهما مسببان لأنه لا يتصل منهما شيء بالمحل وكذلك الراكب في غير الإيطاء والكفارة حكم المباشرة لا حكم التسبب . وكذا يتعلق بالإيطاء في حق الراكب حرمان الميراث والوصية دون السائق والقائد لأنه يختص بالمباشر. ولو كان سائق وراكب قيل لا يضمن السائق ما فعلت الدابة لأن الراكب مباشر فيه كما ذكرنا والسائق مسبب والإضافة إلى المباشرة أولى وقيل : الضمان عليهما لأن كل ذلك الضمان ألا ترى أن محمداً ذكر في الأصل أن الراكب إذا أمر إنساناً فنخس المأمور الدابة ووطئت إنساناً كان الضمان عليهما فاشتركا في الضمان والناخس سائق والآمر راكب فتبين بهذا أنهما مستويان والصحيح الأول لما ذكرنا والجواب عما ذكر في الأصل أن السبب إنما يضمن مع المباشرة إذا كان السبب شيئاً لا يعمل بانفراده في الإتلاف كالحفر مع الإلقاء فإن الحفر لا يعمل شيئاً بدون الإلقاء. وأما إذا كان السبب يعمل بانفراده في الإتلاف فيشتركان وهذا منه . الأصل يقول : رجل قاد قطاراً من الإبل في طريق المسلمين فما وطئ أول القطار وآخره مالاً أو رجلاً فقتله فالقائد ضامن ولا كفارة وإن كان معه سائق يسوق الإبل إلا أنه تارة يتقدم وتارة يتأخر فإنهما يشتركان في الضمان وإلا كان معهما ثالث يسوق الإبل وسط القطار فما أصاب بما خلف هذا الذي في وسط القطار أو مما قبله فضمان ذلك عليهم أثلاثاً يريد به إذا كان هذا الذي يمشي في وسط القطار ولا يمشي في جانب القطار ولا يأخذ بزمام بعير يقود ما خلفه لأنه سائق لوسط القطار فيكون سائقاً لكل بحكم اتصال الأزمة فأما إذا كان الذي في وسط القطار آخذاً بزمام يقود ما خلفه ولا يسوق ما قبله فما أصاب مما خلف هذا الذي في هذا القطار فضمان ذلك على القائد الأول ولا شيء فيه على هذا الذي في وسط القطار لأنه ليس بقائد لما قبله ولا سائق حتى لو كان سائقاً له يشارك الأول في الضمان؛ كذا المغني. وفي الينابيع : وإن كان السائق في وسط القطار فما أصاب من خلفه أو بين يديه فهو عليهما. وإن كانوا ثلاثة نفر أحدهم في مقدم القطار والآخر في مؤخر القطار والثالث في وسط القطار ; فإن كان الذي في الوسط والمؤخر يسوقان والمقدم يقود القطار فما عطب بما أمام الذي في الوسط فذلك كله على القائد وما تلف مما هو خلفه فهو كله على القائد ولا شيء على المؤخر إلا أن يكون سائقاً وإن كانوا يسوقون فالضمان عليهم جميعاً. السغناقي : ولو كان الرجل راكباً وسط القطار على بعيره ولا يسوق منها شيئاً لم يضمن ما تعيب الإبل التي بين يديه لأنه ليس بسائق لما بين يديه وهو معهم في الضمان مما أصاب البعير الذي عليه أو ما خلفه. وقال بعض المتأخرين : هذا الذي ذكر إذا كان زمام ما خلفه بيده يقوده وأما إذا كان نائماً على بعيره أو قاعداً فلا ضمان عليه في ذلك فهو في حق ما خلفه بمنزلة في هو
Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury upon Them, etc. What the rider is liable for, the driver and the leader are also liable for, and upon the rider is the expiation, not upon them. And if a boy collided in the road and ordered a man to prod his animal, and he prodded it and it killed a man, the blood money of the stranger is upon the proder and the rider together, and the blood of the orderer of the prodding is wasted. And if it walked from its place then kicked from the force of the prodding, the liability is upon the proder, not the rider. And if it did not walk and kicked the proder and another man and killed them, the blood money of the stranger is upon the proder and the rider, and half the blood money of the proder is upon the rider. And if the rider did not stop it on the road but it became restive and stopped, and he and another prodded it to make it walk, and it kicked a person, then nothing is upon them. And also in it: A man hired from another an animal to go on it for his need, and its owner followed him, so he may drive it. If the rider stopped in the road at a gathering and the animal became restive, and the owner prodded it or struck it or drove it and the animal, while standing, kicked and killed a person, the liability is upon the rider and the driver together. And also in it: A boy rode an animal by his father's command, then the boy rider ordered another boy to prod it, the ruling therein regarding a minor who is permitted is like that of an adult. And if he was not permitted to do that, and he ordered a boy until he prodded it, and it walked and kicked from the prodding, then upon the proder is the liability, and nothing upon the rider. And if he ordered that and it trampled a person and killed him, and its walking was from the prodding, then the blood money is upon the acquirer (aafilah) of the proder, and they do not have recourse thereby upon the acquirer of the rider. And also in it: A man rode an animal of a man who had stopped it on the road while its owner was absent, and the owner of the animal ordered a man until he prodded it, and it kicked a man or kicked the orderer, then his blood money is upon the proder. And if the orderer had stopped it on the road then ordered a man until he prodded it and it killed a man, then his blood money is upon the orderer and the proder in halves. A man permitted a man to enter his house while he was riding, and he entered riding, and his animal trampled something; he is liable for it. If he was a driver or leader, there is no liability. He brought in a camel with its saddle, and a trainee fell on it and was killed. The masters differed: some said there is no liability on the owner of the trainee; some said if the owner of the trainee entered without permission of the house owner, he is liable; and if he entered with his permission, there is no liability. And the jurist Abu al-Layth adopted this, and upon it is the fatwa. In Fatawa al-Khulasa: and if the camel was not trained, its ruling is like that of a trained one. And in al-Fatawa: He tied his donkey in his land to eat fodder; then a man's donkey came and injured it, making it severely defective. He said: He does not seek recourse for the decrease in defect from the owner of the donkey. I (the commentator) said: al-Qadi Badi' al-Din said: If its owner was with it, he is liable; otherwise, he is not liable. He (the author) said: (And what the rider is liable for, the driver and the leader are liable for) i.e., everything the rider is liable for, they are liable for because they are causes like the rider in non-trampling, so liability is incumbent upon them due to transgression in it like the rider. And his statement: 'And what the rider is liable for, the driver and the leader are liable for' holds true and applies inversely in the correct view. And al-Quduri mentioned that the driver is liable for the kick with the leg because it is in his sight, so he can avoid it with the walking, while it is hidden from the sight of the rider and the leader, so they cannot avoid it, unlike the bite and the bump. And al-Shafi'i said: They are all liable for the kick. The proof against him is what we mentioned. And his statement, peace be upon him: 'The leg is free (jarar)' means the kick with the leg (is exempt). He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And upon the rider is the expiation, not upon them) i.e., not upon the driver and the leader. His intent is in trampling because the rider is a direct cause in it because the destruction is by his weight and the weight of his animal as a consequence. For the movement of the animal is attributed to him as the cause, and they are indirect causes because nothing from them connects to the place. And similarly, the rider in non-trampling. And expiation is the ruling of direct causation, not the ruling of indirect causation. And similarly, in trampling, regarding the rider, the deprivation of inheritance and bequest is attached, not the driver and the leader, because it is specific to the direct cause. And if there were a driver and a rider, it is said: the driver is not liable for what the animal did because the rider is a direct cause in it as we mentioned, and the driver is an indirect cause, and attribution is to the direct cause first. And it is said: liability is upon both because all that is liability; do you not see that Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl that if the rider ordered a man and the ordered person prodded the animal and it trampled a person, liability is upon both? So they shared in liability, and the proder is a driver and the orderer is a rider. Thus it becomes clear that they are equal. And the correct is the first based on what we mentioned. And the answer to what was mentioned in al-Asl is that the indirect cause (sabab) is liable along with the direct cause only when the indirect cause is something that does not work by itself in causing destruction, like digging with throwing: the digging does nothing without throwing. But if the indirect cause works by itself in causing destruction, then they share. And this is from it. Al-Asl says: A man led a caravan of camels on the path of Muslims, and what the first of the caravan and its last trampled of property or a person and killed him, the leader is liable and there is no expiation. And if there was with him a driver driving the camels, except that he sometimes goes ahead and sometimes lags behind, then they share in liability. And if there was also a third with them driving the camels in the middle of the caravan, then what is hit by what is behind this one in the middle of the caravan or what is before it, the liability for that is upon them in thirds. He intends this if this one who walks in the middle of the caravan does not walk on the side of the caravan and does not take the halter of a camel leading what is behind him, because he is a driver of the middle of the caravan, so he becomes a driver of each due to the connection of the halters. But if the one in the middle of the caravan is taking the halter, leading what is behind him and not driving what is before him, then what is hit from what is behind this one in this caravan, the liability for that is upon the first leader only, and nothing is upon this one in the middle of the caravan because he is not a leader of what is before him nor a driver until if he were a driver for it, he would share with the first in liability; thus in al-Mughni. And in al-Yanabi': If the driver is in the middle of the caravan, then what is hit from behind him or in front of him, it is upon both. And if they were three persons, one in the front of the caravan, another in the back of the caravan, and the third in the middle of the caravan; if the one in the middle and the back are driving and the front is leading the caravan, then what gets damaged from what is in front of the one in the middle, that all is upon the leader, and what is destroyed from what is behind him, it is all upon the leader, and nothing is upon the back unless he was a driver. And if they were all driving, the liability is upon all of them. Al-Sighnaqi said: If a man was riding in the middle of the caravan on his camel and does not drive anything from it, he is not liable for what defective happens to the camels that are in front of him because he is not a driver of what is in front of him, and he is with them in liability for what hits the camel he is upon or what is behind him. And some later scholars said: This is what was mentioned if the halter of what is behind him is in his hand, leading it. But if he was sleeping on his camel or sitting, there is no liability upon him in that; regarding what is behind him, he is like... [cut off]
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqaiq, discussing the chapter of diyat (blood money) specifically regarding injuries caused by animals and the liability of the rider, driver, and leader. The author begins by quoting the matn: 'What the rider is liable for, the driver and the leader are also liable for.' He then explains that this means they share liability because both are causes (sabab) like the rider, except in cases of trampling (i'ta') where the rider is the direct cause (mubashir) due to his weight, while the driver and leader are indirect causes (musabbib). This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh: direct causation imposes full responsibility, including kaffarah (expiation) and deprivation of inheritance/bequest, while indirect causation does not. The commentary then details multiple scenarios to illustrate these principles. For example, if someone prods an animal and it kicks or tramples someone, liability shifts based on whether the animal moved from the prodding or not. The passage also records a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that all three (rider, driver, leader) are equally liable for the animal's kick (nafha). The Hanafi response relies on a hadith 'al-rijalu jubbar' (the leg is free), meaning the kick with the leg is not subject to diyah, and on the reasoning that the driver, who can see the kick and avoid it, bears liability, whereas the rider and leader cannot see it. The commentary further explores direct versus indirect causation through an analogy: digging a pit (cause that does not work alone) and throwing someone in (direct act) versus a cause that works alone (like the rider's weight in trampling). Finally, the passage presents a detailed case from Al-Asl about a caravan of camels, illustrating how liability is distributed among a leader, driver, and those in different positions, emphasizing that liability follows control and direct action. The overall teaching is that Hanafi law carefully distinguishes between direct and indirect contributions to harm, and that liability and expiation are aligned with the degree of directness.

Key Terms
الراكب (al-raakib): The person riding the animal; in this text, the rider is considered a direct cause (mubashir) for harm caused by trampling (i'ta') due to his weight.
السائق (al-saa'iq): The person driving the animal from behind; in this text, the driver is liable for kicks (nafha) because he can see the animal's hind legs.
القائد (al-qa'id): The person leading the animal from the front; usually treated similarly to the rider in terms of inability to see kicks, so not liable for nafha.
النفحة (al-nafha): A kick with the hind legs of an animal; in this text, distinguished from trampling (i'ta') and bites (kadm) and bumps (sadm).
الإيطاء (al-i'ta'): Trampling or stepping on something/someone; the rider is directly liable for this because the harm results from his weight on the animal.
المباشرة (al-mubashara): Direct causation; the harm is directly attributed to the person's action (e.g., the rider's weight causing trampling).
التسبب (al-tasabbub): Indirect causation; the person's action is a cause but not the immediate physical act (e.g., driver's driving leading to trampling).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentary, when a ridden animal kicks someone (nafha), who is liable?
A) The rider only
B) The driver only
C) The rider and driver jointly
D) The rider, driver, and leader jointly

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, liability for animal harm depends on direct causation: the rider bears full responsibility for trampling, but not for kicks, while the driver bears liability for kicks.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
12 min
Liability for Animal-Caused Deaths: Direct vs. Indirect Causation
Explaining liability rules for riders, drivers, and leaders in caravan accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a direct agent (mubashir) and an indirect cause (musabbib) in animal-caused deaths and apply the Hanafi rules for diyah and kaffarah accordingly.

Core Idea
Expiation attaches only to the direct actor (rider), not to the leader or driver who merely set the animal in motion.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the chapter of injuries by animals. It clarifies the critical distinction between direct and indirect causation in liability for death. The Hanafi school holds that the rider bears the expiation (kaffarah) because the death is directly caused by his weight, while the driver and leader are only indirectly liable for monetary compensation (diyah). The text then explores multiple scenarios involving caravans, multiple drivers, and collisions, consistently applying this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the rule: the rider (rakib) must pay kaffarah for trampling deaths, while the driver (sa'iq) and leader (qa'id) do not.
The rider is mubashir (direct), driver/leader are musabbib (indirect).
2
Analyze the reasoning
Explain why: the death is caused by the rider's weight and the animal's weight as a subsidiary; the driver/leader only provide movement but do not contact the victim.
Kaffarah is a ruling of direct agency, not indirect causation.
3
Examine the exception
When an indirect cause works alone to cause destruction (e.g., digging a hole that someone falls into without a direct actor), liability is shared. The goading case from Muhammad's al-Asl is discussed.
The text resolves that the indirect cause must work independently to share liability.
4
Apply to caravan scenarios
Work through the roles: leader of caravan always liable for diyah (no kaffarah); driver shares diyah if present; riders in front of the trampling camel are liable; pillion rider shares kaffarah with the rider.
Sleeping rider on camel is not liable unless holding the rein.
5
Compare the collision dispute
Two horsemen/pedestrians collide and die: Hanafi says each 'aqila pays full diyah, Zufar/Shafi'i say half. Understand the basis: full direct causation vs. shared causation.
The thamarah al-khilaf: full vs. half diyah.

على هذا الذي في وسط القطار لأنه ليس بقائد لما قبله ولا سائق حتى لو كان سائقاً له يشارك الأول في الضمان؛ كذا المغني. وفي الينابيع : وإن كان السائق في وسط القطار فما أصاب من خلفه أو بين يديه فهو عليهما. وإن كانوا ثلاثة نفر أحدهم في مقدم القطار والآخر في مؤخر القطار والثالث في وسط القطار ؛ فإن كان الذي في الوسط والمؤخر يسوقان والمقدم يقود القطار فما عطب بما أمام الذي في الوسط فذلك كله على القائد، وما تلف مما هو خلفه فهو كله على القائد ولا شيء على المؤخر إلا أن يكون سائقاً، وإن كانوا يسوقون فالضمان عليهم جميعاً. السغناقي : ولو كان الرجل راكباً وسط القطار على بعيره ولا يسوق منها شيئاً لم يضمن ما تعيب الإبل التي بين يديه لأنه ليس بسائق لما بين يديه وهو معهم في الضمان مما أصاب البعير الذي عليه أو ما خلفه. وقال بعض المتأخرين : هذا الذي ذكر إذا كان زمام ما خلفه بيده يقوده، وأما إذا كان نائماً على بعيره أو قاعداً فلا ضمان عليه في ذلك فهو في حق ما خلفه بمنزلة
في
هو

فارسان أو ماشيان فماتا ضمن عاقلة كل دية الآخر ولو ساق دابة فوقع السرج على
المتاع الموضوع على البعير الظهيرية ولو أن رجلاً يقود قطاراً وآخر من خلف القطار يسوقه وعلى الإبل في المحال نيام أو غير نيام فوطئ بعير منها إنسان فقتله فالدية على عاقلة القائد والسائق والراكبين الذين قدام البعير على عواقلهم على عدد رؤوسهم، والكفارة على راكب البعير الذي وطئ خاصة لأنه بمنزلة المباشر . قال في المنتقى : إذا قاد الرجل قطاراً وخلفه سائق وأمامه راكب فوطئ الراكب إنساناً فالدية عليهم أثلاثاً، وكذلك إذا وطئ عير مما خلف الراكب إنساناً، وإن كان وطئ بغير إمام فهو على القائد والسائق نصفين ولا شيء على الراكب. وذكر في المنتقى مسألة القطار بعد هذا في صورة أخرى وأوجب الضمان على القائد وعلى من كان قدام البعير الذي أوطأ من الركبان قال : وليس على من خلفه من الركبان شيء إلا أن يكون إنساناً مؤجراً ويسوق فيكون عليه وعلى السائق الذي خلفه يشتركون جميعاً فيه . الخانية : رجل يقود دابة فسقط شيء مما يحمل على الإبل على إنسان أو سقط الدابة أو سرج لجامها على أنسان فقتله أو سقط ذلك في الطريق فعثر به إنسان ومات يضمن القائد، وإن كان معه سائق كان الضمان عليهما القاضي : وسئل أيضاً عن صاحب زرع سلم الحمار إلى المزارع فربط الدابة عليه وشد الحمار في الدالية بأمره فانقطع خيط من خيوطها فوقع الحمار في حفرة الدالية فعطب الحمار هل يجب الضمان على المزارع؟ فقال: لا. قال محمد في الجامع الصغير : رجل قاد قطاراً في طريق المسلمين ولم يعلم به فأصاب ذلك البعير إنساناً فضمانه على القائد دون الرابط، وإن كان كل منهما سبباً للإتلاف فهل يرجع على عاقلة الرابط ؟ قال : لا يرجع وإن لم يعلم. ولم يفصل محمد في الجامع الصغير بين ما إذا ربط البعير بالقطار والقطار يسير وفي بعض كتب النوادر: إن القطار إن كان حالة الربط فقادها القائد بعد الربط لا يرجع القائد على عاقلة الرابط، علم القائد بربطه أو لم يعلم، فإن كان حالة الربط فالقائد يرجع على عاقلة الرابط إذا لم يعلم بربطه . وفي المنتقى : وإذا
القطار يسير
يسير
سار الرجل على دابة وخلفه رديف وخلف الدابة سائق وأمامها قائد فوطئت إنساناً فالدية عليهم أرباعاً وعلى الراكب والرديف الكفارة، وإذا سار الرجل على دابته في الطريق فعثرت بحجر وضعه رجل أو بد كأن بناه رجل أو بما صبه رجل فوقعت على إنسان وأتلفته فالضمان على الذي وضع الحجر وبنى الدكان وصب الماء لأنه مسبب الإتلاف وهو متعد في هذا السبب ولا ضمان على الراكب وفي الكفارة إذا أرسل كلباً أو دابة أو طيراً فأصاب في فوره شيئاً ضمن في الدابة دون الكلب والطير . وفي الصغرى الطحاوي: وعن أبي يوسف أنه يضمن الكل؛ كذا في الجامع الصغير .
قال
رحمه الله : ولو) اصطدم فارسان أو ماشيان فماتا ضمن عاقلة كل دية الآخر) وقال زفر والشافعي رحمه الله تعالى : يجب على عاقلة كل واحد نصف دية الآخر، ذلك ، وروي
عن
Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury upon Them and Other Matters... (And on the rider is the expiation, not on both, i.e., not on the driver and leader. His intent is regarding trampling, because the rider is a direct agent in it because the destruction is by his weight and the weight of his animal is subsidiary. The movement of the animal is attributed to him as the cause, and they are indirect causes because nothing from them reaches the place. Likewise the rider in non-trampling. And expiation is the ruling of direct agency, not the ruling of indirect causation. Likewise, trampling in relation to the rider entails deprivation of inheritance and bequest, unlike the driver and leader, because it is specific to the direct agent. If there is a driver and a rider, it is said: the driver is not liable for what the animal did because the rider is the direct agent as we mentioned, and the driver is an indirect cause, and attribution to direct agency is preferable. And it is said: liability is on both because all that is liability. Do you not see that Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl that if a rider ordered someone, and that ordered person goaded the animal, and it trampled a person, liability was on both, so they share liability. The goader is a driver and the orderer is a rider, so it becomes clear that they are equal. The correct is the first for what we mentioned. The response to what was mentioned in al-Asl is that an indirect cause is only liable together with direct agency if the cause is something that does not work by itself in destruction, like digging with throwing, for digging does nothing without throwing. But if the cause works by itself in destruction, they share. And this is from that. Al-Asl says: A man led a caravan of camels in the Muslims' path, and what was trampled by the first and last of the caravan, whether property or a person, killing him, the leader is liable and no expiation. If there is with him a driver driving the camels, but sometimes he goes ahead and sometimes behind, then they share the liability. And if there is a third with them driving the camels in the middle of the caravan, then what is hit by what is behind this one in the middle of the caravan or what is in front of him, the liability is on them in thirds. This is intended if this one walks in the middle of the caravan and does not walk on the side of the caravan and does not take the rein of a camel leading what is behind him, because he is the driver of the middle of the caravan, so he is a driver for all by virtue of connection of the reins. But if the one in the middle of the caravan takes the rein leading what is behind him and does not drive what is in front of him, then what is hit by what is behind this one in this caravan, the liability is on the first leader and nothing on this one in the middle of the caravan because he is neither a leader for what is in front nor a driver, unless he were a driver for it, then he would share with the first in liability. This is from al-Mughni. In al-Yanabi': If the driver is in the middle of the caravan, then what is hit from behind or in front, it is on both. If they are three persons, one in front of the caravan, another at the back of the caravan, and the third in the middle of the caravan: if the one in the middle and the back drive and the front leads the caravan, then what is damaged in front of the one in the middle is all on the leader, and what is destroyed behind him is all on the leader, and nothing on the back one unless he is a driver. If they all drive, then liability is on all of them. al-Sighnaqi: If a man rides in the middle of the caravan on his camel and does not drive any of them, he is not liable for what the camels in front of him injure because he is not a driver for what is in front, and he is with them in liability for what the camel he is on or what is behind it hits. Some later scholars said: This is stated when the rein of what is behind him is in his hand driving it. But if he is sleeping on his camel or sitting, there is no liability on him for that. So he is in relation to what is behind him like... Two horsemen or two pedestrians die, the 'aqila of each guarantees the blood money of the other. If he drives an animal and the saddle falls on the baggage placed on the baggage camel... If a man leads a caravan and another drives from behind the caravan, and on the camels are riders, sleeping or not, and a camel from them tramples a person and kills him, the blood money is on the 'aqila of the leader, the driver, and the riders who are in front of that camel, on their 'aqilas per head. The expiation is specifically on the rider of the camel that trampled because he is like the direct agent. Al-Muntaqa: If a man leads a caravan, behind him a driver, and in front of him a rider, and the rider tramples a person, the blood money is on them in thirds. Similarly, if the rider tramples a person from what is behind him, it is on them in thirds. But if he tramples without an imam (rider?), then it is on the leader and driver halves, and nothing on the rider. And al-Muntaqa mentioned the case of the caravan after this in another form and obligated liability on the leader and on those of the riders who are in front of the camel that trampled. He said: Nothing on the riders behind him unless there is a hired man who drives, then it is on him and on the driver behind him, they all share in it. al-Khaniyya: A man leads a beast, and something from the load on the camel falls on a person, or the animal itself falls, or its saddle or bridle falls on a person and kills him, or it falls on the road and a person trips on it and dies, the leader is liable. If there is a driver with him, the liability is on both. al-Qadi: He was asked about a farmer who handed the donkey to the cultivator, and he tied the animal to it and tightened the donkey on the pulley by his order, and a thread from its strings broke, so the donkey fell into the hole of the pulley and died. Is liability necessary on the cultivator? He said: No. Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man led a caravan in the Muslims' path and did not know about it, and that camel hit a person, the liability is on the leader, not the one who tied. Even if each is a cause of destruction, can he claim against the 'aqila of the tier? He said: He cannot claim, even if he did not know. And Muhammad did not differentiate in al-Jami' al-Saghir between whether the camel was tied to the caravan while the caravan was moving. And in some books of al-Nawadir: If the caravan was stationary at the time of tying, and the leader led it after tying, the leader cannot claim against the 'aqila of the tier, whether the leader knew of his tying or not. If it was moving at the time of tying, then the leader can claim against the 'aqila of the tier if he did not know of his tying. And al-Muntaqa: When a man rides on an animal with a pillion rider behind him, behind the animal a driver, and in front a leader, and it tramples a person, the blood money is on them in fourths, and on the rider and pillion rider is the expiation. When a man rides his animal on the road and it trips over a stone placed by a man, or a shop built by a man, or water spilled by a man, and it falls on a person and destroys him, the liability is on the one who placed the stone, built the shop, and poured the water, because he is the indirect cause of destruction and he is transgressing in this cause. There is no liability on the rider. Regarding expiation: if he releases a dog, an animal, or a bird, and it hits something immediately, he is liable in the case of the animal, but not in the case of the dog and bird. In al-Sughra al-Tahawi: From Abu Yusuf that he makes liable all. This is in al-Jami' al-Saghir. He (the author) said: (If) two horsemen or two pedestrians collide and die, the 'aqila of each guarantees the blood money of the other. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: On the 'aqila of each is half the blood money of the other. That is narrated from...
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the chapter of injuries by animals. It begins by affirming the key Hanafi principle: the rider (direct actor) bears expiation (kaffarah) for a trampling death, while the driver and leader (indirect causes) do not. The reasoning is that destruction occurs from the rider's weight and the animal's weight as a subsidiary; the driver and leader only provide impetus but do not directly contact the victim. This distinction carries over to inheritance and bequest exclusion—only the direct actor is deprived. The text then addresses a dispute: some say the driver shares liability, citing a case from Muhammad al-Shaybani's al-Asl where a goaded animal leads to shared liability. The commentary resolves this by distinguishing causes that work independently—if the cause alone can cause harm, liability is shared; if not, only the direct actor is liable. It then presents extensive scenarios from various sources (al-Mughni, al-Yanabi', al-Sighnaqi, al-Muntaqa, al-Khaniyya, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Nawadir) detailing how liability and expiation are distributed among leaders, drivers, and riders in caravan accidents based on their roles. For example, a leader is always liable but bears no expiation; a driver shares liability but not expiation. When multiple persons are involved (e.g., rider, driver, leader, pillion rider), shares are apportioned. Specific cases include a sleeping rider (no liability), a hired driver (shared liability), and stumbling over an obstacle (only the one who placed it liable). The passage concludes with a ruling on collision of two horsemen/pedestrians: each 'aqila pays full diyah for the other—a Hanafi position opposed by Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who say half each. The Hanafi view is based on full direct causation by each, whereas the opposing view apportions causation. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with further proof.

Key Terms
إيطاء (I'ṭā'): Trampling or causing an animal to step on someone, resulting in injury or death.
مباشر (Mubāshir): Direct agent who causes harm by physical contact (e.g., the rider whose weight crushes the victim).
مسبب (Musabbib): Indirect cause who sets in motion events leading to harm without direct contact (e.g., driver, leader).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a caravan accident where a camel tramples a person to death, who bears the expiation (kaffarah)?
A) The leader of the caravan
B) The driver of the camel
C) The rider on the camel that did the trampling
D) All three share it equally

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly distinguishes direct and indirect causation: expiation (kaffarah) is only for the direct actor (mubashir), while monetary liability (diyah) can be on indirect causes (musabbib) and their 'aqilas.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
12 min
Liability for Animal-Caused Injuries with Multiple Persons
Rulings on liability of leaders, drivers, and riders for animal injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the liable parties in multi-person animal-caused injury scenarios and apply the Hanafi rules for distributing blood-money among 'aqilas.

Core Idea
Liability follows causation: direct agents expiate; indirect causers share blood-money proportionally.

The Hanafi school provides detailed rules when multiple persons are involved in an animal-caused injury. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents various scenarios: from two horsemen colliding to a caravan with leader, driver, and riders. The key legal concepts are the direct agent (mubāshir) and causing agent (mutasabbib), with blood-money paid by the 'aqila. You will learn how liability is apportioned among those who lead, drive, ride, or tie animals, and the conditions for recourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of scenario
Determine whether the case is a collision between two independent animals, a caravan with multiple handlers, an obstacle on the road, or another situation. Each type has a specific rule.
Scenarios include: two horsemen/pedestrians, saddle falling, caravan with leader/driver/rider, rope tying, obstacle (stone/shop/water), sending dog/bird.
2
Distinguish direct agent from indirect cause
The rider or the one whose animal directly tramples is the mubāshir (direct agent). The leader, driver, or obstacle placer is the mutasabbib (indirect cause). The mubāshir bears expiation; the mutasabbib shares blood-money.
Expiation (kaffara) is only on the direct agent. Blood-money (diya) is on the 'aqila of all liable parties.
3
Apply the distribution rule for caravans
For a caravan with leader, driver, and riders, blood-money is divided per capita among those whose roles contributed to the injury. The text says 'on their 'aqilas according to the number of their heads.' The rider in front is counted; the rider behind may not be liable unless he drove.
Example: leader, driver, and one rider: blood-money in thirds. Leader and driver only: halves.
4
Check for transgression (ta'addi)
An indirect cause is only liable if there was transgression. For example, placing an obstacle in the road is transgression. If there was no transgression (e.g., the animal was tied properly), the indirect cause may not be liable.
The case of the crop owner who handed the donkey to the sharecropper: no liability because no transgression.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
The passage ends with a difference: Abu Yusuf holds that one who sends a dog, beast, or bird is liable for any immediate harm. The earlier view distinguished between beasts (liable) and dogs/birds (not liable). This affects the ruling.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi may vary by region.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فارسان أو ماشيان فماتا ضمن عاقلة كل دية الآخر ولو ساق دابة فوقع السرج على المتاع الموضوع على البعير الظهيرية ولو أن رجلاً يقود قطاراً وآخر من خلف القطار يسوقه وعلى الإبل في المحال نيام أو غير نيام فوطئ بعير منها إنسان فقتله فالدية على عاقلة القائد والسائق والراكبين الذين قدام البعير على عواقلهم على عدد رؤوسهم، والكفارة على راكب البعير الذي وطئ خاصة لأنه بمنزلة المباشر . قال في المنتقى : إذا قاد الرجل قطاراً وخلفه سائق وأمامه راكب فوطئ الراكب إنساناً فالدية عليهم أثلاثاً، وكذلك إذا وطئ عير مما خلف الراكب إنساناً، وإن كان وطئ بغير إمام فهو على القائد والسائق نصفين ولا شيء على الراكب. وذكر في المنتقى مسألة القطار بعد هذا في صورة أخرى وأوجب الضمان على القائد وعلى من كان قدام البعير الذي أوطأ من الركبان قال : وليس على من خلفه من الركبان شيء إلا أن يكون إنساناً مؤجراً ويسوق فيكون عليه وعلى السائق الذي خلفه يشتركون جميعاً فيه . الخانية : رجل يقود دابة فسقط شيء مما يحمل على الإبل على إنسان أو سقط الدابة أو سرج لجامها على أنسان فقتله أو سقط ذلك في الطريق فعثر به إنسان ومات يضمن القائد، وإن كان معه سائق كان الضمان عليهما القاضي : وسئل أيضاً عن صاحب زرع سلم الحمار إلى المزارع فربط الدابة عليه وشد الحمار في الدالية بأمره فانقطع خيط من خيوطها فوقع الحمار في حفرة الدالية فعطب الحمار هل يجب الضمان على المزارع؟ فقال: لا. قال محمد في الجامع الصغير : رجل قاد قطاراً في طريق المسلمين ولم يعلم به فأصاب ذلك البعير إنساناً فضمانه على القائد دون الرابط، وإن كان كل منهما سبباً للإتلاف فهل يرجع على عاقلة الرابط ؟ قال : لا يرجع وإن لم يعلم. ولم يفصل محمد في الجامع الصغير بين ما إذا ربط البعير بالقطار والقطار يسير وفي بعض كتب النوادر: إن القطار إن كان حالة الربط فقادها القائد بعد الربط لا يرجع القائد على عاقلة الرابط، علم القائد بربطه أو لم يعلم، فإن كان حالة الربط فالقائد يرجع على عاقلة الرابط إذا لم يعلم بربطه . وفي المنتقى : وإذا سار الرجل على دابة وخلفه رديف وخلف الدابة سائق وأمامها قائد فوطئت إنساناً فالدية عليهم أرباعاً وعلى الراكب والرديف الكفارة، وإذا سار الرجل على دابته في الطريق فعثرت بحجر وضعه رجل أو بد كأن بناه رجل أو بما صبه رجل فوقعت على إنسان وأتلفته فالضمان على الذي وضع الحجر وبنى الدكان وصب الماء لأنه مسبب الإتلاف وهو متعد في هذا السبب ولا ضمان على الراكب وفي الكفارة إذا أرسل كلباً أو دابة أو طيراً فأصاب في فوره شيئاً ضمن في الدابة دون الكلب والطير . وفي الصغرى الطحاوي: وعن أبي يوسف أنه يضمن الكل؛ كذا في الجامع الصغير .
Two horsemen or two pedestrians and they die, the 'āqila of each bears the blood-money of the other. If someone leads a beast and the saddle falls on the goods placed on the camel... If a man leads a caravan and another drives from behind the caravan, and on the camels in the saddlebags are sleeping or not sleeping, and a camel from them tramples a person and kills him, the blood-money is on the 'āqila of the leader, the driver, and the riders who are in front of the camel, on their 'āqilas according to the number of their heads; the expiation is specifically on the rider of the camel that trampled because he is like the direct agent. In al-Muntaqa: If a man leads a caravan, with a driver behind and a rider in front, and the rider tramples a person, the blood-money is on them in thirds. Similarly, if a camel from behind the rider tramples a person, and if the trampling is without a rein, it is on the leader and driver half each and nothing on the rider. In al-Khāniyya: A man leads a beast and something from the load on the camel falls on a person, or the beast or its saddle or bridle falls on a person and kills him, or that falls in the road and a person trips over it and dies - the leader is liable; if there is a driver with him, liability is on both. Al-Qādī was asked about a crop owner who handed the donkey to the sharecropper, who tied the beast to it and fastened the donkey to the waterwheel by his order, then a string broke and the donkey fell into the waterwheel pit and died - is there liability on the sharecropper? He said: No. Muhammad said in al-Jāmi' al-Saghīr: A man led a caravan in the Muslims' road without knowing, and a camel from it struck a person - the liability is on the leader, not the one who tied it; if each was a cause, can he recourse to the 'āqila of the tier? He said: No recourse, even if he did not know. In al-Muntaqa: If a man rides a beast with a companion behind, a driver behind the beast, and a leader in front, and it tramples a person, the blood-money is on them in fourths, and the expiation on the rider and the companion. If a man rides his beast on the road and it trips on a stone placed by a man, or a shop built by a man, or water poured by a man, and falls on a person and kills him, the liability is on the one who placed the stone, built the shop, or poured the water because he is the causing agent and he transgressed in this cause; no liability on the rider. In al-Sughrā al-Taḥāwī: from Abū Yūsuf that he is liable for all; thus in al-Jāmi' al-Saghīr.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage collects multiple rulings on liability when animals cause injury and multiple persons are involved. The key principle is that liability falls on the 'āqila (the victim's kin group responsible for paying blood-money) of those who are directly or indirectly causing agents. The rulings distinguish between direct agents (mubāshir) like the rider of the trampling camel, who also owes expiation, and indirect causes (mutasabbib) like leaders, drivers, or those who place obstacles. In scenarios with a leader, driver, and riders, the blood-money is distributed according to their roles and the number of persons. The passage also discusses cases where a person ties an animal to a caravan without the leader's knowledge - the leader is primarily liable but cannot recourse to the tier's 'āqila unless the tying was done while the caravan was stationary. A final ruling from Abū Yūsuf extends liability to someone who sends a dog, beast, or bird that causes harm instantly - he holds the owner liable in all cases, contrary to the earlier distinction.

Key Terms
عاقلة (‘āqila): The male agnatic relatives of the perpetrator who collectively pay blood-money (diya) for unintentional killing or injury.
مباشر (mubāshir): The direct agent who physically commits the act causing harm, e.g., the rider of the trampling camel.
متسبب (mutasabbib): An indirect cause of harm, such as placing an obstacle or ordering an action, leading to liability if there is transgression (ta‘addī).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when a leader, driver, and rider are involved in a caravan and the rider's camel tramples a person, the blood-money is distributed:
A) Entirely on the rider's 'aqila
B) Equally among the leader, driver, and rider's 'aqilas
C) On the leader and driver only
D) On the rider's 'aqila for the full amount, with the leader and driver paying expiation

Key Takeaway
In multi-person animal injuries, Hanafi law distributes blood-money among all indirect causers (mutasabbib) per capita, while expiation falls solely on the direct agent (mubāshir).

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Full Blood Money in Mutual Collision: Hanafi vs Zufar/Shafi‘i
Hanafi ruling on collision death with response to Zufar and Shafi‘i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on blood money when two persons collide and both die, and explain why the other's act is fully causative.

Core Idea
In a permissible collision, each person's own movement is disregarded; the other's movement is fully liable.

When two persons collide and both die, who pays blood money? The Hanafi school holds that each party's 'āqila pays the full diya of the other, while Zufar and al-Shafi‘i argue for half. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the Hanafi position, rooted in the distinction between acts that are permissible absolutely versus those restricted by safety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine the exact scenario: two horsemen or pedestrians collide accidentally and both die. The question is whether each 'āqila pays full, half, or no diya.
The scenario is accidental, not intentional.
2
State the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi‘i argue that each party dies by his own act and the other's, so half the diya is attributed to each. They cite a report from ‘Ali and analogies to intentional collision and well-digging.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi dalil: each person's own movement (like walking) is mubāḥ absolutely towards himself, so it does not count as a cause of his own death. The other's similar movement is restricted by safety towards others, making it the sole cause of death. This is supported by a preferred report from ‘Ali imposing full diya.
The Hanafis distinguish between permissible acts (no liability on self) and prohibited acts (liability on self).
4
Compare analogies
The opponent's analogies involve prohibited acts (intentional wounding, digging a well without permission), so each is liable for his own share. In the collision case, the acts are permissible, so the analogy fails.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi law, each 'āqila pays the full diya of the other. Under the opposing view, each pays half. This difference affects the compensation received by the heirs.
The tarjih (preference) is for the Hanafi position due to the logical distinction and the stronger report from ‘Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ولو) اصطدم فارسان أو ماشيان فماتا ضمن عاقلة كل دية الآخر) وقال زفر والشافعي رحمه الله تعالى : يجب على عاقلة كل واحد نصف دية الآخر، ذلك ، وروي عن علي رضي الله عنه لأن كل واحد منهما مات بفعله وفعل صاحبه فيعتبر نصفه ويهدر النصف كما إذا كان الاصطدام عمداً وجرح كل واحد منهما نفسه وصاحبه أو حفرا على قارعة الطريق بئراً فانهدم عليهما أو وقفا فيه يجب على كل واحد منهما النصف فكذا هذا. ولنا أن قتل كل واحد منهما مضاف إلى فعل صاحبه لأن فعله في نفسه مباح كالمشي في الطريق فلا يعتبر في حق الضمان بالنسبة إلى نفسه لأنه مباح مطلقاً في حق نفسه، ولو اعتبر ذلك لوجب نصف الدية فيما إذا وقع في بئر في قارعة الطريق لأنه لولا مشيه وثقله في نفسه لما هوى في البئر، وفعل صاحبه وإن كان مباحاً لكنه مقيد بشرط السلامة في حق غيره فيكون سبباً للضمان عند وجود التلف، به وروي عن علي رضي الله عنه أنه أوجب كل الدية على عاقلة كل واحد منهما فتعارضت روایتان فرجحنا ما ذكرنا ويحتمل ما روي عنه أنه أوجب كل الدية على الخطأ توفيقاً بينهما. وأما ما استشهدا به من الاصطدام وجرح كل منهما نفسه وصاحبه وحفر البئر في الطريق فعلى كل واحد محظور مطلقاً فيعتبر في حق نفسه أيضاً فيكون قاتلاً لنفسه، وهذا الحكم الذي ذكرناه في العمد والخطأ في الحرين. ولو كانا عبدين هدر الدم لأن المولى فيه غير مختار للفداء، ولو كان أحدهما حراً والآخر عبداً يجب على عاقلة الحر قيمة العبد كلها في الخطأ ونصفها في العبد فيأخذها ورثة الحر المقتول ويبطل ما زاد عليه لعدم الخلف، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لأن قيمة العبد المقتول تجب على العاقلة على أصلهما لأنه ضمان الآدمي، وإذا تجاذب رجلان حبلاً فانقطع الحبل فسقطاً أو ماتاً ينظر؛ فإن وقعا على القفا لا تجب لهما دية لأن كل واحد منهما مات بقوة نفسه، وأن وقعا على الوجه وجب على عاقلة كل واحد منهما دية الآخر، وإن قطع إنسان الحبل بينهما فوقع كل واحد منهما على القفا فديتهما على عاقلة القاطع، وكذا على هذا سائر الضمانات وقد قدمنا شيئاً من هذا عند قوله «ولو ضرب بطن امرأته فراجعه قال في النهاية وفي تقييد الفارسين في الكتاب بقوله «وإذا اصطدم «الفارسان ليست زيادة قائدة فإن الحكم في اصطدام الماشيين وموتهما بذلك ؛ كذلك ذكره في المبسوط، سوی أن موت المصطدمين في الغالب إنما يكون في الفارسين ا.هـ. وقال في العناية آخذاً في النهاية : حكم الماشيين حكم الفارسين لكن لما كان موت المصطدمين غالباً في الفارسين خصهما بالذكر ا هـ. وقال في معراج الدراية : وكذا الحكم إذا اصطدم الماشيان والتقييد بالفارسين اتفاقي أو بحسب الغالب ا هـ . وتبعه الشارح العيني. أقول : عجيب من هؤلاء الشراح مثل هذه التعسفات مع كون وجه التقييد بالفارسين بينا لأن الباب الذي عرفته باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها ولا يخفى أن اصطدام الماشيين ذلك ليس من في شيء ء فكان خارجاً عن مسائل هذا الباب رجل وجد في زرعه في الليل ثورين فظن أنهما لأهل القرية فبانا أنهما لغيرهم فأراد أن يدخلهما فدخل واحد وفر آخر فتبعه ولم يقدر عليه فجاء صاحبه يضمنه . قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : إن
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): If two horsemen or two pedestrians collide and die, the ‘āqila of each bears the blood-money of the other. Zufar and al-Shafi‘i said: The ‘āqila of each must bear half the blood-money of the other. This is reported from ‘Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) because each died by his own act and the act of his companion, so half is considered and half is waived, like if the collision was intentional and each wounded himself and his companion, or they dug a well on the public road and it collapsed on them or they stood in it – each must bear half. Our proof: The killing of each is attributed to the act of his companion because his own act regarding himself is permissible like walking on the road, so it is not considered regarding liability towards himself because it is absolutely permissible towards himself. If it were considered, half the blood-money would be due when one falls into a well on the public road because without his walking and his own weight he would not have fallen into the well. The act of his companion, even though permissible, is restricted by the condition of safety regarding others, so it becomes a cause for liability when harm occurs. It is reported from ‘Ali that he imposed full blood-money on the ‘āqila of each, so the two reports conflict and we give preponderance to what we mentioned. It is possible that what was reported from him imposing full blood-money was in the case of error to reconcile them. As for their analogy to the intentional collision and each wounding himself and his companion, and digging a well on the road – in those cases each has committed an absolute prohibition, so it is considered regarding himself and he becomes the killer of himself. This ruling applies in cases of intentional and accidental for free persons. If both were slaves, their blood is wasted because the master has no choice of ransom. If one is free and the other a slave, the ‘āqila of the free must pay the full value of the slave in case of error, and half of it for the slave; the heirs of the slain free person take it, and the excess is void because there is no successor. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad because the value of the slain slave is owed by the ‘āqila according to their principle as it is liability for a human being. If two men pull a rope and it breaks and they fall and die, it is to be examined: if they fall on the back of the neck, no blood-money is due for them because each died by his own strength; if they fall on the face, each‘s ‘āqila must bear the blood-money of the other. If a person cuts the rope between them and each falls on the back of the neck, their blood-money is on the ‘āqila of the cutter. And so on for other liabilities. We have already mentioned some of this at his statement 'If he struck the belly of his wife and she responded...' He said in al-Nihāya: The restriction to two horsemen in the book by saying 'If two horsemen collide' is not an additional benefit because the ruling for two pedestrians is the same; it is only mentioned because death from collision usually occurs with horsemen. In al-‘Ināya: The ruling for pedestrians is the same as for horsemen, but since death from collision is mostly with horsemen, they are specifically mentioned. In Mi‘rāj al-Dirāya: Likewise the ruling if two pedestrians collide, and the restriction to horsemen is coincidental or based on common occurrence. The commentator al-‘Ayni followed him. I say: It is strange from these commentators to make such forced explanations despite the reason for restricting to horsemen being clear because this chapter is about animal torts, and it is obvious that the collision of pedestrians has nothing to do with it, so it is outside the topics of this chapter. A man found two bulls in his field at night and thought they belonged to the village people, but they turned out to belong to others. He wanted to bring them in; one entered and the other fled, so he followed it but could not catch it; then its owner came to hold him liable. The chief imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: ...
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an inter-madhhab dispute. The matn states that when two horsemen (or pedestrians) collide and both die, the ‘āqila of each pays the full blood-money of the other. Zufar and al-Shafi‘i argue for half each, citing a report from ‘Ali and analogies to cases where each contributes to his own death (intentional collision, digging a well). The Hanafi response distinguishes: a person’s own act (like walking) is absolutely permissible and thus does not diminish the liability of the other, whose act – though permissible – is restricted by the duty of safety. The Hanafis also cite a contrary report from ‘Ali imposing full blood-money, which they prefer. The text then discusses variations: slaves (no blood-money if both slaves; if one free and one slave, full value of slave on free’s ‘āqila), and the case of two men pulling a rope (liability depends on how they fall). The passage ends with a criticism of commentators who thought the restriction to horsemen was coincidental; the author argues it is because the chapter concerns animals, so only horsemen are directly relevant.

Key Terms
مباح (mubāḥ): Legally permissible; an act that carries no sin or liability for the actor when done for oneself, such as walking on the road.
عاقلة (‘āqila): The group of male relatives (usually the tribe or clan) who are responsible for paying blood money (diya) on behalf of a member who commits accidental homicide.
دية (diya): Blood money; financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim of unlawful killing or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the Hanafis hold that each party bears the full blood-money of the other in a collision case?
A) Because each party is equally at fault
B) Because the other's act is prohibited and thus fully causative
C) Because each party's own act (moving) is permissible and does not reduce the other's liability
D) Because the case is analogous to digging a well

Key Takeaway
In permissible acts, one's own movement does not reduce the other's liability; full blood money is due.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate

Rope-Pulling and Collision: Attributing Cause in Hanafi Tort Law
Hanafi ruling on rope-pulling death with liability based on fall manner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the manner of falling determines liability in rope-pulling accidents.

Core Idea
Falling on the face indicates the other's pull caused death; falling on the neck means each died by his own strength.

When two men pull a rope and it breaks, how is liability determined? The Hanafi school looks at how they fall: if on the back of the neck, no blood money; if on the face, each pays the other's diya. This lesson explains the reasoning and shows how the same principle applies from the collision case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two men pull a rope from opposite ends, the rope breaks, and both fall and die. The question is whether any blood money is due.
2
Distinguish based on fall
If they fall on the back of the neck (qafā), no diya is due because each died by his own strength. If they fall on the face, each 'āqila pays the full diya of the other.
If a third party cuts the rope, that third party's 'āqila pays diya regardless.
3
Understand the cause
Falling on qafā indicates death from one's own pull (the rope breaking caused a backward fall). Falling on the face indicates death from the other's pull (the other's force caused the fall).
4
Apply the principle
This case aligns with the collision principle: each person's own strength (like his movement) is not counted against him. Only the other's force is causative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The passage ends with a criticism of commentators who thought the restriction to horsemen was coincidental; the author argues it is because the chapter concerns animals, so only horsemen are directly relevant.

Key Terms
قفا (qafā): The back of the neck; falling on the qafā indicates death from one's own force, while falling on the face indicates the other's pull caused death.
دية (diya): Blood money; financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim of unlawful killing or injury.
عاقلة (‘āqila): The group of male relatives (usually the tribe or clan) who are responsible for paying blood money (diya) on behalf of a member who commits accidental homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two men pull a rope, it breaks, and they fall on their faces. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) No diya is due
B) Each 'āqila pays half diya of the other
C) Each 'āqila pays full diya of the other
D) The cutter's 'āqila pays diya

Key Takeaway
The manner of falling in a rope-pulling accident determines the cause of death: falling on the face indicates the other's pull killed him; falling on the back indicates self-caused death.

Liability in a Fatal Collision Between Two Horsemen (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Resolving a dispute between the Hanafi school and the Sahibayn on liability in a fatal collision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred Hanafi ruling on diyah when two free persons die from a mutual collision and explain how the author reconciles the conflicting narrations from Ali.

Core Idea
In a fatal collision of two permissible acts, the death is attributed entirely to the other's action, imposing full diyah.

When two horsemen collide and both die, who bears the blood-money? The Sahibayn analogize to cases where each party's action is prohibited, leading to half liability for each. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, however, distinguish based on the permissibility of each person's own motion. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the reliance on a narration from Ali, and the practical outcome for the 'aagila.

Guided Path
1
State the core issue
Identify the specific question: when two free persons die from a collision, how much diyah is obligatory on each 'aagila?
The dispute is between Abu Hanifa & Muhammad (full diyah each) and the Sahibayn (half diyah each).
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's analogy
Understand why Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue for half liability. They compare to intentional collision (both act prohibited) and digging a well (both act prohibited). In those, each is partly responsible for his own death, so half.
Prohibited action (muharam) leads to each being a 'killer of himself'.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's response
Note the key distinction: here each person's own motion (like walking) is permissible (mubah) regarding himself, so it does not create liability for his own death. The other's action, though permissible, is restricted by a safety condition toward others, making it the cause of death.
The walking analogy (falling into a well) is used: the walker's own walking is not a cause of his death.
4
Weigh the narrations from Ali
Two narrations exist: one imposing full diyah on each 'aagila, another (possibly) imposing full diyah on the line of error. The author reconciles them and gives preponderance to the full diyah view, which supports Abu Hanifa's position.
The author uses the principle of tarjih to resolve the apparent conflict.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the Sahibayn's view, half diyah from each 'aagila; under Abu Hanifa's preferred view, full diyah from each. Also consider mixed cases (free and slave) where the ruling varies.
Remember: for two slaves, no diyah; for one free and one slave, specific rules apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علي رضي الله عنه لأن كل واحد منهما مات بفعله وفعل صاحبه فيعتبر نصفه ويهدر النصف كما إذا كان الاصطدام عمداً وجرح كل واحد منهما نفسه وصاحبه أو حفرا على قارعة الطريق بئراً فانهدم عليهما أو وقفا فيه يجب على كل واحد منهما النصف فكذا هذا. ولنا أن قتل كل واحد منهما مضاف إلى فعل صاحبه لأن فعله في نفسه مباح كالمشي في الطريق فلا يعتبر في حق الضمان بالنسبة إلى نفسه لأنه مباح مطلقاً في حق نفسه، ولو اعتبر ذلك لوجب نصف الدية فيما إذا وقع في بئر في قارعة الطريق لأنه لولا مشيه وثقله في نفسه لما هوى في البئر، وفعل صاحبه وإن كان مباحاً لكنه مقيد بشرط السلامة في حق غيره فيكون سبباً للضمان عند وجود التلف، به وروي عن علي رضي الله عنه أنه أوجب كل الدية على عاقلة كل واحد منهما فتعارضت روایتان فرجحنا ما ذكرنا ويحتمل ما روي عنه أنه أوجب كل الدية على الخطأ توفيقاً بينهما. وأما ما استشهدا به من الاصطدام وجرح كل منهما نفسه وصاحبه وحفر البئر في الطريق فعلى كل واحد محظور مطلقاً فيعتبر في حق نفسه أيضاً فيكون قاتلاً لنفسه، وهذا الحكم الذي ذكرناه في العمد والخطأ في الحرين. ولو كانا عبدين هدر الدم لأن المولى فيه غير مختار للفداء، ولو كان أحدهما حراً والآخر عبداً يجب على عاقلة الحر قيمة العبد كلها في الخطأ ونصفها في العبد فيأخذها ورثة الحر المقتول ويبطل ما زاد عليه لعدم الخلف، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لأن قيمة العبد المقتول تجب على العاقلة على أصلهما لأنه ضمان الآدمي.
Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, because each one of them died by his own action and by the action of his companion, so half is considered and half is waived, as if the collision was intentional and each one of them injured himself and his companion, or they dug a well on the main road and it collapsed on them or they stood in it, then half is obligatory on each one of them. So too this case. And we say that the killing of each one of them is attributed to the action of his companion, because his own action regarding himself is permissible like walking on the road, so it is not considered with respect to liability regarding himself because it is absolutely permissible regarding himself. If that were considered, half the blood-money would be obligatory when one falls into a well on the main road, because without his walking and his own weight he would not have fallen into the well. And the action of his companion, although permissible, is restricted by a condition of safety regarding others, so it becomes a cause for liability when destruction occurs thereby. And it has been narrated from Ali that he imposed the full blood-money on the 'aagila of each one of them, so two narrations conflict, and we gave preponderance to what we mentioned. And it is possible that what is narrated from him—that he imposed full blood-money on the line of erring—is to reconcile them. As for what the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) used as evidence from the case of collision and each one injuring himself and his companion, and digging a well on the road, then each one is absolutely prohibited, so it is considered regarding himself as well, so he becomes a killer of himself. And this ruling that we have mentioned applies in intentional and erroneous killing among free persons. And if both were slaves, the blood is wasted because the master has no choice for ransom. And if one of them is free and the other a slave, then the value of the slave in full is obligatory upon the 'aagila of the free in the case of error, and half of it upon the 'aagila of the slave. The heirs of the slain free person take it, and whatever exceeds is cancelled due to lack of successor. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because the value of the slain slave is obligatory upon the 'aagila according to their principle, as it is a guarantee of a human being.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage addresses the liability when two horsemen (or people) collide and both die. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) analogize to intentional collision where each injures himself and the other, and to digging a well on the road, both of which involve a prohibited action from each, so they assign half liability to each. The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and, here, the author) responds that each person's own action (like walking) is permissible in itself, so it does not create liability for one's own death. The liability falls entirely on the other's action because although his walking is also permissible, it is restricted by a condition of safety toward others—so when it causes harm, it becomes the cause of guarantee. This is supported by a narration from Ali where he imposed full blood-money on the 'aagila of each, but this is reconciled by another narration imposing full blood-money on the line of error. The disputed cases involve prohibited actions from both, so they differ. If both are slaves, no blood-money because the master cannot be forced to ransom. If one free and one slave, the free's 'aagila pays the full value of the slave in error, and half if the slave is the victim, with the balance cancelled.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aagila): The group of male relatives (patrilineal) who bear the blood-money for erroneous killing; in this context, they pay the diyah for the liability of their member.
فداء (fida'): Ransom; in the context of slaves, the amount paid to free the slave from liability for a crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, what is the liability when two free persons die from a collision?
A) No blood-money is due because both died.
B) Half the blood-money is due from each 'aagila.
C) Full blood-money is due from the 'aagila of each for the death of the other.
D) The blood-money is paid by the state.

Key Takeaway
In a fatal collision between two free persons, the preferred Hanafi view is that each 'aagila pays full diyah for the death of the other, based on the permissibility of one's own motion and a narration from Ali.

Liability in Rope-Pulling Accidents: The Principle of Fall Direction
Differentiating liability based on how they fall after pulling a rope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing liability for death in a rope-pulling accident based on whether the victim fell backward or forward.

Core Idea
Liability for death in a rope-pulling accident depends on whether the person fell backward (own force) or forward (other's force).

When two people pull a rope and it breaks, causing them to fall, the Hanafi school examines the direction of the fall to determine who caused the death. This seemingly small detail reflects a deep legal principle about causation (sababiyyah) and direct force (mubasharah). Understanding this ruling helps you see how Hanafi jurists assign liability in cases of shared or indirect causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Parties
The act is two men pulling a rope that breaks, causing both to fall and die. Identify the legal question: who is liable for the blood-money?
The rope breaking is incidental; the focus is on the movement before the break.
2
Examine the Manner of Fall
The ruling distinguishes two outcomes: falling on the back (qafa) vs. falling on the face. Each indicates a different direction of force at the moment of the break.
Falling on the back suggests the person was pulling backward; falling on the face suggests being pulled forward.
3
Apply the Liability Rule for the Two Pullers
If both fall on their backs: no blood-money because each died by his own pulling force. If both fall on their faces: each is liable for the other's death (full diya on each one's 'aagila).
The 'aagila pays the diya, not the individual directly.
4
Consider the Third-Party Case
If a third person cuts the rope and both pullers fall on their backs, the cutter is liable for both deaths. The cut is a direct cause overriding the pullers' own force.
This shows that when a direct external cause intervenes, the original force becomes irrelevant.
5
Generalize the Principle
The passage states: 'And likewise all other guarantees follow this principle.' Thus, the rule of looking at direction/force applies to similar cases of shared causation.
This is a legal maxim for determining causation in joint actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تجاذب رجلان حبلاً فانقطع الحبل فسقطا أو ماتا ينظر؛ فإن وقعا على القفا لا تجب لهما دية لأن كل واحد منهما مات بقوة نفسه، وأن وقعا على الوجه وجب على عاقلة كل واحد منهما دية الآخر، وإن قطع إنسان الحبل بينهما فوقع كل واحد منهما على القفا فديتهما على عاقلة القاطع، وكذا على هذا سائر الضمانات
And if two men pull a rope, and the rope breaks, and they fall and die: then it is considered. If they fall on their backs, no blood-money is due for them because each one died by his own force. And if they fall on their faces, then upon the 'aagila of each one is the blood-money of the other. And if a person cuts the rope between them, and each one falls on his back, then their blood-money is upon the 'aagila of the cutter. And likewise all other guarantees follow this principle.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling for when two people are pulling a rope and it breaks, causing them to fall and die. The key factor is the direction of the fall. If they fall on their backs, it indicates they were pulling backward, so the force that caused death came from each person's own effort—thus no liability on the other. If they fall on their faces, it shows each was pulled forward by the other's force, so each is liable for the other's death (full diya on their 'aagila). If a third party cuts the rope, and they fall on their backs, the cutter is liable for both deaths because his action caused the fall, even though they fell backward. The principle extends to all similar guarantees.

Key Terms
قفا (qafa): The back of the neck; falling on the back of the head indicates falling backward due to one's own force.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee for harm caused; here it refers to blood-money.
عاقلة ('aagila): The group of male agnate relatives who pay blood-money on behalf of a killer.
دية (diya): Blood-money; financial compensation for killing or wounding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two men pull a rope and it breaks, and both fall on their backs and die, who is liable?
A) No one, because each died by his own force.
B) Both are liable for each other's death.
C) The one who pulled harder is liable.
D) The 'aagila of both men pay each other's diya.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the direction of a fall in a rope-pulling accident reveals the causal force and determines liability for blood-money.

Master's Options for Simultaneously Injured Slaves
Ruling on simultaneous slave injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the master's options when two slaves simultaneously injure each other and explain why mutual recourse is barred in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
When two slaves injure each other simultaneously, each master either surrenders his slave or pays full ransom — no mutual recourse because each master's right attaches to a complete slave.

Imagine two slaves, each with a stick, strike each other at the same time. Both survive and heal. In Hanafi jurisprudence, this scenario raises a unique question: what compensation is due? The key lies in the fact that each master's claim is against a specific sound slave, and the exchange of ownership settles the matter irrevocably.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the injury
Two slaves meet and simultaneously strike each other with sticks. Both survive and recover from their wounds.
The simultaneity is crucial for the ruling.
2
Recognize that both slaves recover
The text states 'wa bari'a' (they recovered), meaning the slaves healed. If they died, the ruling would differ.
Recovery means the slave remains alive and continues to be owned.
3
Determine the master's two options
Each master has a choice: either surrender his slave to the other master or pay the full ransom (fida') for the injury his slave caused. The ransom is the full arsh (monetary compensation for injury).
The ransom is not half; it is the full value of the injury because each injured a sound slave.
4
Understand why mutual recourse is prohibited
If one master later seeks compensation from the other, the other would likewise seek it, resulting in a deadlock because each master's right is attached to a complete slave. The exchange of ownership or payment already settles the right.
The phrase 'لا يتراجعان بشيء' means they do not seek recourse against each other beyond the initial choice.
5
Explain the rationale based on exchange of ownership
When a master surrenders his slave, he effectively transfers ownership to the other, satisfying the other's claim to a complete slave. If ransom is paid, it compensates for the full injury. Both outcomes eliminate any further claim.
The rationale prevents circular litigation and ensures finality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبدان التقيا ومع كل واحد عصا فأضربا وبرئا خير مولى كل واحد منهما بالآخر ولا يتراجعان بشيء سوى ذلك لأن كل واحد منهما ملك عبده من صاحبه ولا يفيد التراجع لأنه لو رجع أحدهما لرجع الآخر لأن حق كل واحد منهما ثبت في رقبة كاملة فما يأخذ أحدهما من صاحبه فذاك بدل الآخر وتعلق به حقه فلا يفيد الرجوع، وإن اختار الفداء فدى كل واحد بجميع أرش جنايته لأنهما لما ضربا معاً فقد جنى كل واحد منهما على عبد صحيح فتعلق حق كل واحد من الموليين بعبد صحيح فيجب بدل عبد صحيح.
Two slaves met, each with a stick, and they struck each other and recovered. The master of each is given a choice regarding the other, and they do not seek recourse from each other beyond that, because each one has taken ownership of the slave from his companion, and recourse does not benefit because if one pursues recourse, the other would also pursue recourse, since the right of each is established over a complete slave. So what one takes from his companion is a substitute for the other, and his right is attached to it, so recourse is not beneficial. If he chooses ransom, each pays the full value of the injury caused by his slave, because when they struck together, each one has inflicted injury upon a sound slave, so the right of each master is attached to a sound slave, so a full sound slave's value is required.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
If two slaves hit each other simultaneously and both recover from wounds (or die? Here they recover), the masters face a choice: each can either surrender his slave to the other (since each has a claim on the other slave for compensation) or pay ransom (fida') equal to the injury caused. The unique point is that they cannot seek mutual recourse after settlement because each master becomes owner of the other's slave in full, so any attempt at recourse would be cancelled out. If ransom is chosen, each pays the full blood-money for his slave's injury because each injured a sound slave belonging to the other. This applies when both hit at the exact same time.

Key Terms
برئا (bari'a): Both recovered from their injuries; i.e., they survived and healed.
أرش (arsh): The monetary compensation for injury to a slave (or free person) that is not pre-determined as diyah; it is calculated based on the reduction in value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When two slaves simultaneously strike each other and recover, what does the master of each slave have the option to do?
A) Only demand compensation from the other master.
B) Either surrender his slave to the other master or pay the full ransom.
C) Keep his slave and pay nothing.
D) Kill the other slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when two slaves injure each other simultaneously, the masters must settle by exchanging ownership or paying full ransom, with no further recourse.
